Work and (Play Ball)

by WitchHeartsRunFree

Summary

Agatha had been catching in the league for five years. She thought she had seen it all. She was a dominant hitter against every team they faced.

When she steps up to the plate against a new mysterious pitcher, she realizes she hasn't seen anything yet.

-------------

Or, the Agatha All Along x The League of Their Own crossover, enemies to lovers baseball AU

Chapter 1: The rookie

“When they really went to complete overhand pitching, I was home free. I knew all the pitches and they had a lot of trouble hitting off me.”

Jean Faut - The only known professional baseball pitcher, man or woman, to pitch two perfect games in a career.

 

The 1948 season was a lesson in change for everybody in the league.

Underhand pitching was out. Overhand pitching was in. 

They were already two months into the season, and most hitters were still struggling to adjust. The ball was also smaller, travelling faster, and moving in new ways. That would be an issue for anyone. Anyone except the star player on the Kenosha Comets, Agatha Harkness. 

Agatha was a veteran of the league at this point, having faithfully played as catcher for the same team since its inception five years ago. She topped the table with her batting average and home runs. Her teammates thought her bat was magic because it had an uncanny way of always finding the ball. Pitchers feared her, outfielders cursed her, and fans were in awe of her raw strength and ability. She was a darling of the papers and regularly interviewed by adoring journalists and radio hosts. 

The sun was pounding down on this July day as the Comets faced off against the newly-formed Springfield Sallies. They were only created this year as the popularity of the league was growing and more cities wanted their own ball club. The sky was completely clear, the stands were packed - a perfect Saturday for baseball. The anthem was sung, the players were all stretched and ready. 

“Play ball!”

The home team took to the field and started tossing the ball around. 

Ruth Taylor took her spot as the starting pitcher for the Sallies. She had adapted her pitching style to a sidearm slinger but couldn’t quite match her underhand velocity. It was a whole different ball game now. The league had started a degree away from the traditional softball and now it had tipped right past the line towards baseball. Most of the league’s pitchers couldn’t make the transition as easily. It was a death blow and meant many had left the game entirely. 

Ruth was one of the few lucky ones who could adjust and keep her position, but it was a battle. 

By the time the game reached the fourth inning, the Comets were up by 5 runs and the Sallies coach made his way out to the mound to relieve Ruth. 

“Who’s that?” Agatha elbowed Wanda and pointed to the new pitcher warming up on the mound. 

Wanda squinted out of the dugout, “Never seen her before.”

They watched the mystery woman toss out a few warm up pitches. 

“Doesn’t look like a problem. I wonder what happened to their normal reliever, Betty, now that gal could really throw.” Agatha remarked. 

“She got engaged last Christmas! Didn’t you get a wedding invite? They’re moving out to Madison.” She lowered her voice, “And I heard that he didn’t want her coming back this year.”

Agatha rolled her eyes and went to get her bat, “What a shame. And now she’s been replaced by this rookie, what is this game coming to?” 

“What do you have against love?” Wanda looked at her aghast. 

“Love is for suckers.” Agatha laughed and stepped out of the dugout into the on deck circle. She took a few swings to get loose. “Show this newbie how we do things, Shirley.”

The lead-off hitter smiled at her and nodded as she passed on her way to the plate. Shirley Cohen was a solid contact hitter that the team relied on to get on base. A bit of an anxious person, but when her confidence was up, she was a great ballplayer. 

The pitcher wound up. She brought both hands up behind her head as she leaned back. Her leg went high into the air, straight out in a line as she shifted her weight to her back foot. 

What the…? Agatha thought as she watched the motion. What is she doing?

“Strike!” The umpire’s voice rang out. 

Shirley turned back to look at the ump, “What?” 

The catcher tossed the ball back to the pitcher who was smirking. 

“No balls, one strike.” The umpire reiterated. 

Agatha rubbed her eyes. How did I miss it? I didn’t even see it leave her hand.

She wasn’t going to make that mistake again. She put her bat down on the ground and leaned against it, focussing entirely on the pitcher. She was pretty young, brown hair to her shoulders, mischievous eyes. There was something wild in those eyes. 

There she goes again with that wind-up. Okay, focus on her hand. The ball rolled from her fingers this time and started high. That’s way outside the strike zone - don’t swing, Shirley.  

The batter took a chop at it, nervous due to the last pitch, and almost lost her footing as the ball dropped into the catcher’s mitt. Her face was twisted in confusion as she looked back at Agatha. 

“Strike!”

The pitcher was calm as she stretched her neck side to side. She took a deep breath, and Agatha watched the final pitch zip right into the catcher’s glove. Shirley didn’t even have time to think about swinging. 

“Strike three, you’re out!” 

The home crowd cheered wildly as the pitcher took a dramatic bow. Agatha frowned. She hated cockiness. Confidence was one thing, but cockiness pissed her off. 

Shirley made the slow walk back to the dugout. Her eyes were dazed. It was like she had just seen a ghost. Agatha gave her a soft pat on the shoulder as she passed, then tried to steady herself to get ready.

Time to welcome this rookie to the big leagues.

“Coming up to the plate, they call her “Angel” and it’s not just because of her heavenly good looks, number 3, Agatha Harkness.” The announcer's voice resonated throughout the stadium. She took her stance in the box. 

“Don’t blink.” The catcher, Carson Shaw, whispered at her from behind home plate.

“Shut it, Shaw.” Agatha spoke out of the side of her mouth, trying to focus. 

The pitcher nodded subtly then stood tall. She started her ridiculous wind-up. 

Eye on the ball. Eye on the ball. 

It soared through the air, but Agatha could see the arcing motion. That’s inside. 

“Ball.” The umpire called. 

“Good eye, Angel!” She heard Wanda yell out. 

The pitcher was studying her now. She felt those deep brown eyes looking at her with curiosity. She stared straight back.

The wind-up, the pitch.

It’s high. Lay off it. 

“Ball.”

Agatha let out an audible scoff. Another rookie pitcher who can’t handle the pressure. That strikeout must have been a fluke. 

She watched the pitcher try to reset. She was circling the rubber and stretching her hand. They made eye contact, and Agatha decided to give her a wink. 

Oop, she did not like that. The pitcher’s eyebrows turned down at the gesture, and she spun around, rolling her shoulders back as she rolled the ball over in her glove. She took a longer pause this time as she readied herself. 

She kicked out, her arm swung around quickly, and Agatha watched the ball flying towards her. Wait, it was actually flying directly towards her. Jesus Christ. 

She hit the ground as fast as possible to avoid the incoming bullet, and the ball hit the fence of the backstop. Even the catcher hadn’t seen that coming. 

Agatha jumped up and threw her bat down. She hastily wiped the dirt from her hands. All critical thinking went out the window.  

She charged the mound. 

“Are you kidding me?!” She got right into the pitcher’s face. “You could have knocked me out!”

Her teammates were yelling at her to get back. The crowd had gasped and gone quiet. One wrong move, one overheard curse word, and she could be fined or suspended. In that moment though, she couldn’t care less. 

“Huh? What the hell are you playing at?” Agatha was a breath away from the pitcher. Their cap bills were just touching. 

The dark-haired rookie just stayed silent and stared. She licked her lips and did the single thing that sent Agatha over the edge. Her lips went up in a slow smile, and she shrugged. 

Something in her snapped. Her rage boiled to the surface like a roiling kettle. She shoved the smug, overconfident amateur with all her might, causing her to backpedal and fall into the dirt.

The pitcher kept her mouth shut and still had that infuriating smile plastered on her face, even as she was laying on the ground. 

“Hey! Harkness!!” The coach stormed out onto the field, followed by a few teammates and the umpires. 

The head ump pointed to her and dramatically gestured his thumb behind his shoulder. “You’re outta here!” 

“What!!?” Agatha barked. “She’s the one who almost took my head off!” She said pointing to the pitcher who was being helped up by the second baseman. 

Wanda held onto her shoulders to keep her steady and hold her back. “Watch it.” She whispered in Agatha’s ear, but all she could hear was her own blood pumping through her veins.  

“Stop talking. Now!” Coach Grant pulled her by the arm to get her away from the scene of the crime. “I apologize for this one. She’s not feeling well today, Rich.” He addressed the ump. 

“She’s got one minute to leave the grounds before I make it a two-game suspension.” 

Agatha went to pipe up but felt the coach quickly leading her back to the bench. “Coach, you saw what she did. She threw a headhunter.” 

“And she would have dealt with the consequences. Instead, you flipped your wig and are out of this game.” 

“She did it on purpose!”

“That’s baseball.” Doc Grant was a seasoned catcher who played four years for the Cincinnati Reds. He tore his ACL his last season and was forced to hang up his glove. When the opportunity to coach came around, he jumped at the chance. 

Just shy of 40, he was the youngest coach in the league, but he loved the game. While others turned their noses up at the idea of women playing ball, he welcomed the challenge. He took a particular liking to Agatha and not just because she was also a catcher. She had grit, drive, and the unrelenting desire to win. 

“Go home, Harkness. Cool off.” He pointed her to the change rooms. 

“But-”

“I don’t want to hear it. Go!” 

Agatha groaned and grabbed her mitt before dragging her feet to the locker room. Wanda followed at her heels. 

“Are you here to tell me off, too?” She looked over at the redhead. 

“If you hadn’t gone after that pitcher, I was ready to throw hands.” She jokingly held up her fists like a boxer. 

“Ha! That would have been a real crack up. So you’re on my side?”

“Obviously. It’s not like we have helmets or anything. One fastball to the skull and that could be lights out. That was dangerous.” Wanda threw an arm around Agatha’s shoulder as they walked down the hall. 

The two hadn’t been fast friends. In fact, her presence had annoyed Agatha from the jump, or more specifically the presence of two stragglers that came with the new arrival. Wanda was traded to the Comets two years ago from Fort Wayne. She showed up with her twin three-year-old sons in tow, as her husband had been killed in action on the frontlines. 

“Coach, we can’t let some kids run around the dugout. It’s not…it’s not safe!” She had argued. 

“What am I meant to say, Hark? She’s a widow for Christ's sake. She has nobody else. They have nowhere else to go. She’s a damn fine ballplayer; she’s staying.” 

She couldn’t say aloud the real reason that she wanted the twins out. They were a painful reminder of her own past. The thing that had spurred her to try out for the league in the first place. The awful memory that had pushed her to run. 

Wanda was like a pesky tick. She had burrowed her way into Agatha’s life. Slowly but surely she had grown to like her, despite her best efforts to not. Maybe it was her joyful naivete, her surprisingly strong arm, or her loyal friendship. Whatever the case, the pair were now as close as ever.

Agatha dropped her glove into her bag and started unbuttoning her uniform. “Aren’t you up to bat soon? This ain’t a peep show.” 

“Nothing I haven’t seen before, babe. And there’s still a few to go. I haven’t even heard the announcer restart the game yet. Just wanted to make sure you’re okay.” 

“I’m fine. Don’t worry, Red. I’ll go to the hotel and sleep it off. We can hit the bar tonight when everyone’s back. I think one of the gals got a lead on a hotspot downtown.” 

“Yeah, alright. I’ll see if Lilia can watch the boys. You sure you’re good?”

“I’m sweet, really. Get back out there.” Agatha wanted to be alone. This was the first time she had ever been ejected from a game. She’d masterfully toed the line her whole career between following the rules and getting what she wanted. The umpires never gave her grief. She had solid relationships with the team chaperone, Lilia, with the coach, with all the umps, with the bosses in the head office. 

One match-up with this rookie, and she’d thrown it all away. Her reputation potentially sullied from a moment of weakness. How pathetic. 

Wanda turned to leave before Agatha remembered, “Wait!”

“What?” 

“Did you catch the pitcher’s name?” Agatha asked. 

“Rio. Rio Vidal.”

Agatha watched her friend walk away. She repeated the name over and over again in her mind. 

Rio Vidal. Rio Vidal. Rio Vidal. 

It was annoyingly unique. Foreign. Punchy. Irritating.

She reached into her bag and pulled out her journal. She sat on the long bench in the middle of the room. After every game, she liked to write down her thoughts, feelings, and reflections on her performance. It was a fun keepsake each season and helped her learn and get better. She already had four filled books sitting on her shelf back home. She was only a few pages into the fifth. 

July 10th, 1948

I singled in the first inning against Ruth Taylor. It was a solid ground ball into the outfield. I need to watch my timing on the curveball. I made it all the way around the bases as the team went on a great run at the plate. 

I hit a flyout in the third to left field. I was just under it, but the power was there. I thought it had a chance, but oh well. I should practice my swing this week and ask Coach for some more tips. 

In the fourth inning, a new pitcher came into the game. Nobody knew her, so I think she must be a new recruit this season. She struck out Shirley in three pitches, which hasn’t happened since her first season with us. It was…

She stopped her pen. What was it? It was amazing, shocking, impressive as hell. She gripped the pen in her fist and pushed her hand into her forehead in frustration. 

It was really surprising. I was up to bat next and thought I’d teach her a thing or two. I saw the first pitch clearly and let it sail by. The second one was close, but I knew it was another ball. I let my ego get the better of me and decided to give the pitcher an innocent wink. She made me pay for that. The next ball was aimed right at that eye. 

I charged at her like a bull towards a red cape. I was steaming. She had no right to do that. I pushed her, and even then, she still didn’t speak. Still couldn’t explain what her problem was. I don’t even know what her voice sounds like.

She picked the wrong gal to mess with. I’m going to make her regret that pitch. 

Rio Vidal. Even her name makes me angry. 

Rio Vidal.

I hate Rio Vidal.

Chapter 2: Choking up

“The salaries being offered are good enough to assure the finest and fairest of talent. For instance, the lowest salary called for in a current contract is $45 a week, more than the average stenographer or factory girl gets. The best salary is $85 a week, comparing favorably with the pay received by players in the top minor leagues. And in usual baseball style the girls get all their expenses paid while they’re on the road.”

Gene F. Hampson, The Courier-News. May 25, 1943

Agatha waited in the locker room for the game to finish. She laid down on the bench and listened to the announcer’s commentary that echoed throughout the stadium. 

“And that’s another strikeout from the Sallie’s rookie pitcher, Rio Vidal, making it five for the night. What an arm, folks!”

Where did they find this girl? Agatha had faced her share of talented hurlers during her years on the team. She had seen speed and accuracy before but not to this level. 

Lefty Reyes on the Belles had dominated the mound for the first few years. Her pitches were nicknamed “rockets” because of their unhittable velocity. Then there was Grace Nickelson on the Peaches whose signature pitch was a riseball that could fool even the best hitter. However, both of these stars were underhand pitchers. Rio was an entirely different breed. 

“Final score - Comets 5, Sallies 3. Give a hand to all the players, and we’ll see you tomorrow! Goodnight!”

The girls started trickling into the locker room now that the game had wrapped up. Agatha sat up, eager to hear the details. 

“You’re still here?” The starting pitcher, Lupe Garcia, asked as she tossed her glove into her locker. 

“Nowhere else to go.” Agatha said simply. 

“Harkness!” The coach’s voice boomed as he entered the room. “Over here.” He pointed to the spare office. 

She didn’t like the sound of this but knew the coach didn’t tolerate lollygagging. She hurried over. 

“Sit.” He nodded to the foldable chair and shut the door. 

“Coach, I-” 

“I don’t want to hear any excuses. You’re lucky that the umpire likes you. If any other gal had pushed another player, they would be out for the week or worse. What were you thinking?”

Agatha rubbed her forehead, “I wasn’t. I’m sorry, Coach. She got the better of me. I won’t let it happen again.”

“It better not. This is your only warning. Step out of line again, and you’ll be riding the bench. I’m sure Terry would love to get some playtime as catcher.”

“Terry couldn’t catch a cold if someone coughed in her face.” Agatha remarked. 

The coach glared back at her, “Hey, playing on this team is a privilege. Do you know how many girls would kill to have your spot?”

Agatha thought back to the open tryouts at Wrigley Field in 1943. 

She had received a telegram from the office of Mr. Wrigley. 

You are invited to try-out for the newly-created All-American Girls Professional Baseball League. Please report to Wrigley Field on May 5, 1943. Equipment not provided. 

The house had been empty. She had simply stared at the small slip of paper in disbelief. Was this a goof? Was it a cruel joke? How did they even know who she was?

While the questions bounced around her brain, her mouth broke out into a smile. She hadn’t even realized she was doing it, but the idea of playing professional ball…how could she not? 

She looked up and around the room, expecting Ralph to round the corner any second, expecting Nicky to race towards her and jump for joy at the news. But they wouldn’t. They couldn’t. 

They were both gone. They weren’t coming back. 

She spotted one of Nicky’s race car toys in the corner of the dining room. She stood from the chair and went to grab it. She must have missed it. It had only been a few months. She put it on the table, looking at the little painted details. He used to obsess over the different models and beg each birthday for a new one. She pushed it and watched the car soar to the ground and flip over. 

Agatha sat in a daze, the memory overwhelming her for a moment. She stood up, went to the kitchen, and grabbed the phone. She spun the dial quickly as the pattern was well worn in her mind. 

“Peggy?”

“Hey, doll. How are you?”

“Fine. I just got a very curious telegram.” Agatha said and wished she had brought it over to the phone. 

“Holy mackerel! I was about to call to tell you the same. It’s from that gum man, Wrigley!” 

Agatha gasped, “Yes! Mine too! Do you think it’s the real deal?”

“It must be. Chicago is a long way away. We’ve only got two weeks, you’re going right?” Peggy’s enthusiasm was bursting through the earpiece. 

“I don’t know, Peg. My mother has been unwell, Ralph has enlisted, there’s nobody to look after the house, and what would I tell work?”

“Well, I’m going. When are we ever going to get a chance like this again? The try-outs are on the actual field that the Cubs play on! I’m going to tell the boss that I have to go to my great aunt’s funeral in Chicago. If I make the team, I’ll think of some other excuse. Don’t make me go alone!”

“You’re terrible. Let me think about it. I’ll ring you tomorrow.” They exchanged goodbyes, and Agatha went to the living room to collapse on the sofa. 

“Ow.” She shifted to the side and rummaged under the cushion. She pulled out three army men that must have fallen down the cracks. Her eyes welled with tears, and she released a devastating sob. Her hands rubbed the green figurines and the wet streaks cut a river down her face. 

I need to get out of here. I can’t live in a constant reminder. 

Agatha decided then and there to take the chance and booked a one-way ticket to Chicago alongside her co-worker and friend, Peggy Carter. The two of them were star players on the Salem Steelers, the company softball team where they played and worked. A scout had spotted them at one of the city league games and put them on the list with a few others from town. 

Of the 280 women who tried out for the inaugural season of the league, only 60 were chosen on the four original teams to play. Against all odds and chance, both Agatha and Peggy made the roster for the Kenosha Comets. Their professional career had begun.  

 

 

“I know, Coach. I’m sorry. It’s just that pitcher-”

“Nope, no excuses. I don’t care if she insulted your hairdo or called your mama a low-life nothing. There is no reason to resort to violence, got it?”

“Yes, sir.”

“Next practice you’re going to run the field until I say stop. And you’re going to write that girl an apology.”

Agatha jumped to her feet, “Sir! You can’t be serious.”

He stared at her, “Do I look like I’m joking? We’re done here. I expect you to hand me that letter by tomorrow. Have a good night, Harkness.”

Before she could protest again, she was shown the door. 

Wanda met her and led her to her bags, “Come on, the bus is outside. Let’s get out of here.”

Agatha was too enraged to open her mouth, for fear of what might come out. She let Wanda hand her the bag of her things and followed the team to the bus that would take them back to the hotel. She sat in the back as usual. 

“Sooo….do you want to talk about it?” Wanda prodded. 

Agatha’s mouth was a tight line. She shook her head. 

“Okay then. If it makes you feel any better, I got a hit off that pitcher. It was a lucky single, but I got on base. We just need to practice against an overhand pitcher more. I think we need more time. She’s not unhittable.” 

“Where are we going to find someone who can throw at her speed? Lupe is great but more of a breaking ball gal. We need a pitcher who can match that crazy fastball.” Agatha said. 

“I’ll ask Coach. I know the scouts are searching far and wide for overhand pitchers. There may be some more mid-season recruits coming for all we know.” 

Shirley sat up and turned around to face them over the seats, “Did you guys hear about that broad from Hong Kong? I heard Coach saying that a scout saw her play in California and that she had one of the fastest arms he had ever seen.” 

“Do you really think the league would give her a shot? Maxine from my hometown team could probably make the Red Sox starting lineup with her level of talent, but she couldn’t even get a try out because of the color of her skin.” Agatha was well aware of the color barrier in place in the league. It was a wonder that they let several Cuban and Puerto Rican girls play at all. Then again, hypocrisy and racism generally went hand in hand.  

“Who knows? With all these new teams and new rules, they are desperate for new players. The war’s over, the men are back. It’s only a matter of time before they push us out. Why not let some chick from the Far East join the downfall?” Shriley was a realist, if nothing else. Her neurotic anxiety kept her optimism low. She had no delusions about the long-term future of the sport. 

“Who peed in your porridge? The stands are still packed at all our games, the paychecks are still clearing, let’s just enjoy this moment!” Wanda chimed in. She was beyond grateful for her position on the Comets. It paid more than her father made, let her travel with her sons, and allowed her to do what she loved. 

“At least Rio probably won’t be playing tomorrow. She’ll need to rest her arm after all those innings. We should discuss with Coach a game plan though.” Agatha said.

“Off to bed everyone! Game starts tomorrow at noon, so the bus leaves at 10:30am. Remember to get your beauty sleep.” The team chaperone, Lilia, announced. 

Agatha rolled her eyes. She didn’t need beauty sleep. She needed a drink. 

Peggy hopped up and whispered to the group in the back as everyone rustled to get up and out, “We’re walking over to The Sidewinder downtown. Once the lights go out at 10, be ready to roll.”

Agatha rubbed her hands together and grinned, “Perfect, I need this.” 

They grabbed their stuff and filed into the hotel. It was a nice place in a quiet neighborhood, nothing fancy but neat and tidy. Agatha and Peggy were roommates on the road and back in their home city of Kenosha. 

“Do you think there will be any airmen at this joint?” Peggy opened her suitcase and searched for her favorite dress.

“Doubtful. When’s the last time we saw any flyboys? They’ve probably been discharged already and are back with their wives.” Agatha sat at the small wooden desk in the room and carefully ripped out a page from her journal. 

“Boo, I’m glad the war is behind us, but I’ll sure miss those servicemen. They knew a thing or two about pleasing a woman.” 

Agatha put her hands over her ears, “Lalalalalala. I don’t want to hear any of that.”

“Oh, you prude! You could clean up if you’d just open up a bit. The guys are always begging to dance with you.”

“I’m just…not interested. I want to focus on the game. Is that so wrong?”

“A girl deserves a little fun now and then. Was Ralph the last bloke you were with? It’s been over five years, Angel.” Peggy looked at her with a mix of pity and concern. 

“I’m fine! I just haven’t seen anyone that looks interesting. I don’t want to be stuck in another marriage of convenience with some guy who doesn’t speak his mind, who expects me to do everything. Who would want to be with a divorcée anyway? They’d probably run for the hills if they heard that.”

“You’re gorgeous, my friend. Any man would be lucky to have you. Let’s see what’s on the menu tonight. Promise me, you’ll at least look.”

“I have to write this letter first. If I can’t get this out, I’m not going at all.” Agatha turned back to face the paper and pen. 

“Don’t be silly. Just say you’re sorry. It’s not that hard! I’m going to go take a shower, get writing!” Peggy grabbed her towel and washkit, then closed the door. 

Agatha leaned against her elbows on the desk. Just start. You heard Peggy, it’s easy. She picked up the pen. 

Dear Rio, 

She stared at the paper. What she wanted to write was: How dare you? If you try that nonsense again, I’ll do much worse than a pathetic push. You’re lucky that I was held back. I can’t wait to wipe that smug smile off your face. 

She knew better than to try to cross Coach. She tried again. 

Dear Rio, 

I deeply apologize for my actions at our last game. I let my emotions get the better of me, and I should have remained calm. I was upset at the pitch you threw, but I should not have charged you. If you pitch in the strike zone next time, we won’t have a problem. 

I hope you will forgive me. I am very sorry. 

Sincerely, 

Agatha Harkness

There. Done. She found a spare envelope in her bag and sealed the letter inside. She wrote Rio’s name on the front and put it with her equipment. 

Peggy came back in a few minutes later, looking as fresh and dewey as ever. “What are you wearing tonight? Do you want to borrow one of my dresses?” 

“What’s wrong with my clothes?” Agatha held up one of her usual getups. 

“Nothing, nothing. It’s just…they say ‘old maid’ rather than ‘on the market’, if you catch my drift.”

Agatha put a hand in front of her mouth, feigning hurt. “Fine, you’ve caught me on a weak night. I’m your doll. Dress me up.” 

Peggy didn’t have to do much. Agatha was stunning, but she tended to hide behind modest attire and subdued makeup. 

“Final touches. Pout your lips.” Peggy swiped a bold red lipstick across her lips. It was a few shades darker than the standard issue one they had to wear at every game. 

Agatha looked at herself in the full-length mirror in the room. Her eyes widened, and she spun around to take in the full effect. 

“Hmmmm…not bad.”

“Not bad? That’s the thanks I get? I’d jump your bones if you’d let me.” Peggy exclaimed. 

Agatha swatted her lightly, “Thanks, Peg. I owe you.”

Peggy checked her watch, “I know. I’m keeping a tally, by the way. We’ve got ten minutes. Let’s go.”

They carefully snuck down the stairs, and other gals joined their quiet parade out of the hotel. Not everyone joined as some were tired, some didn’t approve, some had other plans. But Peggy, Agatha, Wanda, Lupe, Dottie, and Rose all broke free into the warm night. 

It was explicitly prohibited for players to drink or smoke in public places, but a girl’s gotta have a little fun now and then. The veterans knew how to avoid the prying eyes of the chaperones and coaches and cut loose on occasion.  

“Jeepers, Angel! Are you on the prowl tonight? You look amazing.” Lupe whistled as she took in Agatha’s full transformation. 

“Peggy wanted to give me a makeover.” Agatha shrugged but couldn’t hide her smile. 

“You’re going to wish you brought your bat. The guys are going to be rushing to get your dance card.” Wanda laughed. 

It only took about fifteen minutes to get to the bar. “How’d you hear about this place, Peggy?” Agatha asked. 

“One of the spectators gave me a matchbook from here. Said they serve cheap drinks to pretty tourists.” She tossed her hair and batted her eyelashes. 

She opened the door, and they were immediately hit with the noise of a jukebox blaring. The place was almost overflowing with people. 

“Wow, popular spot.” Lupe observed and immediately beelined to the bar. The rest followed. 

The bartender, a well-dressed gentleman in his 40s, nodded to them. “A Manhattan, please.” Agatha ordered. 

“Hey, isn’t that…” Wanda pointed over across the room where several of the Sallies’ players were sitting and drinking their cocktails. 

Agatha looked and her eyes immediately found the person who had been occupying 80% of all her thoughts for the last several hours. Rio was sipping on a beer bottle and smiling at something that one of the other girls just said. Agatha’s hands were unconsciously squeezing into fists. 

“Excuse me. Care for a dance?” A young man tapped Agatha on the shoulder, and she snapped out of her anger. He was tall, clean cut with cropped blonde hair. Probably six or seven years younger than her but looked harmless. 

“Sure. Wanda, hold my drink.” She passed her glass over and took the outstretched hand. He led her to the dance floor in the middle of the space. 

Duke Ellington was playing. He led them through a classic two-step. “Name’s Frank.”

“Agatha.” 

They made their way around the floor. He was a decent dancer and Agatha was no dead hoofer either. She had always loved to move. As they spun, Agatha felt a strange sensation. Like someone was watching her. She tried to ignore it. Tried to focus on the music, the steps, this man, but it became impossible to ignore. 

Her eyes tracked along the bar. Nothing. Along the other dancers. No. 

To the tables of patrons. There. 

Sitting alone now, gripping her beer bottle like it was daring to run, and shooting daggers straight in her direction. 

Rio Vidal.

Agatha watched to see if maybe she was imagining it, but as her partner led them up and down the floor, Rio’s eyes followed. 

“What’s her deal?” Agatha muttered aloud on accident. 

“Huh?” Frank leaned back in confusion. 

“Sorry, nothing.” They kept swaying to the tune but she felt an energy building inside. “Actually, give me one second. I’ll be right back.” 

Agatha crossed the room in just six long strides. 

She slammed her hands onto the table in front of Rio. “Why are you staring at me?”

The pitcher took a slow drink from her bottle and never broke eye contact. She leaned back. 

“Don’t you dare shrug at me again! Say something!” Agatha’s patience was waning. She couldn’t understand what was going on. 

“I like your dress.” Her voice was deeper than she was expecting with a subtle hint of sarcasm like she might be telling the truth or might be lying through her teeth. 

“You…wait, what?” Agatha’s frustration turned to surprise. She felt her cheeks start to warm involuntarily. 

“You look nice.” Rio spoke again as if it was the most obvious statement in the world. Agatha stared back in disbelief. 

Wanda ran up, having spotted them from the bar. “Agatha, come on. I don’t think you should be over here.”

Agatha felt herself being pulled away quickly before she could even respond. She likes my dress? “Wait, Wanda-”

“What are you thinking? Have you lost your head again? You know we’re not meant to fraternize with any other teams! We could get fined if anyone reports you.” Wanda gave her back her drink and sat down on one of the barstools, looking around erratically to see if anyone saw. 

“Did you see the way she was staring at me? I could hardly focus on the music. I could feel her eyes tracking me like prey. It was unnerving!” Agatha downed her drink in one go. 

“Woah there, tiger. Don’t forget we still have a game tomorrow. She’s just trying to get in your head. Probably upset that we beat them.” 

“You good, babe?” Peggy stumbled over after finishing her gallop around the dancefloor with a young gentleman. 

“Yeah, I think I might head back soon.” Agatha said. 

“Awwww, don’t be a drip!” Peggy jested with her and put her hand up to get the bartender’s attention. She ordered three shots of whiskey. They both clinked their glasses and put the drink away fast. 

Wanda declined hers, so Agatha and Peggy shot back half each. “You two are crazy. I’m going to go back and check on the twins. Don’t stay out all night!”

“Anyone catch your eye? How was that blonde guy that you were dancing with?” Peggy probed. 

Agatha had forgotten about him already. Her mind was still reeling from Rio. How could one person be so disarming? What was it about this woman that made her feel like this?

“Agatha?” Peggy pushed as she hadn’t answered. 

“Sorry, no. I don’t see anyone worthwhile. I’m going to see if I can catch up with Wanda, sorry Pegs!” She took off out the bar doors, suddenly feeling very closed in and claustrophobic.  

“Oh well, more for me.” Peggy looked around the room for her next suitor. 

Agatha practically threw herself out of the door and felt immediate relief at the warm summer night’s air. She didn’t realize how suffocating the bar was with the combination of hot dancing bodies, cigarette smoke, and the strong tang of alcohol. 

She couldn’t see Wanda, but it was very dark out and the streetlamps barely provided any illumination. She decided to walk back alone. 

Her mind went to the one place she was trying to avoid. Why did Rio say she liked my dress? She must be trying to rattle me. 

Well, it’s working. 

No, it’s not. 

Ummm, yes it is. 

Stop thinking about her. 

I can’t.

You have to. 

Why don’t you stop thinking about her?

I’m you. We’re we.

We need to stop thinking about her!

Agatha let out a loud groan as she fought herself in her mind and realized she probably looked insane. She looked around and over her shoulder but was still alone on the street. Luckily (hopefully) nobody had heard her or seen her. 

We’re fine. We’re fine. 

Ugh, no I mean, I’m fine! 

Must be that last shot of whiskey. I think I’m just tired.

Maybe a good night’s sleep is all I need…as long as I don’t dream about her. 

Chapter 3: Batting practice

“No pants wearing, tough-talking female soft-baller will play on any of our four teams.”

Ken Sells, league president, 1943–44. Cincinnati Enquirer.

 

Agatha woke up to the door opening and closing quickly. She blearily opened her eyes to see Peggy tip-toeing in wearing the same clothes from last night. 

“Good night?” She rasped out. 

Peggy removed her heels and fell back on her single bed. “Nothing special. I’ll give it a 4/10. And that might be generous”

“That bad?”

“He was enthusiastic, but it was also perhaps the quickest seven minutes of my life.” Peggy cracked up and Agatha joined in, both of them giggling on their beds.  

“What time is it?” Agatha clumsily reached for her watch. 

“Just after 8. I’m going to try to get another hour of shut eye.” Peggy stripped off her dress and shimmied under the covers in just a slip. 

“I’m going to get breakfast while I can. Do you want me to bring you anything?”

“A muffin if they have any.” Peggy spoke with her eyes still closed.

Agatha threw on a casual skirt and blouse, then hurried downstairs to the hotel dining room. The rest of the team was already seated and eating the provided breakfast. She took a free chair next to Wanda. 

“Billy, eat your eggs. Leave Tommy’s plate alone.” She scolded her son who was more interested in his brother’s food. 

“Mom! I want some more bacon!” Billy whined. 

“Here, kid.” Agatha picked up her spare piece with her fork and deposited it on the boy’s plate. 

“Thanks, Aunty Aggie.” He grinned and grabbed the crispy piece with his hand. 

“Use a fork!” Wanda admonished him with a sigh. “These kids, I swear.” 

“Are you ready for today?” Agatha asked. 

“Of course, you?” 

“I’m always ready.” 

Wanda lowered her voice, “What time did Peggy crawl in?” 

“A few minutes ago.” Agatha chuckled, “She’ll be ready.”

“She better be. We don’t want to have Helen at shortstop again. Remember last time Peggy was sick? I think a scarecrow could have stopped more balls than Helen.”

“Ladies!” Lilia walked in and drew their attention immediately, “remember to look your best today. We got a new shipment of makeup, so if anyone needs a new tube of lipstick or eyeliner, come see me. Iron your uniforms before we go. If you would like a lucky crystal, I have a few spares.”

“There she goes again with the crystals.” Agatha muttered under her breath to Wanda, who covered her mouth to stifle her laughter. 

“She means well. Tommy, chew with your mouth closed, dear.” Wanda said. 

“Bus is waiting out front. I expect you all lined up and ready soon. Coach said the batting lineup is the same as yesterday. Lois is the starting pitcher. Agatha is back in at catcher. Rest remains the same. Dismissed.” Lilia turned and headed outside to talk with the driver. 

The chaperone had been with the Comets since the beginning. Lilia was a widower from the Great War and had previously worked as a nurse in a public hospital. She was a healer at heart and deeply spiritual, but her aloof exterior often put people off. 

Her bag was a thing of legend among the players. Somehow, she always seemed to have exactly what they needed. Chamomile extracts for sliding wounds, ginger for upset stomachs, dressings and bandages, arnica for bruises, and a cheeky flask of whiskey to calm the nerves. She suffered no fools, but she cared about these players like they were her own children. 

Lilia never had her own kids. She loved her husband, but she couldn’t envision a future where the two of them raised anything besides hell. When he died, she moved on from that once held dream. She kept her career at the Milwaukee General Hospital and lived a fairly solitary existence. 

It was pure chance that one of the doctors approached her one day and asked if she had any interest in baseball. She liked it well enough and attended a few Milwaukee Creams games, the local minor league team. 

“They’re starting up a ladies league all over the Midwest.” He explained. “My daughter was contacted by a scout who saw her playing in the local softball circuit. Apparently they are searching for talent all over the country!”

“Wow, she must be a real ace. Girls playing ball…I’ve never heard of such a thing.” Lilia said. 

“Well, they are also looking for women to work as chaperones on the teams. There are going to be a lot of young girls, and I think they want some guardrails in place. I play golf with one of the managers, I could put in a good word, if you’re interested.”

“What do I know about chaperoning some baseball players?” Lilia scoffed. 

“I would hate to lose you. You’re one of the most competent nurses on the night shift, but if I may be frank, you don’t seem particularly happy here.” The doctor looked at her sadly. Lilia didn’t realize anyone paid her any mind, that anyone saw her at all. 

“Do you really think they would consider a kooky old broad? I don’t know if I have much to offer…” 

“I’ll talk to Max and put your name forward. The pay is decent, they’ll pay for travel and meals, and you’ll get to see more of the country. I hope you consider it.”

The offer and contract arrived via a large manila envelope to her home. She accepted immediately, rented out her home, and moved down to Kenosha. It had been an adjustment to say the least, but as she got to know all the players, listened to their stories, and got to know them, she had to admit - she loved it. 

The girls finished their meal and went to get ready. Agatha grabbed a spare blueberry muffin for Peggy.

“Wake up, sunshine!” She called out as she entered their shared room. 

Peggy groaned, “Is it that time already?” Agatha tossed over the muffin which she caught effortlessly. 

“Eat your breakfast and get dressed. Time to play.” Agatha checked her uniform and saw it was still extremely clean as she hadn’t had much chance to dirty it yesterday. She dusted some stray specks off her green cap. She folded the dress and put it in her bag. She then applied her makeup as she was taught during Charm School those first two years of the league. 

She still remembered those classes so clearly.

“Ladies, ladies. You will not be allowed on the field without a proper face of make-up, so I suggest you listen up!” The instructor was a beautiful woman in her 50’s that had looked like she would rather be anywhere else.  

“I thought we were here to play baseball, not pose for a magazine cover…” Peggy whispered to Agatha which elicited a snort. 

“Something funny?” The woman had raised her brow at the two women sitting in the back, who both shook their heads. 

“Right, now remember, there are two eyebrows, not one. If you keep your face in order, you’ll be able to perform well for the fans. They are here to see women play ball, not scrappy tomboys.”

In addition to make-up tips, they were taught proper manners and etiquette. They were shown how to elegantly walk up and down stairs, how to walk in heels, how to drink tea with their pinkies out, how to properly cross their legs when sitting, everything the league thought was becoming of a lady. 

Most of the players rolled their eyes at the lessons, but the subtext was clear. Anyone who looked or acted like a man, anyone who could be accused of being queer, was not welcome here. No short hair, no smoking or drinking in public, no pants, no swearing. 

Peggy and Agatha finished getting ready and threw their bags on their back and took the stairs quickly. Everyone was in a line for the bus. 

“Nice of you to join us, you two.” Lilia said. 

“Apologies, ma’am.” Peggy uttered as they stepped aboard. 

The ride passed without drama, and they were at the stadium in no time. 

“Let’s warm up. Get changed and meet me on the field.” Coach Grant looked excited this morning. The team was on a winning streak. It was their best start ever, and he wanted to keep the momentum going. 

Agatha put on her uniform then started fastening on her catcher’s gear. First the leg protectors, then her chest guard, and finally her mask. She always felt like a turtle in all her gear. It was her armor against the world. 

“Ready, Flo?” She called out to Lois Florentine, the starting pitcher today. She had joined last season and had a really impressive curveball. What she lacked in speed, she more than made up for in accuracy.  

“Yup, I’m ready.” 

They walked together to the warm-up mounds along the first base side. The seats were slowly starting to fill up with fans. Agatha stood behind the line while Lois took to her spot. She tossed a few regular throws over to warm up her arm. Agatha threw them back gingerly as well. Then she crouched in position and threw up her glove, ready to take the real practice throws. 

“A bit too far inside.” She called out as one of the curveballs missed the zone. 

“Got it!” Lois said back and re-adjusted her grip. 

The next ball flew right into her glove, perfect placement. “Nice one!” She remarked. 

Agatha glanced over to the other side of the field where the opposing team pitcher was warming up. No sign of Rio. 

Lois threw for a while longer until they were called back to the locker room. 

“Alright Comets, we’ve got a great chance to pull out a win today. We beat these gals yesterday, and I think we have it in us to do it again. Just stay focussed and remember the basics. Make the easy play, don’t try to be a hero, and stay calm. We’ve been swinging the bat well, so keep it up. It looks like their starting pitcher is Evie Risinger. She has a mean change-up, but if you keep your eye on the ball, it shouldn’t be an issue. 

Look to me for any signals when you’re up to bat or on base. I believe in this team. Let’s show ‘em what we got!” He extended his hand and everyone piled their hands on top of each other. 

Peggy led the chant, “Win on three! One, two, three.”

“WIN!” They all shouted in unison and raised their hands together. 

They started to walk out to the dugout, but Agatha heard the coach call her back. 

“Did you do what I asked?” He asked her. 

She went to her bag and grabbed the envelope. “Here. I didn’t see the pitcher out there earlier. Can you give it to their coach when you go to talk to the umpire?”

He took it from her and nodded, “Glad to have you back. Don’t make this a habit.” 

“Aye, aye, cap.” Agatha went to join the team out on the field as the opposition was hitting first. 

“All good?” Wanda said in a low voice before heading to her position.

“Just fine, Little Red. Let’s have a good game.” 

Agatha sheepishly greeted the home plate umpire as she took her spot behind the plate and started tossing the ball with Lois, “Hiya Rich. Sorry about yesterday.”

“Harkness. I’m keeping a close eye on you. Don’t test me.” He warned before donning his own mask. 

“Me? Well, I never!” She replied, which got a laugh out of him. The first batter from The Sallies stepped up into the box. 

It was a quick 1,2,3 inning as the defense was solid. The next few innings were much of the same. Neither team could produce anything exciting. The pitching on both sides was great. Agatha got on base in the third but was left stranded. 

The entire time, she kept her eyes on the opposing team’s bullpen, waiting to see the relief pitchers warming up. Would Rio come in again? Did she have a rest day today?

She couldn’t see her sitting on the bench. Maybe she’s off. Teams often rotated their pitchers to allow their arms to rest and recover. Overhand pitching was incredibly stressful on the joints compared to underhand. 

By the sixth inning, there was no sign of the dark and stormy pitcher. Agatha felt the tension release from her shoulders. Esther Jones came in instead. She had been in the league since the beginning. Agatha remembered her from the try-outs. 

Originally, a center fielder Esther had a rocket of an arm and could throw from the back wall all the way to second base on a good day. The coach had begged her to switch to pitching this year and helped train her. She lacked confidence on the mound, but when she was in the zone, good luck getting a hit. 

Agatha was up. She kicked her feet in the dirt and took her stance. She lifted the bat from her shoulder and trained her eyes on the pitcher. There was nowhere on Earth where she felt more in control and yet completely on edge as the batter’s box. It was an intoxicating high. It could end in euphoria or heartbreak, and it was all up to you - whether you could read the pitcher, whether you could swing in time, whether you could connect.

It was a pressure cooker, and she lived for it. 

She let the first pitch go by. A strike but low and inside. She hated those. 

“Lost your edge, Angel?” The opposing catcher, Carson, spoke lowly as she stood and threw the ball back. 

Agatha ignored it. Heckling by the catcher was expected. She was a master at it so couldn’t complain. She dug in again. 

The ball was coming right down the middle. Bingo. She twisted her hips and allowed the momentum to carry the wooden bat down and across the plate. 

Going, going…not quite gone. It landed in the gap between right and center field. Agatha took off like a bullet towards first base. She watched the ball roll to the fence - she could make it to second if she hustled. 

She stood up on second to the roaring cheers of the crowd. A quality double. She clapped her hands as the adrenaline pumped through her. Her breath was coming heavy and fast after that sprint, but she had to immediately get ready to run again as Fern was up next. 

Her eyes went to the opposing team's dugout where she thought she saw a flash of brown hair turn and retreat out. But the pitcher was already throwing out the next toss. Come on, head in the game. 

Agatha scored on a hard-hit single to the outfield by the next batter, and the team won the game by two runs. The Comets were now ahead in the series and adding to their win total. There were still two more months of regular season games before the playoffs, but they were all hoping to qualify after missing out for the last three years. 

Coach Grant addressed them all as they sat in the dugout, “Great game today everyone! That’s what I want to see out there. The defense was impenetrable, and there were some great plays from both infield and outfield. Let’s meet at the park across from the hotel at 5 for some batting practice. I would like to increase our on-base percentage, we should be scoring more runs. See you then.” 

They all got changed, got back on the bus, and headed to their temporary road home. 

“Great arm today, Flo.” Wanda tapped the starting pitcher on the arm. She was sitting a few rows in front. 

“Thanks, Red. I was nervous as usual, but the Sallies still seem very green. I was worried when Shaw came up, but it makes it so much easier having you all behind me.”

“Keep throwing like you did, and you won’t even need us. You’ll be striking them left and right!” Wanda smiled. 

“Aw shucks, don’t embarrass me!”

“Yeah, where’s my compliments? What’s a pitcher without a talented catcher?” Agatha jumped into the conversation. 

“Sorry, yes tell us more about how challenging it is to crouch in a single spot and catch a ball?” Peggy always took a chance to razz Agatha. 

Fossey turned around and added her two cents, “Spend a day in the outfield, Angel. Then tell us which position is tougher!” 

“Ummmm, the infield gets the most action. We have to constantly be aware and ready to react.” Peggy replied. 

“You certainly know all about getting action…” Agatha murmured out, which earned her a swift smack on the head from the magazine Peggy was holding. 

“Every position is important and challenging in its own unique ways.” Wanda tried to placate the growing arguments brewing at the back of the bus. Her practice with the twins came in handy even with adults.  

“Sure, but can y’all admit that first base is the most pressure?” Dottie Simpson threw her perspective into the ring. The charming Southern belle could never watch a discussion pass her by. 

“Are you kidding? Pitchers have the entire weight of the team on our shoulders! The game doesn’t start or stop without us. The win or loss is recorded next to our name.” Flo reignited the debate, and the players continued to bicker for the rest of the ride. 

They reached the hotel, and everyone headed in. Some decided to take a nap before practice, others read in their rooms, some ran to town for some errands. 

“Fancy a smoke?” Peggy nudged Agatha while holding up her pack of Lucky Strikes. Agatha followed her out to the back porch where they took up seats on the worn deck chairs. 

Peggy pulled out her cherished metal Zippo lighter. It had a plane embossed on one side. She had been given it a few years back by a handsome soldier who had just returned from Germany. 

“Whatever happened to that fella?” Agatha asked as she watched Peggy rubbing her thumb along the lighter. 

“Steve?” Peggy looked up and snapped out of whatever memory she had been in. “I never heard from him again. I gave him my address in Salem, but he never wrote. I thought…well doesn’t matter now anyway.” 

She passed a cigarette over and gracefully lit her own then handed the lighter across. The two friends sat in a comfortable silence for a few minutes. 

“Do you want to get married?” Agatha broke the spell. 

“Sure. If the right guy asked, why not?”

“What about Fred?” Agatha knew about Peggy’s previous engagement to Fred Wells that had been called off. 

She took a long drag and blew out an impressive cloud, “He wasn’t the right guy.” 

“Was it because he didn’t want you to play ball?”

“That was a big part of it. I don’t want to be with a man who can’t respect my dreams, my desires. Do you think you’ll get married again?” Peggy stared out into the distance. 

“I can’t imagine a man that could handle me. Ralph was a mistake. A hasty choice to try to escape my mother’s clutches. I haven’t met any man who didn’t look at me like I was some…foregone conclusion. Not a person with my own thoughts and feelings, just another box for them to tick in their life goals. I have my own life. I don’t want to give it up to be some doting housewife.” 

“I wouldn’t mind being an old spinster with you. We could move to the big city, Boston. We could get an apartment near Fenway and get season tickets. I could try to become a detective. You could go to college.” Peggy tapped out some ash into the glass tray on the table beside them. 

“Don’t go getting a gal’s hopes up. That sounds pretty swell to me.” Agatha took another slow pull of smoke and smiled at Peggy. 

Peggy dropped the simmering butt and held out her hand. Agatha cocked an eyebrow but decided to humor her and shook it firmly. 

“I’m going to hold you to that. If we’re both still single when we turn 40, we’re moving in together.” Peggy declared as she still held Agatha’s hand. 

“Deal. Let’s be real though, you’ll probably be married to some rich suitor by the end of the season.” 

“No takebacks! You shook on it.” Peggy stood up and stretched her back. She rolled her shoulders to relax. 

“I never go back on a promise.” Agatha crossed her heart with her finger and also stood. She kicked her legs back to get the blood flowing. Crouching behind home plate for such long stretches was tough on her lower leg muscles. 

Agatha grabbed the newspaper from the lobby and went to relax in their room for a bit. Before long, they were all headed back out to the local park across the street to work on their batting. 

“Remember to stay loose. When you tighten up on the bat, your reaction time gets worse. You want to be able to see the ball and immediately decide whether to swing or not. Keep your posture balanced and your head steady. When the right pitch comes your way, keep your bat level to try to meet the ball squarely.” Coach Grant tried his best to impart his batting wisdom on the players. They had a spare glove on the grass as the stand-in home plate, and he was demonstrating his stance and positioning for them. 

They each took turns practicing their swings and stances. He would make small adjustments to their arms, their grip, their feet. There were so many more mechanics to baseball than most realized. 

Coach started throwing some pitches to practice actually hitting. After a few rounds, Agatha spoke up, “Can you throw us the heat, Coach? We need to be ready for that Sallies’ pitcher. She’s gotta be the fastest in the league.”

He wasn’t a pitcher but had a strong arm and excellent accuracy. “Good point, Angel. Timing is the most important thing to remember with fast pitchers. When you’re on deck, start watching and timing out when to swing. When you’re expecting it, you can be on the front foot and ready to smash it.”

Agatha took two strikes and two balls before she finally connected and sent a ball flying. She did it three more times and felt her confidence growing. 

Next time she faced Rio, she was going to be ready.

Chapter 4: The Payoff pitch

“Each player who steps up to the plate will be as trim as a ballet dancer in her short-skirted pastel uniform. Every curl will be in place, her lipstick on precisely, and a tsk! tsk! to the girl who hits a homer while her face is shiny.”

Jayne Miller, Freeport Journal-Standard, May 28, 1943

 

Agatha woke up early on Monday even though their third game wasn’t until 6pm that night. As part of the league rules, they played each team four times before moving on to the next series. It helped draw fans from the local area and reduced the constant travel by allowing them longer stretches in each city. 

Agatha looked over at Peggy who was still fast asleep. Even in her slumber, she looked like a movie star. 

She felt an excessive amount of energy and nerves building in her. She grabbed her sneakers and changed into her tan gymsuit. She crossed the road and started jogging around the park. There was a paved sidewalk around the entire perimeter. Her shoes beat against the hard ground in a satisfying rhythm. 

It was early morning, but the summer heat was already rearing its ugly head. She felt sweat starting to form all over her body, but the pleasant hum of exertion was pulsing through her veins. She welcomed the distraction from her obsessive thoughts about the game tonight that had been running on a loop since she woke up 

Will Rio be pitching? Will she try to hit me again? What will I do if she does? Why did she stare at me at the bar that night? Why did she compliment me? Where did she come from? Why did she throw a ball at my head? Where did she learn to pitch so fast? Why did she join the league now? Where had she been? 

The steady monotony of her jog pushed all those thoughts to the side. All she could think about was taking the next step, taking the next breath. 

After thirty minutes of non-stop circles around the park, she finally slowed to a walk as she approached the water fountain. The lukewarm water had a bitter aftertaste like metal, but she was so thirsty she didn’t care. She went back to the hotel to take a shower. 

“Jesus, is it raining outside? Why are you drenched?” Peggy was sitting at the vanity, brushing her hair. 

“I had too much on my mind. I went for a run.” 

“A run? Don’t you get enough exercise around the bases?” Peggy couldn’t imagine running outside of practice or a game. 

“I want to make sure I’m ready for tonight.”

“Worried about that pitcher? Surely she won’t try to bean you again. If she does, she’ll have to deal with both me and Wanda.” 

“Thanks Peg. She’d be crazy to try that stunt again.” Agatha grabbed her towel and went to wash up with a cold shower. 

The day passed in a haze of preparation and distraction. Agatha found herself again in the locker room suiting up in her gear as Lupe was ready to warm up. She steeled herself as they entered the field. She glanced across the infield and saw Mickey Sosa getting ready. 

Right, Rio is not a starter. She’ll come in later, if at all. Agatha looked and couldn’t see Rio in the dugout yet. 

Mickey was talented. She used to play on the Lassies but got traded last year. A recruit from Cuba, she was only 20 years old but her sidearm was menacing. It was hard to read the trajectory of each pitch and when she added some spin, it was a hard lesson in timing.

The anthem blared, the announcer started the game, things were underway. The first two innings were over with little action. No hits from either team. Agatha knocked a line drive straight to Greta at first base during her first at bat, which was disappointing, but she was just biding her time, waiting for her rematch with the pitcher who haunted her dreams. 

Mickey was on a roll, and the Comets only had two hits when they reached the sixth inning. Agatha could see the strain on her face though as her pitch count increased. She was going to have to be pulled soon. 

Wanda was up and belted one into the gap over second base. She made it safely to first, and the Sallies manager came out to speak with Mickey. 

Here we go. Agatha watched the dugout and saw her. Rio grabbed her glove and hopped up from the bench. She didn’t walk. She swaggered out to the mound with all the confidence and moxie of a major leaguer. God, she’s insufferable. 

The umpire let her throw a few practice pitches out before Rose “Horsey” Horstman, their third baseman, stepped up. She was a power threat and looked ready. She was a native of Kenosha so had a huge hometown following. Come on, Horsey. Wipe that smile off her face. 

She fouled off the first pitch, swung at a ball, then struck out looking at a nasty change-up. Three pitches was all it took. Agatha saw the runaway glee on Rio’s face as she heard the umpire call that final strike. It’s like she was feeding on it - gaining power from the control. 

One out. Peggy was up. “I’ll get her for you, Angel.” She blew a kiss and walked over. 

First pitch was a strike that zipped by before Peggy could even swing. Agatha watched her shake her head and readjust her grip on the bat. Second pitch missed barely. Third pitch and Agatha heard the wooden crack of the bat as Peggy hit a grounder to short. McCready fielded it easily and tossed it across to Greta for an out.

“Start your bat almost as soon as the ball leaves her hand. It’s faster than you expect.” Peggy came and sat down beside Agatha. “You’d think with her ridiculous long wind-up, there would be time to see it coming.”

“Thanks, I’ll get her.” Agatha grabbed her favorite bat. She went to the on-deck circle and watched Rio closely. Shirley was batting now. Agatha practiced her swing as Rio pitched and tried to adjust to her speed. Shirley lined one down the third base line and got onto first. 

She’s just another pitcher. You’ve done this hundreds of times. Agatha gave herself a pep talk then took her position next to home plate. Rio was watching her, taking in her every movement like she was cataloguing it for later. Agatha watched right back, intent on not being intimidated. 

She spread her feet, brought her arms up, held her bat high, focussed. Rio took her time. She accepted the catcher’s call then stood up. The wind-up, the pitch.

Agatha tried to stay loose. Tried not to tense up. She watched the ball leave Rio’s hand. It was barely a throw. It was a lob - way outside. Is she intentionally walking me?

“Ball.” The umpire yelled from behind her. 

Again, Agatha got set. Rio pitched. A slow throw far outside the zone. 

“Ball.” 

Carson got up from her position behind the plate and jogged out towards Rio. Agatha saw a look of confusion on her face. Rio was clearly not following the catcher’s signs. They had a hasty discussion, and Carson came back and extended her mitt again. Agatha got ready. 

“Ball.” That was three balls, no strikes. One more and Rio will have granted Agatha a free pass to first base.

Agatha stared out at the mound. Rio was looking straight at her. They held eye contact for what felt like minutes but was probably no more than five seconds. And this time, the edges of Rio’s lips lifted and she gifted Agatha a short, deliberate wink. A mirror of her own from their previous meeting. 

Agatha felt her heart start to pound. She felt her nerves start to shimmer under her skin, her hands started to sweat against the grain of her bat. Rio’s arms went back over her head, her leg kicked out like a cheerleader, her throwing arm swung around. 

The ball was a blur. “Strike.” 

Oh god. She did all that on purpose. She threw three balls just to prove that she can. Just to show she doesn’t need them. 

The next ball looked like it was coming straight for Agatha’s waist. She held off and watched in horror as the ball broke and curved right into Shaw’s glove. “Strike.”

No, no, no! She can’t get away with this. She can’t make a fool out of me. 

“Full count!” The umpire’s voice echoed out from behind her. There were three balls, two strikes. It all came down to the payoff pitch. If it was a ball, she would get to walk to first base. If it was a strike, she was out. If she hit it, she could maybe show up Rio. 

I have to hit this. 

She imagined the look on Rio’s face if she knocked the ball out of the park. She was so lost in her daydream that she missed the start of the pitcher’s wind-up. By the time she turned her attention to the incoming ball, it had already passed her by. 

“Strike! You’re out!” The fans in the stands roared their approval for their new star pitcher. 

Agatha was frozen. She couldn’t move. She couldn’t digest this news. The Sallies were already running off the field to get ready for their turn at bat, and she was stuck standing in the box. 

She watched Rio’s teammates patting her on the back as they laughed together. Agatha seethed. She turned and almost collided with Wanda. 

“Are you alright?” Wanda put a hand out to take the bat and reached out with her other hand to guide Agatha’s arm. 

She couldn’t speak. What could she say? Rio had just humiliated her in front of everyone. Her eyes threatened her with tears that she pushed down. Keep it together! There’s no crying in baseball! 

Wanda helped Agatha get her catcher equipment on. She kneeled and fastened the leg guards on for her. Agatha still hadn’t said a word. The audacity of that…of that…how could she….why did she…? Words failed. 

Betty was on the bench this game. She was the team’s rookie back-up pitcher and outfielder. She watched Wanda ease the chest plate over Agatha’s head. “Bad luck, Angel. That pitcher really seems to have it out for you! I’ve never seen anyone do that before. To throw away three balls and then pummel you with three strikes. That’s psychological warfare.” 

“Yeah. Thanks Betty. Psychological warfare, gee that’s a good one. If she wants a war, she picked the wrong enemy.” Something hardened in Agatha then. If Rio was going to go low, then Agatha could go lower. She could dig a trench so deep that Rio would never see the light of day. She wasn’t going to simply roll over and take it.

Lupe was still pitching for the Comets. She had incredible stamina and could often last the entire game if the defense was at their best. Agatha caught her pitches with ease. They were a well-oiled machine, and Agatha knew all her quirks and preferences. 

She favored her curveball but could only control it well in the first few innings. Her fastball was her secret weapon but couldn’t be thrown more than twice in a row. She wouldn’t ask for a slider unless the batter was jumpy and prone to swinging at anything. Agatha was one of the best catchers in the league for a reason. She could adapt to any pitcher. 

On top of that, she had a strong arm that could throw out runners trying to steal, and she could catch long balls thrown home to tag out anyone trying to score. It was no fluke that she had made the all-star team for the last three years. She was popular with the papers, a favorite of the fans, and she relished the attention. 

Being good at something, being good at this, made her feel alive, and for a long time she didn’t think that would ever happen again. Every team they beat was like a tonic to her soul. Every time she connected with the ball and saw the disappointment in the pitcher’s face, she felt like another year was added to her life. It was petty and some may say cruel or childish, but she loved it. 

She loved this game, and she was good at it. 

But out of nowhere, this rookie was blowing up her entire life. Rio Vidal had taken a sledgehammer to her beautiful baseball fantasy and was making her question if it was real at all. 

There was one out and a runner on second. For the first time in these three games, Rio was up to bat. Coaches generally put a pinch hitter, or a substitute, in for pitchers. Pitchers were often poor hitters because their entire focus was on their own craft. They didn’t have time for much else. But every player needed to hit at least once every few games to stay on the roster. Looks like Rio’s time was up. 

She approached the plate with the same cocky attitude that she seemed to move through the world with. This should be interesting. 

Rio stood straight up. No bend to her knees. The bat rested on her shoulder. She simply stood and stared at Lupe.

“What are you doing?” Agatha kept her voice quiet and spoke through her teeth. It was strictly against the rules for catchers to distract the batters but most umpires turned the other cheek.. 

Rio didn’t acknowledge her, didn’t turn her head, didn’t move a muscle. Lupe’s eyes caught Agatha’s. She lowered her hands between her thighs and threw down two fingers and quickly moved her hand to the left - the signal for curveball, inside. 

The pitcher nodded, acknowledging Agatha’s signal. Lupe had a long lunge and threw her curve. Agatha knew where it was going to hit, so she watched Rio out of the corner of her eye. 

As soon as the ball left Lupe’s hand, Rio loaded her swing and like a string being pulled back on a bow, she leaned back and swung her bat around with surprising energy. The ball launched between third base and the shortstop, and Rio took off. 

Wow, she’s got some wheels. Agatha watched Shirley throw the ball into second, and Rio stayed on first. How can she hit too? What baseball factory was she built in?

Greta was up to bat. Agatha squatted down and made the next call for Lupe. She kept an eye on first though. Rio was taking a big lead off the base. She’s not going to steal…is she?

First pitch was a tidy strike. Rio was observing Lupe closely now. She took an extra step closer to second. Agatha saw the subtle move and indicated for a pitch out, a deliberate throw high and out of reach of the batter. Lupe nodded and the moment she started her wind-up, Rio bolted. 

Agatha had already jumped up to stand. She caught the ball and fired it off like a bullet from a gun. Lupe ducked and the ball shot across the field right into Wanda’s glove. She tagged the runner with a second to spare. 

“Out!” The infield umpire raised his fist. Rio’s mouth dropped open. 

Gotcha. 

Agatha couldn’t wipe the smile off her face. She did it. Finally, she had one up on Rio. She watched the runner jog to her dugout and decided to wave at her. Rio caught the gesture and looked away. She threw herself down on the bench and disappeared from Agatha’s view. 

It was the eighth inning when Agatha was back up to bat again. The score was still tied at zero. Her team needed her here. Peggy was on second base. If she could just get a hit to the outfield, Peggy had the speed to make it home.  

Rio watched her approach the plate. She adjusted her cap, and Agatha watched her staring down at the ball in her glove. 

Come on, give me something I can hit. 

“Ball.”

“Ball.”

Not this again.

“Ball three.” 

Agatha got ready. Fool me once and all that. She wasn’t going to let Rio pull this same stunt again. 

“Ball four. Take your base.”

She walked me?! What? Why? 

Agatha tossed her bat, which Billy ran over to grab, before trotting over to first base. Rio didn’t look upset. She didn’t look surprised. She looked smug.

Fern was next in the line-up. Agatha and Peggy looked at each other. They loved being on base together as they were both menances to the defence. Agatha checked with Coach Grant who was standing in the third base box. He wasn’t indicating any signs. They were free to do as they pleased. 

Agatha pretended to stretch both arms out to her sides - the sign to take a large lead off. Peggy nodded. If they both took large leads, Rio wouldn’t know where to throw it. She might make a wild throw and allow them to steal. 

Peggy took her lead, and Agatha took a few steps. 

“Careful, Angel.” Greta warned her. Unusual for the opposing team, but Greta and Agatha had a friendly relationship. They had played together for a year and became relatively close during that time, well closer than most teammates got to Agatha. She purposefully kept most players at arm's length, preferring to focus on the game. 

Agatha figured Greta was just trying to throw her off, make her doubt herself. She took another little jump. Rio’s back was towards her, and she knew her long wind-up would make it hard for her to turn and catch her. Rio glanced toward Peggy. She didn’t look towards Agatha, so she took another shuffle towards second. 

The pitcher started to get ready and stood tall. Then, she spun and fired to the second baseman who moved her glove to Peggy, who slid head first back to the bag. 

“Safe!” the umpire called. That was close.

Agatha nodded to Peggy, but this time Peggy shook her head. She didn’t want to try that again. Agatha went anyway. She stepped off of first base and took two large steps. She had a wide stance like she was straddling a horse, ready to slide back if needed or take off if possible. 

Rio threw a strike that Fern missed. She got ready for the next pitch, and Agatha took her lead off again. She saw Rio peek over her shoulder, but she made no indication of a turn. Agatha took another step. 

Rio spun and threw to Greta who caught it at her chest. The first baseman moved her glove forward and tapped Agatha easily. 

“Out!”

Peggy watched in shock as Agatha had to make the walk of shame off the field. Rio twinkled her fingers at her and smiled. 

“What was that?” Coach Grant turned and barked at her. 

“I wasn’t even the lead runner. She had no reason to throw it at me!” Agatha tried to defend her silly mistake. 

“Yeah sure, but that pitcher obviously has it out for you. You should have known better. Why give her the opportunity?” 

“Sorry, Coach. I wanted to prevent a double play. I thought I could get back in time.” 

“You were careless. That rookie is making you forget all the fundamentals. Another mistake like that, and I’m going to have to consider your place on this team.” Coach did not want to take it that far, but Agatha was distracted. Rivalries could be good, but he had also seen how they could take down both players if they let it get personal. 

Agatha huffed. She was letting Rio get to her. She sat and watched the rest of the inning. Peggy made it home and scored for the Comets, which ended up being the game’s only run. They won again, and Agatha felt relief. She wouldn’t have to face Rio again for a while. She needed to reset and find her groove again. 

“Nice work, Peggy.” She clapped her friend on the back as they walked to the locker room. 

“Thanks. Can’t believe you let her pick you off at first!” 

“I didn’t let her do anything.” Agatha fought back. 

Peggy gave her a knowing look, “Sure, whatever you have to tell yourself, babe.”

“Why would I do that? I didn’t think she was going to go for me!” 

“You didn’t need to take such a huge lead. It’s not like you could steal second anyway. You were just trying to impress the girl.” Peggy had a teasing lilt to her tone. 

“Impress her? Yeah, right.” 

“Yeah…right.” Peggy saw the way that Agatha had changed. They had been friends for over a decade, played on this team for five years, and Rio had unleashed something new. Something had changed. 

Agatha brushed it off and joined the rest of the team in the locker room. They packed up and were back in the hotel within the hour. Peggy conked out almost immediately and was snoring as Agatha pulled out her journal. 

She wrote down her stats for the game, and then her thoughts. 

I don’t know what it is about Rio. I feel insane when I’m playing against her. It’s like she is reading my thoughts and knows my every pet peeve and how to exploit them. 

I hate striking out. She strikes me out. I hate getting picked off. She picks me off at first. I wrote in that apology letter that she should just stick to the strike zone, so what does she do? Throws mostly balls and walks me. 

At the very least, I got a hit off her and threw her out trying to steal. The score is not even yet, but I have some points on the board. 

She is like a headache. I can’t stop thinking about it no matter what else I do. Our team thoroughly crushed theirs though. She probably won’t play tomorrow, and we won’t face the Sallies again for a while. I can’t wait for the break. I need to stop thinking about this rookie. 

Agatha finished her notes and dug in her suitcase for the team calendar. They were headed back home next to face the Blue Sox. She flipped to August. Drat . 

They would go up against Rio’s team again at the start of next month. She circled the date with her pen. August 5th. A double header. 

She had four weeks until she had to see Rio again.

Four weeks to prepare.

Chapter 5: Safe at home

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“You’ve got to remember that this was a time when it was not a popular idea for a woman to be playing baseball. We didn’t realize what pioneers we were, but we were really out there as forerunners, as far as women’s sports are concerned. We constantly bucked public opinion. You know, they looked at you kind of funny and thought maybe you should be in the kitchen, or you should be at home sewing curtains, but you certainly shouldn’t be out there running the bases.”

Lavonne “Pepper” Paire

They easily won the next game, sweeping the series 4-0 against the Sallies. The four hour bus ride back the next morning was full of raucous singing and joyful noise as the Comets celebrated their big wins. The team was now tied for third in the league. There were still two and a half months to go before the playoffs, but this was their best start in history. 

Last year, the team had sunk to the cellar and placed last. It was disappointing and incredibly embarrassing given the talented players they had. The Comets reached the championship in the inaugural season in 1943 but struggled to repeat their success. 

Whether it was due to manager changes, injuries, trades, team cohesion, or better competition, who’s to say? Baseball was a fickle mistress. 

The only thing Agatha knew was that she wanted to get back on top. She was desperate to win the league for the team and finally snatch victory from the Peaches or the Belles. This year could be it. They had the skill, they just needed to perform. 

The bus arrived just after noon outside their home stadium and the girls said their goodbyes. Most lived within walking distance of the ballpark with local families, and the few others who lived farther out either drove or got picked up. They had three days off before their next game. 

“See you two! What time is practice tomorrow?” Wanda threw her bag over her shoulder as she shuffled the twins towards the parking lot. 

“3pm. See you then!” Peggy waved and grabbed her equipment. Agatha fell into lockstep with her as they walked the four blocks to 18th Avenue.
