They shared a room at the home of Gertrude and Pearl, two old maids who owned a ranch-style house and offered to host two ballplayers. Agatha and Peggy had roomed here for two years, having previously stayed with Mr. and Mrs. Remulout, before those two decided to move to Minnesota. 

Gertrude Davis and Pearl Thatcher were childhood friends, who were born and raised in Kenosha. They never married, despite multiple proposals, and seemed perfectly content to live together. After Pearl’s boyfriend died during the influenza pandemic in the 20’s, Gertrude decided to move into her house to cover the mortgage. Gertie, as she preferred to be called, worked at the local library, and Pearl was a secretary at a law firm. They were in their early 60’s but as active and as hilarious as ever. 

They didn’t know the first thing about baseball, but they loved the community and were always keen to help out. When the league asked for volunteer hosts for the new club, they put their hands up and offered up their spare bedroom. 

The house was kitschy, wood-panelled yet covered in seemingly endless framed pictures, and adorned with every kind of knick-knack. The two women each had their own bedroom, so Agatha and Peggy had to share the third bedroom, which was fine. It was a decent size and easily fit the two twin beds, plus there was a large closet and space for a tall dresser. There was a basement where they could store their baseball gear, and they even got their own bathroom, which was a real luxury. 

Pearl was a true chef in the kitchen, and she generously cooked for the whole house. They often invited Wanda and the boys over for dinner on off-nights, and other gals on the team would often beg for a dinner invite. 

The pair rarely came to any of the Comets games, but they always listened to the broadcast on the radio. If they ever heard anything about an injury to Agatha or Peggy, there would be bandages or antiseptic on the table when they came home. Or just because, they would walk in to milk and cookies waiting for them.

Agatha never had a doting or loving mother before and living here seemed to heal a small part of her soul. 

“We’re back!” She unlocked the door and burst through into the living room. 

“Pearl, wake up. The girls are here.” Gertie gently jostled her napping friend on the couch beside her. 

“Did you miss us?” Peggy asked playfully. 

“Missed the quiet maybe!” Pearl grumbled with her eyes still closed. 

“Congratulations on the wins. We listened to it all. How about that new pitcher? What was her name - Rhea? It was hard to hear the announcer properly.” Gertie sat up and put the newspaper on the coffee table. 

“Rio…” Agatha tried to disguise her disdain. 

“Right, Rio! She sounds like a real pain in the neck for you, Agatha. I thought Pearl was going to spill her hot chocolate when they said you had rushed over and pushed her down. What came over you, dear?”

Agatha bit her lip, the anger radiating off her in waves, and Peggy jumped in, “She had it coming. Throwing a ball at a batter’s head is way out of line. You could kill someone like that.”

“If you say so, but remember to be ladies out there. You shouldn’t be stirring up any trouble.” Gertie admonished them but then gestured to the kitchen. “There are some cheese sandwiches and fruit on the counter if you’re hungry.”

“Thanks! We’re starving.” They went to drop their bags in their room then devoured the leftover lunch. It was as delicious as always. Pearl could transform even the most simple meals into delectable treats. There was just something about a sandwich made by someone else that made it taste that much better. 

Agatha decided to have a catnap after eating. She had a hard time sleeping on the bus rides, between the constant noise and having to sit upright. She preferred to read, play cards, or study up on her notes for the next game. When she woke up, the sun was starting to set. 

“Hey, want to go catch a film? I saw Dottie and Lupe at the park, and they invited us.” Peggy was lounging on her bed. 

“Yeah, I could use the distraction.”

“We’re leaving in 10. Dottie said she’d pick us up.”

Agatha threw on her favorite lavender shirtwaist dress with small white polka dots and donned a pair of modest pumps. She wrangled her hair into presentable waves and grabbed her purse. 

The old Studebaker pulled up in front of their house, and they piled in and rode downtown to the old picture house. 

“What’s showing? It’s been a while since I’ve seen anything.” Agatha asked. 

They studied the posters outside the box office. 

“Looks like we can see Easter Parade with Fred Astaire and Judy Garland, or The Emperor Waltz with Bing Crosby and Joan Fontaine.” Lupe explained. 

“Oooh, I prefer Ms Judy if nobody objects.” Peggy threw out her opinion and the group agreed. 

“I’ll get the popcorn and drinks.” Agatha took off to the concession stand with Peggy. 

“Are you good?” Peggy asked as they waited in the long line. 

“Copacetic. Why? Do I look like I’m not good?” 

“I know you, Agatha. You look like you’re a thousand miles away.”

“Well, hopefully Fred and Judy can snap me out of it.” She joked. Truthfully, she was stuck on a rollercoaster named Rio that seemed to be running wild in her mind. Every choice she had made, every moment leading up to every moment they faced each other. What she could have done differently, what she wished she could have done. 

It was constant, but if she really dug deep…she didn’t hate it. No other player had ever challenged her to this level. Five years of playing and she had gotten complacent. Rio was like grabbing an electric fence - a shock to the system. 

“Agatha, you’re up.” 

She was pulled from the confines of her brain. “Huh? Oh, sorry. Two popcorns and four Cokes.” 

It was nice to sit in the dark and escape to a different world for a while. 

When the movie finished, they walked slowly to the car and decided to cruise around the neighborhood. 

“I can’t believe that people can really dance like that! Ann Miller really swept the floor; I wish she could teach me a thing or two.” Peggy was gushing over the musical numbers. 

“How about Fred! He’s still got it.” Dottie swooned. 

“Sure, but Judy and Ann do everything he does except backwards and in heels! There’s no comparison really.” Agatha pointed out and everyone laughed. 

“Can you imagine if Judy’s character played ball? That would be a gas!” Lupe added. 

“I don’t think she would even make it past try-outs. If the coach said go to left field, she could be running around searching for hours.” Peggy joked, as the character in the movie suffered from severe left-right confusion. 

The group shared other funny scenarios where this confusion could result in hilarious results, and before they knew it, they were back out the front of Peggy and Agatha’s house. 

“See you at practice tomorrow. Thanks for the ride!” Agatha called out as they walked up the pathway to the front door. 

The next day they arrived at their home park, Simmons Field at 2:30pm. They had moved into this new field this year, having played the previous years as Lakefront Stadium. Agatha would miss the stunning waterfront views but wouldn’t miss the fog and chilling lake breeze that seemed to hit every night, even in summer. 

“You good, Angel?” Wanda looked up as Agatha and Peggy walked into the dressing room. 

Agatha threw her mitt down, “What’s going on? Is everyone talking about me behind my back or something?” 

Wanda looked to Peggy for back-up and put her hands up, “Whoa whoa, nobody’s saying anything. I’m just checking that your head’s in the game. The Blue Sox have been on a winning streak too, so we need to be prepared.”

“Don’t get your knickers in a twist, doll.” Peggy reached out and placed a hand on Agatha’s shoulder. “Sometimes friends care about their friends, remember?”

Agatha resisted the urge to run and started putting her cleats on, “Sorry, Little Red. I’m fine. I think a nice hard practice will shake me from this funk.” 

“Don’t let Coach over hear that. He’ll have us running the bases all night.” Wanda said. 

They changed and walked out to the field with their mitts. The twins were carrying the bats and a bucket of balls each. They loved coming to every practice and every game. 

Tommy already talked about his dream to become a pitcher in the major league one day. Billy was less interested in the baseball of it all, but he loved helping the team and talking with the gals. He had a special connection with Agatha, despite her many efforts to steer clear of the boys. 

“Agatha! Do ya think you’ll hit a homer today?” He looked up at her and almost dropped the bucket he was carrying. 

She ruffled his curly hair, “I sure hope so, kiddo. Will you fetch it if I do?”

“Yes, definitely! Tommy and I are going to be picking up all the balls during batting practice.”

“That’s super helpful. Are you going to catch any?”

“Oh no! I don’t want to get hit in the head! Tommy’s been practicing, maybe he’ll catch some.”

Agatha laughed. He was a precious kid, and she couldn’t help caring for him. “If your mom buys you a mitt, you won’t get hit in the noggin. The mitt will protect you.”

“I don’t know…I’d rather just sit in the dugout. I just got the new Donald Duck comic! I love Huey, Dewey, and Louie. The pictures are so funny.” Billy always had his head in a comic book. 

At the mention of the characters, Agatha suddenly flashed back in time. 

“Mama, can I have a Mickey Mouse doll for Christmas?” Nicky asked her one day as they walked home from the corner store. 

“Is he your favorite character? I thought you liked Donald more?” 

Nicky didn’t respond right away. Agatha could almost see his brain working. He had the most adorable concentration face. “But Mickey has Pluto! I want to get a dog one day too.” 

“A dog! That’s a big responsibility. Dogs need lots of attention, care, and love. Not all dogs are as well-behaved as Pluto.” 

“I will walk it and feed it. I promise! Can I get a dog when I turn six?” 

Agatha had to close her eyes to try to stifle the tears. 

They would never get a dog. 

He would never turn six. 

She tried to focus on the actual little boy still waiting for a response, ‘You’ll have to show me later, okay?”

“Okay! I will!” He was nearly jumping with excitement and one of the bats fell from his hands and rolled away. 

Coach Grant was already on the field stretching his arm. 

“Bring it in, girls! We’ve got our first game against the Blue Sox on Saturday, and they are currently on a roll. They’ve been averaging at least three runs each game, as they have a strong line-up of batters. Our defence is going to need to be solid to hold them. Today we’re going to work on grounders and covering the outfield. Let’s get started with a warm up run, then grab your mitts.”

The first hour was spent on conditioning and fielding drills. Although Agatha almost exclusively played catcher, she liked to keep her glove and arm in shape, so she practiced with the outfielders as well. It was a warm afternoon, and she was already sweating. 

When her muscles were aching and her lungs were working hard, she didn’t have time to worry about a certain pitcher or think about any past mistakes or haunting memories. It was the perfect distraction. They took a short break before jumping into batting practice. 

Coach took the mound, “Their pitchers aren’t anything special. I expect that we’ll have a strong showing against them. Mary is their starter and has a decent curveball. I’ll throw you a mix of pitches. Don’t hold back.”

One by one they took their turn at the plate. Wanda slammed one to the outfield, and Tommy tried his best to catch it but underestimated the distance and it flew over his head. 

Agatha was up and let two go by before she saw the pitch she wanted. A low fast ball, her bread and butter. She hit it straight out over Coach, and it soared over the fence. Home run. 

She could hear Billy’s little claps as she walked back to the line and tipped her cap at him. Agatha had the current home run record this season. Jo Deluca of the Peaches set the league record in a single season at 28 in 1944. Agatha was already at 10 and hoping to challenge for the record. It helped that the ball was getting smaller every year and easier to hit, but that was neither here nor there. 

“Great work today. Take tomorrow off, and I’ll see you here late Saturday afternoon for a quick practice before the game at 6pm.” Coach released them for the night. 

Friday was spent in a blur of chores and errands. Agatha usually helped Pearl with the grocery shopping, and Peggy helped with the cleaning and laundry. They also usually used their free time to go to the bank and deposit their cheques.

As veteran players they were both earning $110 a week, which was the most money either had ever seen. Agatha only made $30 a week at her factory gig, so this was a massive windfall. The league also covered their meals on the road, and their rent was comically low. She was able to put away a majority of her earnings into her savings account. 

At this point, she wasn’t sure what she was saving for. Her house back in Salem was paid off thanks in part to Ralph, who had sent his military wages home during the war and from her regular job during the off-season. She had a car back home. She didn’t want for anything…well nothing that money could buy anyway. 

Saturday rolled around. Another day, another game. 

“Good luck, girls! Win another for us.” Gertie said from the couch as Agatha and Peggy headed off to the ballpark. 

“We believe in you…or whatever makes you feel good.” Peggy grumbled without looking up from her novel. 

They walked along the sidewalk and neighbors greeted them and wished them luck all the way there. The town was incredibly proud of its ballplayers, and the fans were very passionate and kind. The stands were almost always full to capacity at home games. 

“Do you think you’ll win today, Angel?” A young girl asked as she followed along behind them. 

“What do you think, Jackie?” Agatha turned and smiled at the pre-teen. 

“I think you will! I bet my brother a packet of M&M’s that you’d win tonight.” 

“Well get ready to enjoy some chocolate.” Peggy reassured the young fan, who squealed and ran ahead of them to the field. 

“Are you that confident?” Agatha looked at Peggy slyly. 

“Why not? I’m not about to crush that kid’s hopes, and we’ve been playing great. I think we could beat any team in this league right now.”

“I wish I could bottle your confidence.” Agatha said. It’s not that she didn’t believe in the team. She just knew how things could turn on a dime. High expectations often led to a long fall to reality. 

She could write a book about the danger of hope. 

In this case, her worries were unfounded. The Comets eked out a win 1-0 against the Blue Sox. Shirley had batted in Wanda from third base for a run, and they were now on a seven game winning streak. Agatha had also netted two more hits for her record and had thrown out a runner trying to steal second. It was a good game all around. 

To top it off, when the game ended, she had heard her name being shouted from the stands. This wasn’t unusual but this particular voice sounded very persistent. Agatha turned to look and saw Jackie waving a distinct pack of candy in the air with a huge toothy grin on her face. Agatha nudged Peggy, and they both burst out laughing.

Sometimes the joy of the game came from a well-deserved win, and sometimes it came from something as small and insignificant as a successful wager over some chocolate.

Chapter End Notes

Just wanted to note that most of the quotes that I use at the start of the chapters are from the book "The Incredible Women of the All-American Girls Professional Baseball League" by Anika Orrock.

If you have any interest in the real league, this is a really beautiful book with stunning illustrations and stories from the players.

Thanks for reading! Hope someone else is getting a kick out of this like I am.

Chapter 6: Double or nothing

“At first we took a lot of ribbing, ‘Go home where you belong!’ ‘Go take care of your kids!’ but eventually we won them over.”

Vivian Kellogg

 

“Agatha, do you want to come with us tomorrow?”

She looked over at Lupe, “Where are you going?” They had just finished up their second game against the Blue Sox. Another win in the bank. 

“Horsey and Shirley wanted to go watch the Racine game at 11.” The team often went to watch the Belles play since Racine and Kenosha were only 20 minutes apart from each other. It was a great way to scope out the other teams and pass the time. 

“Who are they playing?”

“The Sallies.” Lupe replied, and of course, Agatha was in. She didn’t know if Rio would be pitching, but she couldn’t pass up the opportunity. She roped in Peggy as well, and a few other gals jumped in to join. 

The next day they split up into two cars and motored up to Racine to catch the game. The Belles were not at their best this year. They had won the trophy in 1946, but they lost their entire pitching roster this year when the league went overhand. Two of their infielders left after getting married, and the new additions weren’t gelling with the rest of the team. 

They grabbed seats in the stands behind home plate where they could get a good view of the game. Agatha looked to the bullpen. She saw her immediately. Rio was warming up with Shaw. 

“Your arch nemesis is pitching.” Peggy pointed across the field and Agatha hastily tried to grab her hand, not wanting to draw attention to themselves. 

“She’s not my nemesis.” Agatha mumbled under her breath. 

The Sallies were in the field first, and it looked like Rio was actually starting the game. Interesting . Agatha wondered what had changed. Maybe the coach was just trying something new. 

“First up to bat is the center fielder, Virginia Booker!” The announcer’s voice rang from the speakers around the stands. 

Agatha watched Rio take a deep breath. She wondered if this was her first start, if she was nervous, if she got scared. 

She settled both hands in her glove, brought them both over her head, and kicked out her leg. Agatha watched the ball zip into the catcher’s glove. 

“Strike.” They could hear the ump’s call clearly. 

“That looked like a change-up.” Peggy said. 

“How could you tell from here?” Shirley was sitting behind them and leaned forward to ask. 

“It was much slower than her normal fastball. Booker swung way too early, so it must have looked like it was coming in hot. Either that or she’s just a poor hitter.” She laughed. 

Rio’s next pitch just missed the strike zone and was called a ball. She then fired one right down the middle. Agatha could hear the connection when the ball hit the catcher’s mitt. 

“Okay, yeah that first one definitely wasn’t that fast.” She added. Other pitchers were fast, but Rio seemed to turn the dial up. She also seemed to have more control compared to the others. 

With every batter that came up, Agatha tried to imagine herself in their place. She would watch the pitches go by and try to time up exactly when she would swing. By the fourth inning, she felt like she might have a chance, but Rio didn’t seem to have the normal weaknesses of most pitchers. She didn’t favor any of her pitches, and she didn’t have any noticeable pattern. She would sometimes throw three fastballs in a row for one batter, then strike another out with just off-speed pitches. 

She was surprisingly adaptable, but everyone has their faults. Whenever a batter managed to get a hit off her, Rio couldn’t hide her upset and didn’t immediately react to the hit. Agatha studied her like a scientist studying an animal in a cage. She was keen to learn everything there was to know about this rookie. 

“Remember to blink, babe.” Peggy whispered out of the corner of her mouth. 

Agatha hadn’t realized she was staring so hard. She shoved her friend and redirected her gaze to the field. Rio pitched for seven innings before being relieved. She only allowed four hits and no runs. A very impressive start. The Sallies were actually leading with one run. If they could just hold the Belles, they would earn a much-needed win. 

By some miracle, they were able to pull it off and win 1-0. Agatha watched the Sallies celebrate and even Rio cracked a smile, despite her usual stoic expression. 

Shirley checked her watch, “We should probably leave in a jiffy to make it back home for our own game.” 

“Good call, ready everyone?” Peggy stood up, and they made their way to the exits. Their game started in two hours, but everyone needed to get home, grab their equipment, and get to the ballpark. 

“Thanks for inviting us Lupe, that was good recon.” Agatha said. 

“No problem. If we want to win the title this year, we need to know our competition inside and out.” Lupe explained as they got into the car. 

“Amen.” Agatha agreed. She didn’t want her baseball career to end without lifting the trophy at least once. 

They won game three against South Bend and were warming up for their final game against their opponents. This was the longest winning streak the Comets had ever been on. If they won tonight, it would tie for the longest streak in league history. No pressure.

“Team, regardless of tonight’s outcome, I am so proud of everyone. You have all played your hearts out, and I couldn’t ask for much more. Now let’s give these folks a show, and prove why we deserve to win it all this year!” Coach Grant riled them up before they took to the field. 

“Let’s do this!” Wanda shouted to a harmony of cheers and whoops. 

For some reason, everything had just been clicking with the team lately. Everybody was having success at bat, they had no errors in the field, and the pitching had propelled them to many victories. In all of Agatha’s years with the team, this was the most confident she had ever felt. 

She walked out onto the field for the national anthem and stood along the third base line with her teammates as a local singer attempted to belt out the challenging tune. She often used this time to scan the crowd and see who was in attendance. 

Sometimes she would see the mayor, a huge baseball fan, or the town’s chief of police. The jeweller once gave her a pearl necklace for hitting a home run during the playoffs. They were incredibly lucky to have a dedicated fan base of locals and those from nearby towns. She saw many familiar faces tonight, and then her eyes stopped. 

Second row, behind the backstop, with Greta Gill and Carson Shaw. Why can’t she leave me alone? Rio was standing with her hand on her heart, reciting the anthem. She wasn’t looking at the flag. She was looking at one thing only, one person. 

Agatha looked away. It was too intense and strangely intimate, despite the thousands of other onlookers. Rio’s gaze was carnivorous, like she was ripping the skin off Agatha and feasting on her. 

“A couple of spies at 2 o’clock.” She whispered to Peggy beside her and nodded in the direction of the Sallies players in the stands. 

“It’s not a crime to watch a ballgame, Agatha.” She whispered back. 

She’s right. We just did the same thing. It’s not against the rules. 

The song finished, “Play ball!” 

“We’re batting first. Dottie’s leading off. Stick to the plan, and go for the base hits. No need for any long-balls, we don’t want to give away any easy fly outs.” Coach advised them before taking his place next to third base. 

Dottie and Horsey got on base, and Peggy hit into an unfortunate double-play. Shirley hit a slow grounder straight to first and was out. Agatha would be up first next inning. 

She rushed to get her catcher’s gear on and trotted out to the plate. Babe Nichol was starting today. She was another old-timer who had been on the team since the start. Agatha and her had developed an easy chemistry over the years. 

Babe was recently engaged and contemplating leaving the league. She wanted the typical suburban dream, white picket fence and all. Agatha would be sorry to see her go. She had a nasty sidearm and a knack for reading batters. 

“Nice gams, Angel!” 

She turned toward the catcall and saw Greta waving at her. She hated when men shouted out at her, but Greta was an old friend. They had first met at tryouts and maintained a friendly sort-of friendship throughout the years playing against each other. Agatha liked Greta’s unabashed confidence, and Greta respected Agatha’s wit and skill at the game. 

“Take a picture, it’ll last longer.” She shouted back to the enjoyment of the other fans. Even Rio laughed at the old joke. 

As she squatted down behind the plate, she gave the signal to the pitcher and waited. A hush generally fell over the crowd while the batter was up to bat. But, Agatha could just hear the distinct voices of Greta and Carson. 

“....used to be married, I think. I’m not sure.”

“Yeah, I heard she’s divorced…”

She caught the ball that was aimed at her glove. Are they talking to Rio about…me? She felt self-conscious suddenly. Maybe I misheard.  

“...from New York like you Greta…”

Okay, definitely not about me. 

“No way, she’s from outside Boston…”

Nevermind.

The next pitch was a ball. She was losing track of the count as all her energy was going towards her listening skills. It was so hard to focus on a singular conversation among the noise and pay attention to the game. 

She called for a fastball. Babe shook her off. She called for it again. Another shake of the head. 

“Time.” She called for a break from the umpire and stood to go see what the problem was. 

“Why are you asking for a fastball? You know this hitter eats those for breakfast.” Babe held her mitt up to shield their conversation from the batter and opposing team. 

“Huh? Oh…sorry. I was…distracted.” Agatha looked back at the batter and kicked herself for her slip up. 

“Get your head in the game. I need you, okay?” 

“I’ve got you, Babe” Agatha jogged back and re-focussed. She tuned out any back chatter and called the right pitch. 

The batter popped it straight up in the air. Agatha ripped off her mask and looked skyward, tracking the ball as it fell back to Earth. 

“I’ve got it!” She yelled. She opened her glove and caught it easily. 

“What a catch, folks! Another brilliant play by the catcher.” The announcer said. 

She waved her glove in the air to applause and saw the Sallies players standing and clapping. Rio gave her a subtle nod of the head in acknowledgement and lifted her applauding hands. I’ll take that. 

The next inning, Agatha was the first batter. She made the journey from the dugout to the box. 

“Don’t choke, Harkness!” Greta’s taunts were clear, but after so many years in this game, Agatha was a pro at filtering out teasing from the crowd. Those first few seasons were brutal with abuse from small-minded men who couldn’t comprehend the concept of women playing ball. 

Agatha gave a stately wave to Greta, who wiggled her fingers back in jest. Rio seemed to be enjoying herself next to her. She was smiling widely. 

She’s got a pretty smile. The wandering sentence floated through Agatha’s mind to her shock and surprise. She looked away before another unwelcome thought came knocking. 

Agatha felt the back of her neck warm. Must be the heat. She justified to herself and set her feet in the box. Her stomach felt as light as air. Her hands were damp as her palms felt sweaty. She readjusted her grip on the bat. It must be hotter than I thought. 

The first pitch was a meatball, big and juicy, right down the middle. Perfect. Agatha leaned back and poured her power into her swing. She waited to feel the vibration of connection, the pop when the ball hit the wood, but instead she felt nothing. 

She watched in horror as her bat slipped from her wet hands and flew through the air like a swinging trapeze artist. 

“Look out!” Someone shouted as the flying projectile went right for Fossey in the on deck circle. She ducked just in time to the relief of the gasping fans. The bat ricocheted off the fence and luckily didn’t make it into the stands. 

Agatha dropped her head and cringed. She had never flung a bat in her entire life. It happened to the best of them but not to her. She wished she could disappear. 

Billy ran over and collected the stray bat. He moved his little feet as fast as he could and gave it back to Agatha. “Here you go.” He said cheerfully, which helped lessen the pain. 

“Nice one, Butterfingers!” Greta’s jibe brought out a wave of laughter from everyone within earshot. Agatha’s anger rose. 

She focussed all her frustration into her body. It was one of the skills that made her so good. She was able to take all her negative emotions and transform them into explosions of power. Where some people’s grief and despair crippled them, Agatha’s made her harder and stronger. 

The next pitch wasn’t as sweet as the first, but it would have to do. She imagined the ball as all the painful jeers and taunts that people hurled at her, and she crushed it with her bat. 

“Look at that ball go! Big dog’s gotta eat! That’s another home run for Agatha Harkness!” The announcer’s excited yell boomed throughout the ballpark. 

She watched the ball hang in the air like it was fighting gravity and turned to see the three Sallies players staring at its trajectory. She saluted Greta as she jogged around the bases in a celebratory galavant. When she made it back around to home, Billy was there grabbing her bat and jumping up and down. She sprinted the last few yards and picked him up, swinging him around in a circle before carrying him under her arm back to the dugout. 

“That was a heck of a dinger!” Coach tipped his cap to her as she went to sit amongst a thrall of pats on the back and congrats. No matter how many homers she hit, it was always just as exciting. 

“You crushed that ball!” Tommy looked up at her in awe. 

“Yeah, crushed it!” Billy echoed his twin, and they sat down on either side of her on the bench. 

“Nice save from that appalling slip of the bat.” Peggy stood in front of her and joked. 

“Don’t remind me.” Agatha leaned her head back and pulled her hat down to cover her eyes. 

“I’ve never seen you make that mistake. Anything to do with those ‘spies’ in the crowd?”

Agatha lifted her cap a miniscule amount to look at Peggy, “My hands were sweaty.”

“Whatever you say.”

The Comets were able to win 2-0 and tie the record at nine wins in a row. Just one more win and they would set a new record for the league. 

“Impressive work out there. Let’s keep it up! We only have one day off before our next run of games against the Redwings. We’re staying in town, so I’ll see you on Thursday before the game.” Coach released them and the team got changed.

Agatha and Peggy were the last out of the locker room. They had been chatting while Agatha wrote in her journal. Shirley and Dottie had also hung around and left with the two friends. 

“Hey Butterfingers! Nice hitting tonight.” They turned and saw Greta, Carson, and Rio approaching them from the parking lot, each holding a beer bottle in their hands. 

“Thanks, doll. Taking notes?” Agatha asked. 

“I’d rather not endanger my fans.” Greta responded. 

“You sharing those around?” Peggy pointed to the unopened case that Carson was carrying. Shaw looked at Greta who nodded. They passed around the bottles. Everyone took one but Shirley. 

“What do you all want anyway?” Agatha eyed them curiously. They all knew the rule about spending time with their competitors.

“It’s not everyday we get the chance to be in the presence of greatness.” Greta started to bow to Agatha, who scoffed and took a hearty swig of her drink. 

“We just had a friendly wager we wanted to propose.” Shaw spoke. 

“Gambling is illegal, which I’m sure you are all aware of. I don’t think the league would approve of this.” Shirley, ever the rule follower, added her two cents. 

“Shush, Shirley. Let’s hear them out at least.” Agatha was interested in what they could possibly have to say. Rio was standing beside Greta, drinking in silence. 

“Ten pitches. If you can hit a homer, we’ll give you $100.” Greta laid out the terms. “Offer goes for any of you brave enough to face this rookie.” She patted Rio on the head. 

Agatha was itching to face Rio again but thought it sounded too good to be true, “And if none of us homers?” 

“You pay us the hundred.” Greta said. 

“I don’t think this is a good idea…” Shirley was debating whether to run or to watch. 

Agatha didn’t have to think about it, “I’m in. Let’s finish these drinks and wait for the ballpark to clear out, then it’ll be empty and available.”

“We’re not meant to play on the field outside of practice and games!” Shirley looked at them all incredulously. 

“You can go back home, darling. No one is forcing you to be here.” Greta said with a tone. 

Shirley looked at Agatha and Peggy, who clearly had no intention of leaving. Dottie shrugged and took another swig of beer, “I want to see how this plays out.”

They all walked around the park and sat in the field just beyond the outfield fence. Shirey was begrudgingly still trailing along. 

“Are you short on lettuce or something? Did you blow all your cash on fancy purses and dresses?” Agatha asked, still confused on why they were making this bet now. 

Greta laid down in the grass and looked up at the stars as she spoke, “I didn’t realize the Comets were the fun police. Thought you gals wouldn’t mind some friendly competition.” 

“Not all of us are so lucky to be on a record-setting winning streak, Angel. We have to find our enjoyment with this game elsewhere.” Shaw had her head resting on Greta’s belly. Agatha watched Greta playing with Carson’s hair aimlessly. There was something oddly tender about the interaction. She wanted to ask about it but felt it was improper to pry. 

“What about you? How did they rope you into this?” Peggy pointed to Rio, who had yet to speak. 

“The only way to get better is to play against the best.” She said without elaboration. 

Agatha chugged the rest of her can and felt the heady buzz from the alcohol. The last lights were turned off. They were alone in the dark, lit only by the summer moon. “Looks like everyone’s gone. Ready when you are.” She stood up and dusted off her dress. 

The rest of the gang stood, and they hopped the short chain link fence to get onto the field. 

The Comets players put their bags down along the first base line. Peggy lent her glove to Rio, and Agatha gave hers to Carson then grabbed her bat. Dottie grabbed a few spare baseballs from a bucket in the dugout. 

“Rules are…there are no rules!” Greta stood on the pitcher’s mound and spun in a circle, stumbling slightly as she lost her footing. “Just kidding. Some rules - Rio will throw out ten pitches per batter, no purposeful balls, and whoever can hit a homer gets the cash.”

“What if more than one of us hits it outta here?” Peggy asked and Rio almost keeled over in laughter. She steadied herself on a similarly cackling Greta. 

“Yeah sure. Good luck.” Greta conceded. 

Peggy, Agatha, and Dottie huddled in a circle near home plate. Shirley was sitting on the visitor’s bench, a silent observer. 

“Who wants to go first?” Peggy asked. “They look pretty corked. I don’t know how many drinks they’ve had, but I don’t think Rio’s at the top of her game. We might have a shot.”

“I’ll go last. I want her a little worn out” Agatha said. 

“I reckon I can take her.” Dottie decided to take the first crack. She wasn’t even sure how she ended up here, but the first baseman wasn’t one to turn down the prospect of fifty bucks. 

Rio was dressed in a plain red a-line skirt and white collarless pintuck blouse. She was buzzed, using a borrowed glove, and wearing Mary Janes. If ever there was time, when they could get a hit off her, this was it. 

The first pitch was a high heater. Swing and a miss. She smiled in satisfaction. 

“Come on Dottie, you got this!” Agatha cheered on her teammate. 

“I can barely see the ball!” Dottie called back over her shoulder. The moonlight was surprisingly bright and it was a clear night sky, but it was a sad substitute for the bright stadium lights. 

The next three pitches soared past her. On the fifth, she connected and popped one up into left field. Greta ran to fetch it. She missed the next two, and grounded the final three. No dice. 

“Nice try!” Peggy tried to console her and took the bat for her turn. Rio seemed to be glowing with pride. Agatha couldn’t wait for her turn. 

“I’m getting bored out here!” Greta yelled from the outfield. 

“Show them what you got, Peg.” Agatha tried to give her some pep. 

The first two pitches were okay but on the very low edge of the strike zone. Peggy let them go by. The third pitch was well-placed and Peggy swung hard. The bat connected and Greta ran back, back, and caught it in deep center field. Close but no cigar. 

Peggy kicked the dirt in frustration as Rio appeared to let out a sigh of relief. 

“You almost had her. Just a touch more power and you’ve got it.” Agatha said. 

She fouled off the next pitch, popped out the fifth and sixth. The seventh ball went soaring to left but hooked foul at the last moment despite Peggy trying to will it fair. The next two were misses, and on the final pitch, Peggy swung for the fences and struck out. 

“Blast! How does she do it?” She grunted in anger as she turned to hand off the bat to Agatha. 

“Give up yet?” Rio taunted from the mound. Agatha chewed her lip and let the annoyance fuel her. 

“Shut up and pitch, Vidal!” Agatha sniped as she took her stance. 

“Yes, ma’am.” 

She let one go by. The second went foul, and the third was too far outside. She watched the fourth pitch leave Rio’s hand and could tell where it was going immediately. She took in a deep breath, rotated her body and released on the ball all her seething rage at this rookie pitcher. 

Crack

They call it the most satisfying sound in sports. 

And for good reason. The sound of a ball perfectly colliding with a wooden bat is like no other noise in the world. It resonates throughout the space and can give goosebumps to anyone lucky enough to hear it. It’s often easy to tell if a ball is destined to be a homer from the sound alone. 

A sound like the one Agatha just unleashed to Rio’s horror. 

They all watched as the tiny white speck disappeared into the deep black sky and soared over the fence. Gone. 

Peggy whooped and screamed in delight. Even Shirley was on her feet clapping and cheering. Agatha made a show of rounding the bases, lifting her arms in the air in celebration. 

When she made it back to home, Peggy and Dottie lifted her up in the air like she was queen of the universe. 

“Double or nothing.” Rio called out from her position. 

They set Agatha down, and she looked at the pitcher. “Sorry? Didn’t lose enough money tonight?”

“You heard me.”

“Yeah, and I’m tired. I want to get home. I won your bet, now pay up.” 

Greta ran in from the outfield with the spare balls. “That was crazy, Angel. Sorry for doubting you.”

“Thanks, now tell this rookie to quit while she still can. We need to get home.” Agatha said. 

“Come on, double or nothing. Don’t tell me you’re chicken?” Rio looked desperate. She clearly was not used to losing. 

“Woah there, hon. Watch yourself.” Peggy added. 

“I’m packing up now. I suggest you do the same.” Agatha was happy with her win. She didn’t want to push it further. She turned to put her bat away. 

“I guess what they’re saying is true. You really are just a has-been.” Rio spat out and everyone’s eyes bulged. Peggy’s jaw dropped. 

Agatha froze and turned slowly. Her hands gripped the bat with extra pressure. “Excuse me?”

“Five years in the league. Your days are numbered.” 

“Rio, shut up!” Carson looked at her with panic.

Agatha took deliberate steps to the mound where the three opponents stood. 

“Gonna push me again? Is that the best you can do?” Rio’s eyes were unfocussed. She was slurring her words slightly. The alcohol must be catching up to her, even though it didn’t seem to alter her pitching performance. 

“Rio-” Greta put a hand on her shoulder to keep her steady. 

“I suggest you stop talking and go home, babe. That mouth is going to get you in trouble.” Agatha was an arms length away. She feared what might happen if she got closer. Rio looked wild. 

Carson spoke up first, “We’re going. We’ll get your cash tomorrow before we leave for the next town.” She handed Agatha’s catcher glove over. 

“No wonder her husband left her. She’s such a-” Rio’s voice was cut off by a harsh, unexpected slap. Like the crack of the bat, this noise silenced the space.  

Peggy pointed her now extended hand out, “Don’t even think of finishing that horrible sentence.” 

Rio held her cheek like it was precious. A beat passed as everyone stared at each other, unsure what to do. Then, like a rabid animal, Rio pounced and tackled Peggy to the dirt. 

“Hey! Hey! What the…Get off of her!” Agatha dropped the bat and tried to grab the pitcher. Greta also tried to break it up, but Rio was hard to get a handle on. She had the swiftness of a fox and was spitting mad. She was swiping at Peggy’s face in retaliation. It was a cacophony of limbs. 

Peggy was doing her best to roll out of it, but Rio was on top of her and pinning her down. Dottie sprinted over to try to help. 

Greta finally got a hold of Rio’s shoulders and pulled her up just as she was winding up her right fist. Agatha was leaning down to pick up Peggy, and Rio’s fist collided with her face.

“OWWW, what the fuck?!?” 

Agatha stumbled backwards, grasping her nose, which was now spouting blood. 

Shirley ran over to witness the chaos and almost fainted at the sound of the curse word. Peggy had a few scratches on her cheek from Rio’s nails, but she wordlessly whipped off her cardigan and handed it to Agatha to staunch the bleeding. 

“Rio, what have you done?” Carson was frozen like a statue, observing the chaos. 

“I’m…I’m so sorry. I didn’t…I wasn’t trying to…” Rio was holding her hand tenderly. Her knuckles were bright red and whispering small drops of blood. She reached for the seated catcher who was still holding a sweater to her nose. 

“Don’t touch me!” Agatha blurted out. She had taken her fair share of hits over the years. She had been knocked by stray throws, collisions at the plate, rough slides. But this was another level of pain. Every breath was painful, her entire face felt like it was throbbing. 

“We should get outta here.” Greta pulled Rio and motioned to Carson. “Meet me here tomorrow at noon, and I’ll give you your winnings. Come on girls.” 

Dottie took the gloves and balls from the Sallies players, and they watched them scurry away. Rio looked back at Agatha with a harsh sadness in her big eyes. Agatha scowled back in anger. 

“Can you walk?” Peggy held a hand down to Agatha who nodded up at her. “Nobody say nothing, ya hear?” She looked pointedly at Shirley, who pretended to zipper her mouth shut. 

The four women swore each other to secrecy. If the coaches or chaperones got wind of what happened here tonight, they would all be suspended or fined. 

Nobody spoke as they hopped the fence and made their way through the night. The silent shame was as suffocating as the heat. 

“Was it worth it?” Shirley asked, breaking the awkward silence. 

Agatha removed the stained cardigan from her face and despite the pain radiating behind her eyes and the drops of blood still dripping from her nose, she spoke triumphantly, “Did you see her face when I clobbered that pitch? Yeah it was worth it!”

Chapter 7: Three strikes

“Believe me, you haven’t lived until you’ve slid on skin.”

Doris “Sammye” Sams

“Bloody hell.” 

Agatha woke up to Peggy standing over her staring. 

“What?” She tried to open her eyes, but it hurt to even move her eyelids. 

“You look like you got hit by a truck.” 

Agatha pulled her pillow out and covered her face, “What time is it?”

“We’ve got five minutes before we miss breakfast.” Peggy sat at the vanity and applied her lipstick. She liberally applied powder to her face to cover the superficial scratches across her cheeks. 

Agatha debated skipping the meal but her stomach forced her up. She stood behind Peggy and looked at herself in the mirror. “Oh god, I can’t go out there like this! Gertie will have a heart attack!” 

She gently outlined the emerging purple bruises beneath both eyes and across the bridge of her nose. Her right eyelid was swollen and pink, and there was a tiny cut on her cheek. 

“Sit, it’s nothing a little…or a lot of Max Factor can’t cover up.” Peggy moved Agatha’s wavy locks behind her shoulders and motioned her to turn around. 

“Are you trying to knock me out again? Can’t you be a bit more gentle?” She complained as Peggy went to work correcting last night’s mistakes. 

“I’m barely touching you! Toughen up, darling.” 

It was a rush job and Agatha’s squirming didn’t help, but it would have to do. Agatha grabbed whatever clothes looked the cleanest in the pile at the end of her bed. Pearl cooked breakfast each morning for the house at 8am sharp. If you missed it, you’d have to settle for a sad bowl of cereal. 

“We’re coming!” Peggy skipped down the hall as the living room grandfather clock chimed the top of the hour. Agatha slowly followed behind, feeling the ache behind her eyes with every step. 

“Well, well, well, cutting it close today.” Pearl eyed them both suspiciously as she plated the last of the pancakes and slathered them with butter. 

“Take it easy on them Pearly, they won their game last night. Tied the record, right girls?” Gertie poured herself a glass of OJ.

“Yes indeed.” Agatha sat at the dining table and waited for Pearl to bring over the towering stack of pancakes. 

Gertie looked at Agatha and then did a double take, “Something you want to share with the class?”

“Hmmm?” Agatha avoided eye contact. 

Peggy saw Gertie and now Pearl closely observing her friend, “Oh, she caught a nasty foul tip off the bat during batting practice before the game. Hit her square in the nose.”

“Forget your catcher’s mask?” Pearl wasn’t buying it. She could have been an FBI agent in another life if she had been born in a different time and place. 

Agatha coughed as some food caught in her throat. She reached for the juice and chugged it. “Yeah, I was…just…I had left it in my bag.”

“Sure.” Pearl said before returning to her breakfast. “Guess it has nothing at all to do with this letter that someone slipped under the door this morning?”

She held up a small letter with Agatha and Peggy’s first names scrawled on it. They looked at each other but neither recognized it. 

Peggy reached across the table and plucked it from Pearl’s hand, “This could be anything. Could be about our upcoming series against the Redwings. Could be a last-minute practice.” She slid her finger between the paper to open the envelope. 

Agatha watched the deep brown eyes scanning the hand-written note in seconds. 

“Coach is asking us to meet him at the ballpark at 9.” Peggy flipped the note over but it was blank on the back. 

“Why?” Agatha asked. They had the day off. Coach never called them in on days off. 

“Doesn’t say. Here.” She passed over the letter. It was short and vague. Just telling them where to go and what time. 

“Someone’s in trouble…” Gertie taunted them with a smirk. 

“What? No way! They don’t call me ‘Angel’ for nothing.” Agatha said. 

“You know that nickname has nothing to do with your behavior.” Pearl’s mouth was half full of pancake, but she couldn’t miss the chance to rib her. Agatha scrunched her nose and shot her an angry look. 

“We should go get ready. We’ve got to leave soon to make it on time.” Peggy shoveled the food onto her fork and worked to clear her plate. Agatha helped clear the table and rinsed the dishes. 

“I’ll handle that, dear. Go.” Gertie waved her away.

“If you get fired, we still expect rent at the end of the month!” Pearl called out as the two retreated to their room. 

“You don’t think…” Agatha started.

“Now don’t you start too. We don’t even know what this is about! We could be getting a raise for all we know. Lord knows we deserve it.” Peggy pinned her hair back while Agatha rifled in her closet for a nicer dress to wear. 

She settled on a pink and white striped day dress that popped with her red lipstick. “Ready?” 

Peggy nodded, and they headed for the front door. 

“Hope the coach isn’t too hard on you two.” Pearl called out from the dining table. 

“Thanks for all your support!” Agatha snarked as they hit the street and started the walk to the park. 

“If Shirley ratted on us, I swear to God…they’re going to have to send me to the big house after I’m done with her. They’ll be searching the bottom of Lake Michigan.” Agatha’s mind was racing with fear, and she resorted to her lesser impulses.

“Keep your hair on, we’ll be fine. Let’s just see what he has to say before we blow our lids.” 

They couldn’t see anyone outside when they arrived despite being right on time. 

“Where is he?” Agatha looked around but they couldn’t see anyone walking up either. 

“Hear that?” They could hear the sound of someone hitting a baseball, so they entered through the side gate and saw the coach tossing balls up in front of him and trying to hit them. 

He saw them coming and paused his practice but didn’t move. He stayed next to home plate, which made for an awkward walk over. It was odd seeing him in casual clothing. He was wearing a pair of old jeans and a ruffled collared white shirt. 

“I’ve never won a championship. Did you know that? In all my years playing, from childhood to the majors and now to coaching. I’ve made it to the playoffs and even the championship game, but I’ve just never been able to clinch it.” He crossed his arms and looked wistfully to the distance.

“Coach-” Peggy began to speak, but he continued and cut her off. 

“You girls are playing great baseball. The entire team. Everyone is doing their best. We’re on this incredible winning streak. If we keep going like this, we might really have a shot. Nothing would make me happier than to win it all with the Comets.”

“Yeah, same goes for-” She tried again, but he spoke over her. 

“That’s what makes what I’m about to say so hard.” He finally looked at them both directly. His eyes lingered at the traces of last night’s mistake. “You are both suspended for the next series. Along with Shirley and Dottie. Rest assured, the Sallies players are also being dealt with by their manager.”

“Coach! You can’t be serious!” Agatha’s voice rose out of shock. 

“Who told?” Peggy blurted out. 

“I think you all forget how small this town is. Did you really think nobody would hear a group of drunks loudly playing baseball in the middle of the night? The chief of police lives the next street over!” He pointed his arm out towards the road.

“But Coach, we’re on a winning streak. One more game and-” Agatha tried to reason. 

“And the team is going to have to attempt to win without four of its best players.” He dropped his head and kicked the dirt with his sneaker. The frustration was evident in his body language. 

“Please, we can’t miss four games.” Peggy begged. Agatha watched her hands, which were clenched so tightly they were stark white. 

“You can and will do whatever I tell you. Actions have consequences. Time to face yours.” He sighed.

“We’re sorry.” Agatha offered. 

“Yeah, that’s not the first time I’ve heard that from you lately, Harkness. If you want to win this thing, you better get your head on straight because this isn’t like you. Same goes for you, Carter. I would expect this from rookies, but not from you two.” 

“Shirley and Dottie don’t deserve the suspension, they were just following us. Please Coach, have a little mercy.” Peggy said. 

“They’ve read the rulebook the same as you. Next time they’ll know better.” He clearly wasn’t open to negotiation. They were just going to have to accept this - there was no alternative.

“Are we free to go?” Agatha was done with this conversation. She wanted a cigarette, she wanted to go home, she wanted to run. 

Coach nodded curtly but finished, “You may be suspended from playing, but I expect you at every practice and game. We are still a team.” 

They walked out together in stunned silence. Neither spoke until they were out of sight of the stadium. 

“That knobhead! We’re going to lose our winning streak and for what? Just because we had the audacity to spend time with other players? It’s a load of horseshit, and he knows it! Someone must have told one of the big fellas in management, so now he has to make a show out of us.” Peggy was fuming. 

“It’s times like these that remind me why I want to live in a big city one day. The neighborhood gossip is so petty. I hope Shirley and Dottie are okay.”

“They should be meeting us at noon. We’re going to see Greta to collect your winnings, remember? We’ll get to hear about what happened with their manager.”

“Oh yeah, although at this point I would exchange the money for a spot on the team again. I can’t believe we’re going to miss an entire series.” Agatha hated long breaks. There was nothing worse than being stuck with her thoughts.

They walked through the front door and feared the interrogation that they were probably going to receive. 

“What’s the verdict?” Gertie turned around from her lounger in the living room. 

“Four game suspension.” Agatha found long ago that the truth was usually the path of least resistance with their hosts. 

“Given the state of your faces, I would say that’s generous.” Pearl said, ignoring the dirty looks from the two ballplayers. 

“Sorry girls, that’s too bad. Hopefully, the team can still pull out at least one more win without you.” Gertie tried to find the silver lining. 

At 12, they said they were going out to get lunch at the local diner. Instead, they took a detour to the ballpark again and saw Greta and Carson already waiting. Instead of approaching directly, Peggy waved to get their attention and pointed to the street next to the park. They saw Shirley and Dottie approaching from the other direction and had them follow. 

They all walked down the road, and Peggy turned down a small side street. 

“What’s with the gumshoe act? Why all the secrecy?” Greta laughed as they gathered next to the florist shop.

“Did y’all talk with Coach yet?” Dottie turned to Peggy and Agatha. 

“Yeah, did he talk with you two?” Peggy asked, and they nodded sadly.

“We tried to get him to go easy on you both, but he wasn’t having it. The stubborn prick.” Peggy shook her head. 

“I knew this was going to happen! I should have never gone along. Now look at what's happened. I’m a delinquent. My perfect record is ruined!” Shirley threw her hands in the air. 

“What are you guys talking about?” Greta exclaimed, stopping all other discussion. 

“Your coach hasn’t talked to you today?” Agatha asked. 

“No…what’s up?” Carson looked confused. 

“We’ve all been suspended for the next series against Peoria. Someone in town saw us last night and reported us. We don’t know who, but it must have been someone important because we’ve never even heard of a suspension this long before.” Peggy explained the situation. 

Greta was pacing, “Oh no, are you serious? We haven’t heard anything yet, but our coach is out of town visiting some relatives or something. We haven’t seen him. Are you saying we’re going to be banned too?” 

Carson’s frown deepened, “Our team is barely holding on. I heard the coach and one of the owners arguing a few weeks ago. If we don’t start selling more tickets and drawing bigger crowds, we might fold. The league may have overexpanded this year, and now they aren’t sure if they can support the new teams.” 

“What!? Why didn’t you tell me?” Greta pulled out her pack of smokes and aggressively pulled one out and lit it. 

“I didn’t want anyone to worry. I wasn’t supposed to hear the conversation…” Carson nervously said. 

“It’s certainly happened before. Remember the Chicks and the Millerettes? They only lasted one year too.” Peggy added. 

Greta puffed out a large cloud of smoke, “Not helping!” 

“You never know, you might just get fined. Or the snitch might not have even recognized you all. You might get lucky.” Dottie tried to offer some relief, but they were both spiraling. 

“Anyway, here’s your dough.” Greta reached into the pocket of her dress and passed over five twenty dollar bills. 

“Thanks. Do you have a pen? I can write down our phone number. Can you let us know what happens?” Agatha asked. Carson opened her purse and pulled out a small notebook and a pen. Agatha scrawled the number down and passed it back. 

“Sure, we should get back. We’re heading to Chicago for our next games. We’ll be back in a few weeks, I think.” Greta threw an arm over Carson’s shoulder and led her out to the main road. 

“Wait!” Agatha called out and they paused their retreat, “Is Rio okay? I mean, is her hand alright?”

Greta had a strange knowing smile on her face when she turned around, “Just bruised, doesn’t seem to be serious. I’ll let her know you were worried.” 

“No…I was just…curious.” Agatha tried to backtrack. 

“Okay, well, we’ll call you if we get a talking to. We gotta run!” Greta and Carson headed to their hotel. 

“Anyone else want to drown their sorrows in a large chocolate milkshake?” Peggy raised her hand and the others shrugged and also put their hands up. They headed to the diner to grab some lunch and tried to lessen their anxiety and worries with sweet, delicious greasy food. 

On Thursday, Peggy and Agatha made the usual walk to the stadium. The hours before a game were usually the most exciting as the possibilities still stretched endless in front of them. The tension was always palpable in the locker room. While some players were a ball of nerves before they hit the field, Agatha found it oddly relaxing. She loved the challenge, the adrenaline. 

On this day though, this familiar walk felt empty. She wasn’t walking towards the big game. She was walking towards the bench.

They entered the side entrance and went to the locker room. As soon as they walked in, Lilia was tutting at them and shaking her head in disappointment. Nobody else seemed aware of the situation yet. The rest of the team took their spots and started changing. The suspended players also donned their uniforms even though they were not going to play. Lilia grabbed the coach when everyone was ready. 

He came stomping through the door, “Helen, Edna, Betty, and Sugar.” He pointed at each of the subs. “You’re all in today.” 

The back-up players all glanced around, clearly checking if this was a dream or not. 

“Some of your teammates decided to flout the league rules, and now they will be riding the bench for this entire series.”

Wanda shot daggers across the room at Peggy and Agatha, who tried to avoid eye contact. 

“If we win this game, we will have set a new league record for the longest winning streak. We all practice together, I’ve seen all your talents. You wouldn’t be here if you weren’t the best. So, things may look a little different for a while, but my expectations are the same. Let’s go out there and win!”

Lilia posted a copy of the batting order and positions on the cork board on the wall. Everyone rushed to check who the troublemakers were. 

Everyone except Wanda, who immediately beelined for Agatha and Peggy. She stood in front of them tapping her foot, not saying a word. 

“It was her!” They both shouted at the same time and pointed at each other. 

Wanda remained silent and turned to face Agatha directly. She knew that the older woman was more susceptible to her wills.

“What? What do you want us to say? That the Sallies showed up after we were walking out. That they challenged us to a home run contest. That we were drinking. That I slammed one off of Rio and won a hundred bucks. It was just harmless fun.” Agatha said. 

“Yeah your face really says harmless fun.” Wanda waved her hands in front of Agatha’s face. The make-up could only cover so much. 

“That was an unfortunate…accident.” She tried to defend herself. 

Wanda just shook her head, looking defeated, “How are we going to win now?”

Agatha stood and put her hands on Wanda’s shoulders. She looked into her eyes and said,  “Have a little faith in the kids. They could use the playing time, and we’ll be here cheering you on.” 

It was a bizarre experience to head out to the dugout with no expectation of playing. Agatha had been a starter since she was first drafted. She helped Helen suit up as catcher. They were both in their 30’s, but Helen had only joined the league this year. She was a teacher by trade and often had to join the season late due to the school year, so she served as a backup infielder and catcher. 

“I’m nervous.” She confessed as Agatha handed over the mask. 

“Lupe is pitching. She’ll look after you. You know all the signs, you’re ready.” Agatha tried to reassure her. She had some doubts but at this moment, those were not important. 

Helen took a big breath in and out before hustling out to her position. Agatha wished she was heading out too, but Billy rushed over and nuzzled up next to her, happy for the new company in the dugout. 

“Why are you in here, Aggie?” He leaned against her arm and looked up at her. 

“I broke a rule, so now I can’t play.”

“Like a timeout?”

“Yeah, pretty much, kid. An adult timeout. At least I get to hang out with you and Tommy.” It was a small consolation but at least the twins would provide a distraction. 

“What did you do?” Tommy skipped over and stood in front of them. 

“Wouldn’t you like to know?”

They both started nodding like bobbleheads. She crooked a finger and motioned them closer. She looked around suspiciously. The other benchwarmers were on the other side of the dugout, but she still lowered her voice to a tiny whisper. 

“One of the opposing ball boys wandered a little too close to home plate. He was bumping his gums, talking smack about our team. You know how I feel about trash talkers. I thought the kid needed to learn a lesson. So as he passed me by, I checked left. I checked right. Nobody was looking, so  I grabbed him and took a big bite out of his arm!” The twins gasped out loud and covered their mouths.

Agatha had to stifle her own laughter and maintain a serious face, “He ran as fast as his legs could carry him. I thought I had gotten away with it, but the umpire took one look at me and saw the blood running down my chin and suspended me! It’s strictly forbidden to attack other teams, you know?”

“MOM!!!” Billy jumped up and ran out into the field while the anthem singer was about to start. Wanda turned immediately and caught her son in her arms as he collided with her legs. Tommy was too busy asking for all the gory details to be scared of Agatha’s tall tale. 

Wanda held his hand and shuffled quickly back to the dugout. She glared at Agatha and pushed Billy to sit, “No more scary stories! I leave you with them for one minute…” 

“You blew my cover, mac. I was just joking!” Agatha jested, trying to win Billy back over, but he was getting comfortable next to Shirley and Peggy instead. 

The announcer’s voice came over the loudspeakers, “First batter for the day, the substitute at first base is Betty Mahoney. It’s her first start for the Comets, let’s give her a hand!” 

“You got this Betty!” Peggy cupped her hands beside her mouth and shouted. 

Agatha saw Betty’s knees shaking slightly. She wasn’t religious, but at that moment, she prayed to any higher power to help Betty. A strikeout was entirely demoralizing, and she didn’t want the sub to feel that pain right now. Not on her first start. 

They watched with bated breath as the count climbed to 2-2. On the fifth pitch, Betty swung and grounded one up the middle for a base hit. The entire team cheered from the dugout as she turned and smiled at them from first. 

The Comets went ahead in the second inning by one run, but the Redwings fought back and tied it up the very next inning. 

“Stay back on the ball, Red. She’s throwing pretty slow, so a lot of the gals have been ahead of the pitches.” Peggy advised Wanda who was up first. 

“You can do it, Mom!” Tommy exclaimed as she walked out to the batter’s box. 

“Let’s go, Wanda.” Agatha clapped loudly, trying to hide her desperation. They needed this win. She wanted the team to set the record, to be remembered. 

The pitcher missed with two balls, then hurled a high fastball. Wanda squared it up perfectly and belted it to the outfield. The right fielder ran, tracking it over her shoulder, but misjudged it and the ball bounced off the back fence. 

Wanda sprinted to second and let her momentum carry her around towards third. 

“She’s going for it!” Dottie stood up from the bench and watched Wanda attempt a triple. It was going to be close. 

“Gee wizz, she’s brave!” Peggy gasped. 

“Brave is a nicer word for it.” Agatha joked but hoped that she was wrong. Hoped that Wanda wasn’t making a foolish choice. 

The right fielder picked it up and fired it to the cut-off, who turned and threw it as hard as she could to third base. 

“Get down!” Coach yelled motioning down for her to slide. Wanda couldn’t see the ball, which was flying through the air behind her. She followed the sign and hit the dirt hard and fast. Her spikes hit the base just as the ball flew into the mitt. 

“Safe!” The field umpire declared to an explosion of applause from the home crowd. Billy and Tommy were jumping and dancing in the dugout, completely overcome with excitement. 

“That doll is crazy. She’s the real McCoy.” Peggy looked in awe. 

Wanda scored on the next hit, and the Comets were able to hold the lead for the next four innings. At the top of the ninth, two outs, the final Redwings batter popped one up into the infield and Horsey caught it for the final out. 

“They’ve done it! Just like that, your Kenosha Comets have set a new league record for the longest winning streak!” The announcer wrote the moment into history as the entire stadium gave the team a standing ovation. 

Agatha and the benchwarmers joined the team out on the field, tipping their caps to the crowd and celebrating in a large bear hug. She held in her disappointment at missing the action and tried to just enjoy the moment with her teammates. 

Back in the locker room, Coach Grant congratulated them all, “I hope you are all proud of this incredible accomplishment. I know I am. If we keep this up, we could go all the way!” 

As everyone dressed and packed their things away, the room slowly cleared out. Wanda got up to go, and Agatha called out, pointing to her leg that was scraped raw from the top of her thigh down to her knee, “Woof, that’s a killer strawberry you got there.”

Wanda stopped and turned to Lupe, “Can you give Agatha and I a moment?” The pitcher nodded and hurriedly left.

“What’s cookin’, good lookin’? Agatha teased. She enjoyed a touch of harmless flirting with Wanda. 

“Not now.” She pinched the bridge of her nose like she was fighting off a headache. “I’m not proud of this,” She gestured to the road rash on her thigh from the slide. 

“You should be! Your crazy baserunning won us this game. You are the reason we have the record.” 

“I shouldn’t have had to be the reason! If you, Peggy, Dottie, and Shirley were in the game we would have won by a landslide. I shouldn’t have to pick up your slack, Agatha!” Wanda’s voice was rising. Agatha very rarely saw the redhead get upset like this. She was normally so composed, so put-together. 

“It’s not like we planned to get suspended. I would have killed to be out on the field with you tonight.” Her voice was low, trying to hide her shame. 

Wanda was not going to let her off easy, “But you weren’t. You left the rest of us to cover for you. You couldn’t ignore your ego for one second to think about the rest of us. You just had to play your little games with that Sallies’ pitcher.” 

Agatha dropped to sit on the bench. Wanda was standing in exasperated anger, her hands on her hips.. 

“It wasn’t like that. It was…” Agatha thought about it. She knew the rules, she knew she shouldn’t have been hanging out with another team, she knew it was a bad idea. She wanted to stick it to Rio, nothing more nothing less. She hadn’t thought about the team or anybody besides herself. Wanda was right. 

“Not all of us have your talent. Some of us are just happy to be here, while we can. I’ve wanted to tell you for a while but didn’t know how to say it…but…this is my last season.” Wanda moved to sit beside Agatha. Her anger settling down into exhaustion. 

“What? Why? You’re a great ballplayer, Red.” Agatha had expected a lecture, but she hadn’t expected this. 

“It’s not that. It’s the boys. They just turned five. I want them to start school in September. I want them to have a stable home. I don’t want them stuck on a bus for hours every other week, living out of a suitcase, going to practices and games, and travelling constantly. I want to give them a normal life, or as normal as I can make for them.” Wanda swiped at the tears that fell down her cheeks.

Ever since she had lost her husband, she had put on such a strong front for her sons and for everyone. She seemed invincible, but now Agatha could see just how vulnerable and scared Wanda really was beneath it all. 

Agatha rubbed her friend’s back and fished a handkerchief out of her bag and passed it over. “Where are you going to go?”

“I’ve heard New Jersey is pretty nice.”

“Who told you that?” Agatha quipped. 

“Oh, don’t you start. I’ve already made up my mind. I’ll finish out the season, but that’s it. I’m done. I want to go out swinging. I want to win.”

“You got it. I didn’t know…I’ll be better. Let’s win this together.” Agatha brought her in for a hug, and they shared a tearful embrace in the quiet of the locker room. 

Chapter 8: Changeup

“Some people use to say, ‘How did you do it? Those long bus rides and all?’ You know, when you’re sixteen years old and you’re doing something you like to do, that was the least of my concerns, the bus ride. It was fun, it was a lot of camaraderie with the girls, we had good times, we had a lot of laughs. . . . I never minded the bus rides.”

Shirley “Hustle” Burkovich

The winning streak ended the very next game thanks to a close contest that broke the way of the Redwings, but the record still stood. The Comets were even featured in one of the national newspapers for their accomplishment. 

“My daddy is going to be beaming when he sees this!” Dottie held up the clipping at batting practice that afternoon. 

“I’m going to show everyone back home. This is incredible!” Helen reread the article again. “They even mention me by name!”

Agatha went to the newspaper stand to get her own copy to put in her journal. It was a great memento for the season so far. 

The team won the third game and lost the fourth. The suspension was finally over for the rule-breakers. 

“We’re back!” Peggy cheered at the conclusion of the fourth game. 

“You’re not back until the coach says you’re back.” Lilia quieted the joy, and Agatha stuck her tongue out at the chaperone behind her back. 

“We’ve been rotting on the bench. Let us have a little fun.” Agatha whined. 

Lilia had no time for arguing, “Ladies, we’re off to Fort Wayne tomorrow morning. Bus will be here at the ballpark at 8am. You know the drill: if you miss it, you miss the game. The sun enters Leo tonight, so I foresee a strong showing against the Daisies.”

“If you say so, Lilia.” Peggy said. She was a skeptic and didn’t buy into any of Lilia’s predictions, despite their eerie knack for being right.

That night, Pearl cooked her signature pot roast for dinner. They invited Wanda and the twins over since there were always leftovers. Pearl had grown up with seven siblings and could only cook her meals in large portions. Agatha saw this as the perfect opportunity to mend fences with her teammate. 

“How do you get this meat so tender? It’s delectable.” Wanda almost moaned when taking a bite of the roast beef. 

“It’s all in the cook time. Low and slow for a few hours gets it just right.” Pearl explained. 

Agatha leaned over to the boys who were sitting at the corner of the table and spoke in hushed tones, “She actually harvests road kill to make sure the meat is extra tenderized.”

Billy immediately spit his food out on the plate. 

“Billy! We don’t spit food out at the table!” Wanda gasped in surprise. 

“Auntie Aggie said this is road kill!” He cried and pushed his food away. 

Between the death stares coming from Pearl and Wanda, Agatha put her hands up in surrender, “Just making a joke! Sorry boys, I was just kidding. Don’t listen to me!”

Tommy giggled and made a show of chomping down on his meat, but Billy was less convinced. He decided to stick to the potatoes and carrots that he doused in gravy. 

“Did you gals hear the news on the wire this afternoon?” Gertie asked at the table. 

“Was it about that new Babe Ruth flick coming out soon?” Peggy wondered. They had all been looking forward to it. 

Gertie continued, “No, about the league. They’re disbanding the Sallies and the Colleens.”

Agatha dropped her fork and knife, which clattered on her plate. “WHAT?!”

“Yeah, apparently their teams have gone belly up. They ran out of cash, and the cities couldn’t support them anymore. The league is considering sending them on the road as travelling teams across the country, but it’s still up in the air.”

“But what about the players?” Agatha asked. She couldn’t believe this. I’ve been waiting for my chance to have a rematch with Rio.

“Well, if they become travelling orphans, they’ll probably keep all the players. Depends what the managers decide, I guess.” Peggy had no clue, but it was a decent estimation. They would likely have to wait to hear just like everyone else. 

“I can’t believe it.” Agatha was flabbergasted. I wanted one more opportunity. Just one more chance. 

“At least you won’t have to face that pesky pitcher again!” Pearl added. 

“Yeah, yeah sure.” She replied blankly. She wouldn’t get to face Rio again. She wondered if she would even get to see her again. 

“Well at least that knocks out two teams from contention for the playoffs. Ups our chances even further!” Peggy said. 

“That’s true. Bully for us, I suppose.” Wanda chimed in. Agatha still just pushed her carrots around in a daze. 

“Do you fancy your chances to win the pennant?” Gertie asked. 

“What’s a pen…ant?” Billy looked curiously at Agatha. 

“It’s pennant. One word. And it means the championship flag. If we beat every team, and win the most games. We will win the pennant, which means we are the champions!” She explained. 

“I think our chances are excellent. You heard the last series, even our subs are world-class. At this rate, I wouldn’t bet against us.” Peggy puffed out her chest and raised a fist in the air. 

Billy copied the motion to Tommy’s amusement. 

“Mickey, the bartender, is starting a betting pool for the play-offs. I’ll put some dough on you girls. I know you won’t let me down.” Gertie was one of the more loyal supporters. 

“Will you finally come to a game if we make it to the championship?” Agatha asked. 

Pearl looked over at her friend, very interested in the answer. Gertie hesitated for a moment, “I’ll definitely consider it greatly.”

The table laughed at her non-committal answer, but it was the most positive response she had ever given. Pearl just smiled softly to herself and headed to the kitchen. 

“Who’s ready for dessert?” She asked while walking in with her famous strawberry chiffon pie. 

“Me, me, me!” Tommy was bouncing in his seat. The other adults echoed his calls as well. Everybody was a fan of the sweet Jell-O treat. 

After indulging in the homemade dessert, Wanda and the boys said their goodbyes and headed back to their place. Peggy and Agatha helped clean up then went off to bed. 

“How about that news from Gertie about the Sallies and Colleens. Such a shame.” Peggy turned over in bed to face Agatha in her’s. 

“I feel bad for the girls. They probably wanted to win just as much as us. Guess that’s why we never heard from Greta about their punishment. Why bother suspending them when they aren’t even in the league anymore?” 

“Oh yeah, forgot about that. I doubt it's the last we’ll see of them though. There’s no way another team isn’t going to snap up all that talent.” Peggy said. 

“Yeah, with Wanda leaving, we’re going to need someone really good next year. I’m gonna miss that girl.” 

“Me too. I can’t believe she’s going to give this up.” Peggy rolled onto her back and looked at the popcorn ceiling in the room. 

“She wants the boys to have some stability. I get it. I would have given up everything for…well, you know.” Agatha took in a steadying breath as the memory almost came knocking at her door. 

Peggy didn’t push. She had known Agatha long enough. “Do you think anyone else is going to leave?”

“I hope we can keep everyone together. Coach doesn’t seem sour on anyone. We all get along well. We could maybe use another sub or two in case of sickness or injury, but otherwise, I think we have a solid line-up.”

She and Agatha had been very lucky to have played on the same team for their entire careers. Most players were traded to other teams that might need their position, or they could be loaned out due to injury or absence, or coaches might just not like certain players and want them gone. 

The life of a ballplayer was generally out of their hands.

On the four hour bus ride to Fort Wayne, Agatha swapped between reading the Disney comics with Billy, playing poker, and chatting with Wanda. They were all masters of distraction after the number of bus journeys they had under their belt. 

“What kind of job are you going to get in Jersey?” She was still disappointed that Wanda was leaving at the end of the season. She had grown to love her company and the presence of the twins. She was going to miss them. 

“I don’t know. I love the radio, maybe I could get a job at the station. I’m sure there must be a lot of those around the city.” Wanda loved to tune into the various weekly programs. 

“That would be real sweet. If you ever want to interview a real ballplayer, I know one you could talk to.” Agatha joked. 

“You’re right. I bet Peggy would love to be on the radio.” Wanda said slyly. 

“Ugh, back to poker I go then.” Agatha popped up and scooted down the aisle to the never-ending poker game in the middle. 

She played and lost several hands before getting bored again. She took a short nap for the rest of the trip and by the time she woke up, the bus was pulling up to the hotel. 

“Freshen up in your rooms, and grab some lunch, We are heading over to the ballpark for the game at 3pm. Lupe, you better not step off this bus in those!” She looked up and down at Lupe, who was still in a pair of jeans. 

“Sorry, Lilia. One second, has anyone seen my skirt?” The pitcher looked under her seat. It was against the rules for any of the players to be seen in public in pants. 

“Here!” Dottie held up the old cotton skirt in her hands and passed it over. Lupe just rolled up her pant legs and pulled the skirt over it. Several of the other girls did the same every trip. It made them look like they had gained fifty pounds overnight because of the way the skirt sat over the bulky jeans. A small sacrifice that some were willing to make. 

Coach was waiting for them in the lobby with their room keys. Agatha grabbed hers, and she asked, “Did you hear about the two teams that shuttered?”

“Yes, probably before you did. What about it?” He didn’t seem to be in the mood for idle chit-chat. 

“What’s going to happen to the players?”

“That’s above my pay-grade, Harkness. I haven’t heard anything yet. Not that it’s any of your business anyway.”
