“It’s just…there are so many skilled players on both teams. We could really bolster our roster with a trade or something.” With Wanda leaving, Agatha knew they were going to need a new infielder, and the Sallies had both Jess and Greta. 

“I have heard that Shaw is quite the catcher.” He cracked a smile. 

“Hey now, that position is not up for grabs.” She took the hint and walked away to the elevators. 

The game that afternoon felt as easy and natural as ever. They were back to the full line-up with Agatha, Peggy, Dottie, and Shirley back in rotation. There were luckily no hard feelings from the substitutes, who were just grateful for the chance to play the previous games. 

Agatha dropped down behind home plate and felt right at home again. There was no place like it. She had the full view of all the action. It was the best seat in the house. 

Sugar was pitching and kept the game moving at a fast pace. She didn’t allow for much wasted time between pitches and between batters. At one point the umpire had to call time as he wasn’t in position yet, and she had started her wind-up. 

They crushed the Daisies 7-0. It was a thorough stomping, and the team was in high spirits. It felt like nothing could stop them. 

That is until the next day before the Monday game when one of their starting pitchers took the floor. Babe Nichol stood up and announced that she was three months pregnant and that this series would be her last. She had played on the team for three years but got married to her sweetheart during the off-season. They had all wondered how long she would last. 

“Dean got a job offer back home in Knoxville, so we’re going to leave at the start of August. I just wanted to thank you all for the best time these past few seasons. I’ve enjoyed every second, and if any of you are ever down in Tennessee, please stop by.” She gave a short bow and returned to her locker. Everyone came over to congratulate and speak with her. 

Betty looked particularly pleased. As the back-up pitcher, she had eagerly awaited her shot on the mound. This would be her moment to finally shine. 

“We’re going to miss you, Babe. You were a heck of a pitcher. I’m sure you’ll be just as talented at motherhood. That kid is going to be so lucky to have you as a mum.” Peggy said softly. 

“The first few months are the toughest. Make sure you are sleeping when you can. You can always call me if you need advice.” Wanda offered. 

“Thanks, I’ll hold you to that. I’ve learned so much just watching you with the twins.” She said. 

“If this kid ever tries to mouth off, just nail ‘em with a pillow in the kisser. I bet you could hit a kid from across the room with your control.” Agatha slapped her leg and laughed at the image in her head. Wanda shoved her, and she almost fell off the bench. 

“We don’t throw things at children, remember Agatha? Don’t listen to her!” Wanda urged Babe who was giggling. 

“I’ll miss the game, but I’m going to miss this more. All of you girls. I don’t know where I’ll find this again.” She looked at all of the players around her. Agatha caught the small twinkle in her eye. 

Peggy comforted her, “I’m sure we’ll see each other in the future. You know where to find us! We’ll always save you a seat.” 

Babe pitched her last game that night on July 24th, 1948. She allowed one run, but the team put up another dominant win at 5-1. At the end of the game, they hoisted her (gently) onto their shoulders and paraded the winning pitcher around the field. Even the Fort Wayne fans, who filled the stadium, cheered and applauded the opposing team. 

The next game was a loss after the Daisies went on a lucky rally in the ninth inning, but the Comets won game four and moved up to the top spot in their division. If they held it for the next month, they were guaranteed the best spot in the play-offs. 

Winners of the season went straight to the semifinals and could skip the initial games. 

On Friday, they were back in Kenosha and practicing for their next run of games against Racine at home. The schedule had been shifted following the loss of two teams. 

Peggy and Agatha walked up as usual and greeted their teammates warmly. They all started tossing the ball around while waiting for Coach to arrive. He was never normally late, but they knew the normal routine. 

Agatha was playing catch with Wanda when she noticed him walking up, but he wasn’t alone. 

Is that…?

“Oooof.” The ball hit her in the shoulder as she completely missed it with her glove. She couldn’t even register the pain. She was transfixed on the person approaching them. 

“Agatha! Are you alright?” Wanda jogged over. Agatha never missed, she was a catcher after all. 

She rubbed her eyes.

I t can’t be. 

No. Please, no.  

Coach Grant walked up and stopped, “Everyone, welcome our new pitcher, replacing Babe. Say hello to Rio Vidal.”

Chapter 9: The truce

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“I never played on a girls team, ever. I never played softball. I played only with the boys and men—grown men—so I never played with the girls. I figured, how hard could that be? Until I saw that first day of tryouts, and then I realized how hard it could be. These girls could play ball.”

Wilma “Briggsie” Briggs

“This is a joke, right?” Agatha barrelled into the coach’s office after practice finished. Rio had simply observed and helped shag fly balls from batting practice. She was still waiting for her new uniform and was resting her arm. 

“Shut the door.” Coach Grant didn’t even look up from his notebook. He knew this was coming. 

“She’s too green. Sure, she can throw the heat, but she’s dangerous. You saw her try to hit me! We don’t need a player like that on the Comets.”

“It’s really thanks to your suggestion that she’s here at all.” He smiled, “You made a good point about poaching those orphan players while we had the chance.”

“I meant to replace Wanda!” Agatha threw her body down into the folding chair. I really did make some mistakes in a past life. I caused this?

“Little Red is not leaving until the season ends. Babe is leaving this week. We need another pitcher to get us through the play-offs. And who better?”

“Coach, how am I going to play with her? You know that we don’t get along!”

“You’re going to have to learn to get along real quick.”

Agatha released a cry of frustration and rage before leaving, afraid she was about to say something she might regret. 

As she poured out into the hall, she was face to face with the object of her fury. 

Rio stood her ground and stared straight into Agatha’s piercing blue eyes, “Here I am. I thought I heard someone talking about me.”

“Oh, eat my ass.” Agatha spat back without thinking. She almost slapped her own hand across her mouth. She had no idea where that came from. She rarely swore, thanks mostly to her staunchly religious mother who loved every opportunity to wash her daughter’s mouth out with soap. Just seeing Ivory Soap on the shelf still made Agatha gag to this day. 

Rio could only laugh at the crass comment, though her steely demeanor faltered for a second, “Am I really that bad?” 

“Just stay out of my way, and we won’t have any problems. My team has worked too hard for too long to let you ruin it.”

“I didn’t ask to be here, and I’m not going to try to mess anything up. I play to win, Agatha.” 

Hearing her name out of Rio’s mouth was strange. It sounded like a lyric, like a melodic sting. It reverberated under her skin.

Agatha was too full of anger to listen to reason, “I’m serious. We have a real shot. If you sabotage our chances at the championship, I’ll…I’ll kill you.” She was only being slightly dramatic. 

“Whoa, whoa. You can’t kill me. Though I wouldn’t mind seeing you try.” Rio smirked. She seemed to enjoy Agatha’s theatrics and penchant for exaggeration. It was apparent that she could stand toe-to-toe with Agatha both on the field and in conversation. 

Agatha grizzled and kept walking to leave, “See you on the field, superstar.” She didn’t look back or wait for a reply. She hustled to the locker room to grab her bag. 

Peggy was waiting as she expected. She could instantly see the irritation on Agatha as she stomped into the room, “So it’s true then?”

“I don’t know how I’m going to do it, Peg.” 

“She’s not some demon from the underworld. She’s just another player. We should try to treat her like anyone else. I’m sure she’s really not that bad.” 

“She drives me wild! I can’t explain it. It’s like she was put here specifically to annoy me.” Agatha shoved her mitt into her bag and zipped it up. They hit the light switch and walked out together. 

Peggy said, “I don’t think you’re that important, babe. You said it yourself though, she’s the best pitcher we’ve ever seen. If she’s on our team, we won’t have to go against her. We can sic her on our opponents, it’s perfect.” 

“I guess you have a point.”

“You guess? I definitely have a point. You’re just too stubborn to hear it.” Peggy laughed. 

“Shut up.” Agatha smiled. She would consider giving Rio a chance, but another part of her just couldn’t trust it. She was waiting for the other shoe to drop. 

It was the last Saturday in July, and they had a double header against the Belles. It was a nice mild day, clear skies, with a slight breeze that cooled things down. Lois was starting the first game, and Lupe the second. Rio would come in as a reliever if necessary. 

In the locker room, Agatha watched the rookie suiting up in the pastel green uniform for the first time. It was like watching a cat walk on two legs, it seemed so wrong. 

“Enjoying the view?” Peggy whispered, catching Agatha staring. 

“No, I was just…it’s just weird, isn’t it?” She fumbled over her words. 

“She’s a Comet now. Don’t make her feel like a freak or something.”

The coach came in after they were all standing and ready to take the field, “Let’s have another good game. The Belles are in second place right now and knocking at our door. This is also Rio’s first series with the team, please make her feel welcome. Normal double-header protocol. If you feel too spent after the first game, let me know and I’ll sub you out.” 

Rio shyly waved from the back of the group. Agatha was surprised by how quiet she was. It could be the shock of the new environment and anxiety around a new team, but here, she was much less intimidating than she was on the field. 

“Ready to go, Flo?” Agatha nudged the starting pitcher as they strolled out.

“Ready as ever. How about that rookie pitcher? How lucky are we! She’s a real get for the team.” 

“You don’t have to worry. No one could replace you!”

“I don’t know. I think she could, but I don’t mind. I could use a rest now and then!” Flo was only a year and a half into her pro career, but pitching was incredibly tough on the body. 

The game kicked off, and it was clear that the Belles were going to be fierce competition. Their first three batters all got on base. Even with an impressive double play between the infielders, the opposition went up by one in the first inning. 

By the fourth inning, the Belles were leading 3-0. They had Flo’s number. Coach Grant had seen enough, and he called a time out. Agatha pushed her mask up and watched the conversation. Flo did not look pleased.  Is he pulling her already?

Coach walked with her to the dugout, and Rio came out in her place. 

Well, here we go. Agatha thought. The two had practiced a little but nowhere near enough. They didn’t even have the signs down. This will be interesting.

Agatha had the game ball, so she decided to walk up to the mound and hand it to Rio. 

“What signs do you use?” She asked the pitcher. 

“The usual. One for fastball, two for curve, three for change-up, four for a knuckleball.”

“You have a knuckleball?” Agatha asked in surprise. 

The curves of Rio’s mouth lifted, “I try to save it for special occasions.”

Agatha was intrigued but knew they didn’t have long, “Alright, let’s keep it simple today. I won’t bother with anything complicated. Just try to trust my calls okay?”

Rio nodded. She looked a little nervous, which was surprising given her normal overwhelming cockiness. Agatha handed the ball over, “I’ve seen your stuff, you’ll be fine.” 

She jogged back to her position. Rio walked a small circle around the rubber and then stood tall. They had a minute or so to warm-up before the game restarted. Agatha called for a fastball. Rio stared straight into the catcher’s eyes. Her gaze was so intense, Agatha felt it like it was reaching out and touching her. She watched the pitcher throw her hands back over her head and extend her leg, then fire the ball. 

Ouch! Wow. Agatha felt the sting in her palm from the power of the pitch. Even with her thick mitt, Rio was throwing at such fast speeds that it did little to blunt the force of impact. She was going to need to really focus on how she was catching these pitches, otherwise she wasn’t going to be able to feel her hand at the end of the game. 

She took a few more before the next Belle batter stepped up. She knew Winnie from past seasons. The center fielder had a strong throwing arm but was weak at the plate. Agatha threw down a two, outside. Rio nodded and wound up. She placed it perfectly - a sweeping curveball right on the edge of the strike zone. 

“Strike!” The umpire called and Agatha saw the pitcher light up. She really loves this. It was so obvious in the way Rio relished each strike. Every batter was a new battle, and she loved the fight, loved to win. Not unlike Agatha in many ways. 

She dropped a one, and Rio fired a fastball down the pipeline. The batter swung late and fouled it off. Two strikes. Agatha called it again, and Rio nodded without complaint. She’s listening to me. 

The final ball was perfect. Agatha marveled at the speed and placement. It was at the knees and had such velocity that the batter didn’t even have time to think about swinging. Out. Agatha grinned and threw the ball back to Rio. They made eye contact, and Agatha caught the genuine smile that reached Rio’s big brown eyes. It was so different from the arrogant grin that she normally put on. 

I could get used to this. Striking out a batter was incredibly difficult but oh so satisfying when the pitcher pulled it off. Rio gave her a short nod as if to convey her thanks, and Agatha nodded back. A silent exchange that felt as weighty as any conversation. They had a long way to go to overcome their past encounters, but this was a decent start. 

Rio shut out the rest of the batters in the inning and didn’t allow a single hit. Agatha was more than impressed.

“Great work out there, Vidal.” She offered as they entered the dugout. 

Rio glanced over, “Thanks, not so bad yourself. You really know the opposition.”

“Being an old-timer has its perks.” She explained and then went down to the end of the bench and removed her gear. Wanda joined her as usual. 

“That was some display out there. You two look real sweet together.” 

Peggy jumped in, “Yeah, Angel. We need to get Rio a matching nickname. Is the Devil too on the nose?”

They all cracked up at the joke, and Agatha said, “I was preparing for the worst, if I’m honest. But…I think she wants to win as badly as we do.”

“Coach is no fool. He wouldn’t sign her unless she was serious.” Wanda said. 

“Let’s see if she’s got the stamina. Any good pitcher can last one inning, the greats still got it after a hundred pitches.” Agatha sat down and watched Fossey at bat. They were still down by three and needed a strong attack to be able to come back. 

Rio was sitting with the other pitchers. They tended to flock together. Birds of a feather and all that. Agatha glanced down and saw her chatting with Lupe. She heard that they were rooming together with Horsey at the Smith house. 

She seemed to have such a natural charisma about her. Agatha watched the other players gravitate toward her and try to join the conversation. She was brand-new, which was always a big draw, but it was more than that. There was a feeling that she might say anything, an unguarded danger to her that was intriguing. 

Agatha considered going over but still felt a wall up between them. There was something unspoken that she couldn’t nail down. That strange pull that made Rio try to knock her head off upon their first meeting or made Agatha forget every rule. 

Instead, she watched Fossey hit a hard single and get on base. Followed by Buckets hitting an unfortunate pop-up to the shortstop, and then Betty stepping in as a pinch hitter and getting a single. 

“Let’s go, Dottie! You got this!” Agatha stood up and leaned against the dugout fence. 

Dottie turned and beamed a toothy smile before setting herself at the plate. Two pitches went by and the count was 1-1. The coach tipped his cap and subtly grabbed his elbow - their sign for a bunt. Dottie waited until the timing was right and bunted the ball perfectly down the first base line. 

Fern was off immediately and slid into third safely. Betty made it to second and Dottie was thrown out on a successful sacrifice play. Wanda was up next and primed to bring in the runners. 

“Bring ‘em home, Red!” Peggy shouted. 

“Go, Mom!!” Billy screeched out to the amusement of the entire dugout. 

Wanda looked as cool as ice. She handled pressure well and never seemed to crack. On the very first pitch, Agatha could tell that Wanda was going to swing. The bat connected right on the barrel and the ball shot out to the outfield. The left fielder sprinted for it, but it landed in the corner of the field. 

The two lead runners scored as the fans hollered and cheered in the bleachers. Wanda waved at the twins from second, who excitedly pointed and waved back with pride. The Comets were only down by one run. 

Tommy turned to Agatha and tapped on her arm repeatedly, “Are we gonna win?” 

“Do you think so?” She asked. 

“Yes, yes, yes!” He joyfully yelled and the gals all joined his chant until everyone was shouting “Yes!” Agatha looked over and saw Rio chanting along too. 

Horsey hit a single, which brought Wanda home and tied up the game. Peggy flied out, but the entire team was buzzed with energy from the great inning. 

“Let’s hold them.” Rio said as they walked out to the diamond together, and Agatha could only nod. 

She was struck by Rio’s singular focus and determination to win. She watched the pitcher. It was like seeing a bird arriving at its nest. She was at home on the mound; it looked so natural. She commanded it like a captain at sea. 

It didn’t erase everything, but if they were stuck together now, she might as well try to move on. 

Rio sat down the first two batters in just six pitches. The third hit a liner to Wanda who easily plucked it from its trajectory and got the final out.

The Comets scored once more in the eighth inning, and Rio continued her stellar debut all the way until the final out. Her first win in the books. 

“A round of applause for our new pitcher, Rio Vidal! Don’t forget to stick around as our Comets will be facing off against the Belles again in just one hour!” The announcer said. 

The crowd stood on their feet as the team departed the field. Rio waved her cap to the fans, who were shouting her name. Agatha wasn’t the only one impressed today. 

Back in the dugout, Coach Grant addressed them, “That’s what I’m talking about Comets! It’s one thing to destroy another team, it’s another to come from behind and take the win from them. No small thanks to Vidal for that unbelievable performance. Rest up while you can. Lilia brought sandwiches from the deli if you’re hungry. Lupe, make sure you’re warmed up.”

The team walked into the locker room and saw a platter of subs and fruit waiting. Lilia always knew exactly what they wanted. The folks at the deli were big fans and loved catering for their ball club. 

Agatha grabbed half a turkey and cheese and sat in one of the foldable chairs in the room, stretching her legs out onto the bench in front of her. Peggy and Wanda joined her. The twins were too busy playing tag with each other in the hallway to sit still. 

“How’s your hand, Angel? It looked like Rio wasn’t letting up all game.” Wanda asked between bites. 

Agatha looked down at her left palm. The angry red splotches were fading, “Sometimes it feels like I’m trying to catch a bullet. When it hits the wrong part of my glove, I swear I can feel it in my spine.” 

They laughed. Peggy scanned the room, “Hey Rookie, come sit with us!” She waved Rio over, who was grabbing a sandwich from the table. At first, she looked behind her to see if Peggy was talking to some other rookie but then realized it was her. 

She walked over and grabbed a seat. It was very strange for Agatha to see her two best friends sitting with her recent adversary turned teammate.  

“So…I’ve been dying to ask. Where ever did you learn to pitch like this?” Peggy leaned forward and asked before popping a grape in her mouth. 

Rio looked at them all, enjoying the attention, “My parents said I could chuck my binkie clear across the living room before my first birthday.” 

It sounded plausible until Rio's face broke and she burst out laughing, causing a chain reaction amongst the group. 

“I thought you were serious for a second there!” Wanda gasped, trying not to choke on her sandwich. 

“Sorry, you guys are too easy. In all seriousness, I grew up with seven cousins, all boys. Every single moment of daylight, we spent playing baseball in the street. My uncle showed me how to throw, and my eldest cousin taught me the basics of pitching. When they wouldn’t let me play with them, I would throw pitch after pitch at the brick wall behind our house. I swear that wall looks almost like a slice of swiss cheese, there are so many dents in it.” 

“You didn’t play in school or any organized teams?” Agatha asked. 

“The first uniform I ever put on was for the Sallies. There weren’t any girl’s sports at my school, and I didn’t know of any in my town. The baseball teams wouldn’t let me join.”

“Wow, no kidding? Where did you learn the rules and everything? It seems like you’ve been playing for years.” Agatha couldn’t believe that Rio just appeared out of nowhere with this level of skill. 

“I’ve been surrounded by baseball my whole life. There was always a game on the radio, and we would go see the local team play for free on weekends.”

“Who do you follow in the majors?” Wanda asked. 

“The Cubs.” Rio replied. 

Agatha groaned and failed to hide her disgust, “Why?” 

“My uncle loved Hack Wilson, and then they drafted the first Puerto Rican player in the MLB, Hiram Bithorn. What a pitcher. I tried modeling my wind-up after how the papers described his. I think the whole island supports the Cubbies.” Rio said. 

“I thought you were from Florida?” Wanda asked. 

“I was born in Puerto Rico, but grew up and lived in Florida for most of my life. What are your favorite teams?”

“The Reds” Wanda said proudly, flipping her scarlet hair over her shoulder. 

“Red Sox.” Peggy and Agatha spoke simultaneously. 

“I’m so sorry.” Rio held her heart and offered her condolences. “I can see why you are both so desperate to win. This is probably the only championship you’ll be able to celebrate for a while.” The Red Sox had been on a notorious drought since selling Babe Ruth to the Yankees in 1918. 

“You wound me. Now remind me, when’s the last time the Cubs won the World Series?” Agatha put her hand up to cup her ear, waiting for a response. 

Rio scowled and took a big bite of her sandwich to silence her frustration. (The Cubs hadn’t won a title since 1908). 

“These three aren’t bothering you are they?” Lupe came over and looked to Rio. 

“Hey, it’s not like we’re casting a spell on her or anything!” Agatha said to Lupe’s amusement. 

“Well, the pitchers are going to start warming up in 10 minutes, okay? You coming too, Agatha?” 

Agatha nodded, “Yes, boss. Let me just finish my meal.”

The pitchers and catchers wrapped up early to go practice before the next game started. The crowd had mostly dispersed though a few stragglers lingered. 

“Hey Rio! Hey!” Someone yelled out from the stands. “Your curves are almost as good as the ones you throw!” 

All the girls were used to indecent jeers. The first few years were the worst for it. Men who had no concept of women ballplayers and no cognitive skills to process that new information. Instead they would run their mouths and hurl insults and jabs. Most were just trying to get attention, some trying to get a date, and some just angry old fools. 

It had been a while since they’d had to deal with any, especially at a home game where the shame of a neighbor’s stare was usually enough to stop it. 

Agatha looked out into the stands and caught the eye of the guy. She knew most of the regulars and clocked him immediately, “Does your mom know you’re here, Glen? I’m not sure she would like you speaking to a lady like that!” 

His buddies cracked up at Agatha’s comment, and she saw his ears tint red. He stood up, “Shut up, Angel. I wouldn’t touch you with a ten foot pole!” 

“Is that a promise?” She snarked back and kept walking, turning her back to the petty argument and pulling her catcher’s mask down. She saw Rio smirking but silent as the pitcher kept walking to the warm-up mound. 

Helen kneeled beside her, catching for Rio, while Agatha practiced with Lupe, who was starting. 

“One day that mouth is going to get you in trouble.” The other catcher said between pitches. 

“If you don’t confront these chuckleheads, they’ll think they can speak to us like that. It’s not right. We have to stand up for ourselves - it’s not like anyone else is going to. You’re too far inside, Lupe!” 

“I can’t feel my hand.” Helen removed her glove for a second and tried to stretch her fingers out. Rio’s pitches could feel like catching a brick. 

“You have to try to catch it in the webbing. It’ll feel strange at first but it will lessen the sting.” Agatha advised. 

The second game started with the same intensity of the last. The two teams were equally matched and fighting hard to win. Lupe held her own and went the whole nine innings. Shirley scored in the sixth thanks to a double from Agatha, and the Comets recorded back-to-back wins. Coach released them for the night and gave them the next morning off before their next game tomorrow night. 

As Agatha pulled her bag onto her shoulder in the locker room, Rio put a hand up to catch her eye, “Agatha, can I talk to you for a minute?”

“I’ll see you at home, Peggy.” She urged her roommate on and set her bag back down. Peggy gave her a questioning look, but Agatha tried to reassure her with several quick nods. She could handle herself without it devolving to blows. She hoped anyway.

Rio was leaning against the row of lockers, arms crossed, biting her lip. An unnatural silence stretched between them. 

Agatha threw her hands up, “Well? I thought you had something to say.” She was tired and dirty. She didn’t particularly want to spend any more time at the ballpark. 

Rio caught the catcher’s gaze and Agatha felt that intensity again. Whether it was the size of her eyes or the heat behind her stare, it was hard to say, but Agatha fought the urge to look away. 

“I just wanted to apologize…truly. I’m sorry for how I treated you. It wasn’t fair.” Rio stared directly into Agatha’s steely blues. 

“What’s your deal? What did I do to piss you off?” Agatha dropped back to lean against the column in the center of the room, a few paces away from where Rio stood. She was very anxious to hear the answer. 

Rio started and stopped herself a few times before finally letting out, “It wasn’t you…”

“Don’t give me that ‘it’s not you, it’s me’ crap.” Agatha groaned. 

“Yeah okay, it was you.” She admitted, putting her hands up in front of her, “That was my first game. My first time ever pitching professionally. I wanted to be noticed. I didn’t want to be some forgettable newbie that people wrote off as just another rookie without a clue.”

Rio was staring down at the ground now, kicking her feet against the concrete. She continued, “Your reputation preceded you. I remember seeing your photo and reading about you in LIFE magazine back when the league first started. 

I was working the night shift at the shipyard in Miami, helping fix and maintain the Navy cargo ships. I dreaded every single second at work and would daydream that I was anywhere else. One of my coworkers was reading the latest LIFE , and I saw all you gals on the cover in your uniforms. I read the article at least a dozen times on my break - I couldn’t believe that there were other women out there who could play ball and that you were getting paid for it!

There was a picture of you at bat, and I ripped it out and kept it in my locker. It was my wish, my guiding light that maybe, just maybe, there was a better life out there for me. That one day I could see that sight, you, in real life, from the pitcher’s mound. That one day I wouldn’t be sweating in my coveralls, squinting through my welder’s mask, and counting down the seconds until my shift ended. 

When I finally got here, everyone talked about you. The Angel on the Comets - home run leader, MVP two years in a row, All Star catcher, and one of the highest batting averages in the league. You’re everything I want to be. You represented all my lost time, and I let my jealousy take over. It was stupid - I’m sorry.”

Agatha took it all in. She had never considered how other players perceived her, not really anyway. She had been so singularly focussed on winning and avoiding her past that she hadn’t paid much attention to much else. It wasn’t until Rio flew into the picture that she had thought something about a competitor besides their stats. 

“Wow, so…you kept a photo of me in your locker?” Agatha pursed her lips and thought she noticed a very faint blush coloring Rio’s cheeks. 

“That’s your takeaway? I pour my heart out for the first time in my life, and all you can focus on is that?”

“Sorry! It’s just…cute. The thought of you…staring at my big mug, dreaming of striking me out. You’re a real softie under that aloof exterior you put on.” 

Rio dipped her head and ran a hand through her dark brown hair casually, avoiding eye contact now, “Well…do you have anything to say to me?” She looked up at Agatha like an expectant puppy, waiting for a treat. 

“What? I thought this was your apology. I already wrote you one, remember?” 

“That was a very sad excuse. I ripped it up when my coach gave it to me. I knew you didn’t mean a word.”

Agatha barked out a laugh at that, “Yeah, I was forced to write it, but I did overreact. I shouldn’t have laid a hand on you. I took it too far. Though, I can’t say you didn’t deserve it.”

“How about a truce? What if the ballpark is Switzerland? When we’re playing, let’s put all that aside. I want to win. I want to win with you.” Rio stepped forward and extended her hand. 

Agatha looked down at it - the long fingers, red dirt beneath her nails, calluses dotting her palm - definitely a pitcher’s hand. She felt her heart start to pound and hoped her own hand wasn’t sweating. Why am I nervous?

She grabbed Rio’s hand and shook it confidently. They were both squeezing a tad harder than normal, attempting to project some image of strength, but smiling through it, knowing exactly the game they were playing.

“Deal. You know what they say? Keep your friends close….” Agatha was the first to let go and went to grab her bag.

“Am I your enemy?” Rio cut her off and picked up her own bag. She started walking out as Agatha trailed behind. 

“Not anymore.”

Chapter End Notes

Next chapter will be a big change in perspective. Time to turn the tables and see Rio's side of the story :)

Chapter 10: Welcome to the show

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“When I walked in, I was stunned . . . to see all these other girls my age throwing the ball like I did, swinging, running, sliding—and I thought, ‘Where did all these gals come from?!’”

Dolly Niemiec

 

“You can’t play with us!” The group of 8-year-old boys snatched the sad excuse for a baseball from Rio’s hand. At this point, the ball was more electrical tape than anything else. It was holding onto its last legs, but it was all they had. 

Rio was 7. She looked to her cousin, Christian, for help, but he wouldn’t even look at her. 

“That’s not even a baseball mitt.” The leader of the pack, Roberto, said with a sneer, looking with disdain at the makeshift glove that Rio’s aunt had repurposed from one of her uncle’s bricklaying gloves. It was cut, patched and sewn to fit her small hand. They couldn’t afford anything else. 

“Come on, let’s go.” Her cousin said in an effort to lead them away. “Don’t follow us.” He murmured at her quietly as they retreated. He took a big drink of cola and turned to follow the group of ten. 

She watched them walk away together and couldn’t understand why she wasn’t allowed to join. She played outfield when all the cousins played together. She could hit, and she could definitely throw. 

Rio felt she was about to burst. It was like when her aunt made her wear long dresses or when her teachers smacked her hands with a ruler for daydreaming in class. Without thinking she grabbed a rock from beside the brick wall and aimed it for her target. She used her full energy, leaned back, and chucked it. 

“AHH!” her cousin jumped as the glass bottle he was holding at his side exploded in a shattering crash.

Rio spun and bolted in the opposite direction, cackling with glee. 

 

Three months ago - May 1948

 

“Come on, Rio! You’re going to miss it!” 

The two women ran along the sidewalk to reach the Dade County train station. Rio checked her watch, two minutes until 9am. They had to make it. She needed to get there - her arms pumped faster despite the weight of her suitcase in one hand and her gear on her back 

They tore past the ticketing booth as Rio’s uncle had already secured her ticket weeks ago and burst out onto the platform. The train was roaring to life and people were already leaning out the windows, waving goodbye to their loved ones, twirling their white hankies in the air. 

“The 9 o’clock to Chicago via Atlanta is departing platform 1.” A detached voice announced. 

Rio turned, huffing and out of breath, and threw her arms around her best friend, “I’m going to miss you, Jen. I’ll write to you. I’ll tell you everything. Come visit if you can. I don’t know how I’m going to do this without you.”

Jen squeezed Rio tightly, then stepped back and levelled a glare, ‘You’re the best pitcher I’ve ever seen, Vidal. Show ‘em what you got. Don’t let anyone make you feel like you don’t belong. If any of those gals give you grief, give it right back, okay? I’m just a call away. I’ll look after the cousins for you.”

“Thank you…just thank you for everything. I-” 

“Go, you fool! It’s leaving!” Jen pushed Rio onto the steps of the train as it started to pull away. Rio ran her arm across her eyes to catch the falling tears and waved to her oldest friend. She had never been away from home before. She had never been north of Orlando in her life. This was her first train ride at the age of 26. 

It was surreal. She couldn’t believe she was actually on her way to her new life, her new career as a professional baseball player. It was only a month ago that Jen had seen an advertisement in the local paper about the All-American Girls Professional Baseball League. They were desperately searching for pitchers who could throw overhand. This was her shot.

She had wanted to go for it five years ago, but she was needed at home. Her three older cousins were all drafted, and she had to help her Aunt take care of the house. There were four younger cousins, and her wages at the shipyard helped pay for the ever-increasing cost of groceries and essentials. She couldn’t just leave. But the war was over now, things were slowly getting back to normal. 

When she saw the ad, Jen got her boss to call the scout and pretended to be Rio’s coach. He said Rio was the best pitcher in town and dominated their local league. It was not entirely untrue but certainly not the truth either. Rio had never played on any legitimate team before. Miami didn’t have any women’s softball leagues and none of the baseball teams would ever draft a girl. 

The lie worked, and Rio was granted a trial with a new team in the league, the Springfield Sallies. She would have to travel up to Illinois, and a contract was not guaranteed but if they liked her she would be in. They were offering $55 a week for rookie players and a daily meal allowance. It was certainly more money than she was earning here at a job she hated. 

What did she have to lose?

A chance was all she needed. She explained it all to her Uncle Hector, who was as thrilled as she was. 

“Mija, this is amazing! I’ll buy your train ticket. One-way. I just know that you won’t be needing a return pass.”

“No way, I have my savings from work. I can pay for it.” She knew it was going to be a losing argument but had to try. 

“Never turn down a gift, Rio. I want to do this for you. In return, I expect a good seat when I come up to see you. We’ll find a way if you make it to the championship. Deal?” 

“Of course. Thank you, thank you, thank you.” She felt so full of joy and gratitude that she didn’t know what else to say. 

Now she was on her way. Off to a new city, a new state, a new beginning. She found an empty seat in the middle of the carriage and stored her luggage in the rack above. She stared out the window, imagining what awaited her. 

Norma Walsh greeted Rio at the Chicago Central Station, “Ms. Vidal? I’m Norma, the chaperone for the Sallies. I’ll drive you to town and escort you to the Corner Inn where you’ll stay for the next few days. If you make the team, I’ll arrange you a room with a local family.”

Rio was exhausted from the train journey. It had taken a whole day, and she longed to lay down in a bed. “Thank you, ma’am. What does a chaperone do for the team?”

“None of that ma’am stuff. Just Norma is fine. And we handle most of the logistics like lodging and transportation. I look after all the girls on the team. Part medic, part counsellor, part coach, part mother. It certainly keeps me on my toes.” She led them to her old Ford and popped the trunk. 

The next morning, Rio reported to the field early to join the team for practice. There were five other women vying for a spot on the team. She overheard their small talk but kept to herself. 

“I’ve been playing for my company team for three years. I throw mostly side arm.” One boasted. 

“I played softball all four years of high school, but I can also throw overhand. It’s just not as controlled as my windmill pitches.”

“Have y’all seen the ball they’re using? It’s almost down to a baseball in size. I’ve never played with a ball so small before.” Another lamented, the tone of worry evident in her voice. 

Rio just stood and stretched her glove open and closed. The well-worn leather answered her every command. It was like a second skin at this point. She had inherited it from her eldest cousin, Esteban, when she had turned 15. It was marginally too big, but at this point, she knew its every quirk and trait like a mother knows her child. It was a far cry from the bricklaying glove she grew up using. 

The main players from the Sallies joined them on the field followed by the coach and the chaperone. 

“Today we’ll be practicing as usual, but also trying to find at least two new pitchers for our bullpen. These five will be throwing batting practice for the rest of you. I’ll be evaluating each and taking in all of your feedback at the end. We’ll also run fielding drills to see if they might be better suited to other positions.” 

Rio watched the first three newbies give it their best shot. They each pitched to four batters and attempted for strike-outs. The Sallies were good, surprisingly so. She watched the first baseman, Greta Gill belt one all the way to deep outfield and then the shortstop, Jess McCready, shot a rocket right over second base. 

Her nerves started to rise. She knew she could strike out the guys from the neighborhood in their back-alley games, but this was for real. This wasn’t just schoolyard pick-up, these women were professionals. Could she actually do this? 

“Next! Rio Vidal! Did I say that right?” Coach yelled. 

“Yes, sir.” She said under her breath and walked out to the mound. She pushed down every doubt and tried to summon all the poise she could muster. She thought about the broad from the magazine. Agatha? That gal would never be scared, would never show fear. Rio pretended she was that woman in the picture as she caught the ball and delivered her first pitch.

With the first throw, she felt everyone turn and look. With the second, she silenced all chatter. With the third and final strike, she knew she belonged. 

She hadn’t even left the mound when the Coach shook her hand and offered the position, starting immediately. She had struck out three in a row and only allowed a single from the catcher, Carson Shaw. Only one other pitcher made the squad as a back-up outfielder and emergency reliever.

“Where did you come from? I don’t think there is anyone in this league that can throw as hard as you just did.” Carson came up to Rio after practice finished. 

“Thanks, I’m from Florida. I played ball in the streets of Miami ever since I was old enough to walk. I can’t believe I’m going to get paid to do this.” 

“It never goes away. That feeling. I’ve been playing for five years and sometimes I still pinch myself. These years have been the best of my entire life. It’s changed me, for the better” Carson said. 

Rio ended up rooming with the other rookie, Grace Strickland. Norma set them up with a young married couple, who had two spare rooms and were saving for a baby. The house was nice enough. Rio knew she wasn’t going to see much of it. She intended to sleep here and spend every other waking second practicing. 

She wasn’t here for a vacation. She was here to win. 

“Jen? Can you hear me?” Rio was on the payphone outside the ballpark after her first official practice wearing the uniform. 

“Rio! I’m guessing you made it since you haven’t come back.”

“Yes, they signed me for the season. Can you believe it?”

“I knew it! Do you have to wear those ridiculous uniforms?” Jen asked. They had laughed at the photos of the players when they first saw them years ago. How was a ballplayer meant to play in a skirt?

“Unfortunately, yes. We can’t even wear pants in public! But they pay the salaires, they make the rules. I’m not about to argue!” Rio preferred trousers, especially on the field, but when the choice was playing pro ball in a skirt or going back to her laborious job in pants, she knew what she would pick. 

“Gosh, did I send you to play ball or to the drill academy? I didn’t know they were so strict…anyway, where ya livin’?”

“They got us staying with a couple in town. I can walk to the ballpark, which is handy.”

“They’re not…weird, are they? What’s a couple doing letting two young gals into their abode?”

“No, Jen. They seem fine. They get rent money from us, so it’s a nice deal for them. I’m barely there anyway. I only go there to sleep.” 

“Right, well if that fella tries anything funny, remember to throw punches first, ask questions later. Got it?” 

Rio couldn’t help but laugh at her friend’s bluntness, “I can take care of myself. You taught me everything I know. I gotta run, but I’ll talk to you after my first game in a few weeks.” 

“I’ll be waiting by the phone. Have fun, kid.” Jen said and hung up. 

Rio rode the bench at first. The coach wanted her to get a feel for the game and watch before throwing her into the deep end. She didn’t mind either. It was nice to ease into things and observe the game up close. 

She took note of the pacing, the subtle cues and rituals, the spoken and unspoken rules, and the players. It was like watching a complicated dramatic theatrical production. Everyone had their roles, and the game moved in a delightfully unpredictable, choreographed dance. 

It was very clear from the start that the Sallies were facing an uphill battle. The other teams were much more in sync and clearly had years of experience under their belts. Every team was struggling with the pitching change, but a strong defense could make up for a lot of problems on the mound. 

The Sallies did not have a strong defense. 

They had good players, great even, but a team was forged in repetition and practice. They simply didn’t have as much time to grow together as the others did. They were a ragtag group of transfers and recruits. Greta was top-tier at first base, but the other infielders often threw it just out of her reach. Jess was incredibly athletic and a perfect fit for short-stop, but she didn’t cover second base in key situations. Cookie in left field could catch anything hit her way, but her arm let her down. 

It was the little things that added up and resulted in losses. 

Coach Doyle was trying to give the veteran pitchers time to adjust and settle into the team, but by July, the scoresheet was painful to look at. On the Friday before their next series, he approached Rio after batting practice. 

“Are you ready?” He looked at her directly, which took her aback at first. He rarely addressed her one-on-one. 

She raised an eyebrow, “For…what?”

“To play tomorrow.” 

Rio held in a gasp. This was it - what she had been waiting for. Her debut. “Yes, I’ll be ready, Coach.”

He nodded curtly and left her standing there, in disbelief. 

Greta rushed over and snaked an arm around her shoulder, “Couldn’t help but overhear. Congrats, Rookie!”

Carson was suddenly at her other side, “Maybe we can finally win a game this season if you’re out there with us.” 

Rio felt shy under their praise. She wasn’t used to this type of camaraderie. “I’ll try anyway. Who are we playing?”

“The Comets.” Carson replied. 

“Ooooooh, watch out for Angel. She’s their catcher. Big time power at the plate. Most pitchers choke when she steps up, so don’t let her get in your head. Even though it’s hard to forget her good-looks.” Greta winked at Carson, and Rio saw something pass between them that she couldn’t quite pin down. 

“They’ve also got Peggy and Wanda. Those two do anything to get on base. They’ll chase pitches and somehow hit them way outside the zone. The Comets are very aggressive on offense. We’ll need to be at our best.” Carson was a veritable gold mine of facts on other teams and players. 

Rio felt the nerves start to dial up in her stomach. She knew she had the skill, but there was a difference between knowing it and showing it. 

“We’ve got your back, chickadee. Just throw like you do in practice, and you’ll blow them away.” Greta tried to calm down the panic that she could see behind Rio’s eyes. 

On game day, Rio watched Ruth pitch the first few innings. The Comets were chewing her up and spitting her out. Every inning they scored at least one run, and by the third inning, Rio started warming up her arm. She knew she would be called in at any moment. 

“Vidal. You’re in.” Coach waved her on after pulling Ruth in the fourth. 

She closed her eyes for one second and allowed herself to savor this moment. Her first game, her first time taking the field as a professional baseball player. She tried to soak it all in and remember every little thing - the way her fingers felt in the well-worn grooves inside her mitt, the smell of grass, dirt, and sweat, the sun on her skin, the chatter of the crowd. 

She opened her eyes and took the field. 

Staring down the pipe, she watched Carson for the signs. She ignored the batter entirely, and just looked at the catcher’s mitt, her target. One, two, three. Just like that, her first strikeout. 

The crowd roared, and she did the first thing she could think of - she dipped down for a royal bow. Fans hollered their approval at the gesture, and she relished in the applause. She felt strong, she felt worthy. 

The next batter made her way out. She heard the announcer call her name. 

It was her. 

Angel. Agatha Harkness. 

The woman from the picture. 

This was the singular moment she had dreamed about. The fantasy that had carried her through years of hard labor, endless back-breaking shifts, and pitts of lonely despair. Pitching against the batter from the magazine. 

It was happening. This was real. 

She inhaled long and slow through her nose, holding the air in her lungs, trying to settle her suddenly shaking hand. Carson called for a curveball. Rio nodded and started her wind-up. 

She felt it leave her hand a split second early and let out a soft groan of frustration as it landed inside for a ball. 

“You’re the best pitcher I’ve ever seen…” Jen’s words circled in her mind like a mantra. She let the affirmation fuel her. The catcher threw down a one - fastball. 

The speed was there, but the placement was high. Another ball. 

Agatha was crowding the plate, making it difficult to land the pitches where she wanted them. The umpire had a tight strike zone and wasn’t giving much leeway. 

Rio watched the batter step out and heard her scoff. The sound cut through her bones. She was writing her off, dismissing her with a single noise. Rio paced in a circle to try and stamp down the growing flame inside. Her hands started to twitch, and she wiped a bead of sweat that raced down her temple. 

This is not how she saw this going. She had played this moment in her head ten thousand times. She used to close her eyes before bed and think of the pitches she would throw, how the crowd would cheer, how her teammates would celebrate when she threw the final strike. 

It wasn’t supposed to happen like this. 

Agatha took her stance again. Rio visualized the next pitch in her mind. She saw Carson’s call and focussed on the catcher’s large, waiting glove. Her eyes flicked to the batter. Big mistake. 

She winked. 

And Rio’s mind went blank. Her old pal, anger, was back. Before she could stop herself, the ball had left her hand aimed directly at the source of her anger. 

Oh no. Oh fuck no. 

Agatha hit the deck to avoid the incoming projectile. Rio’s dream turned into a nightmare as the woman from the magazine launched off the page right into her face. She couldn’t even register the words blasting from her mouth. It was too surreal. She was starstruck. 

When it was clear that she had been asked a question, she could only shrug as she didn’t know what had been said. Next thing she knew she was flat out on the dirt. Rio couldn’t register any pain. She didn’t know why she was on the ground. All she could do was smile like an idiot at the raging batter in front of her. 

Finally. 

She had finally found worthy competition. Someone who would actually challenge her skill. Someone who gave as much of a damn. Someone who could make her a better pitcher. 

Agatha Harkness. 

She watched the batter be thrown out of the game. A shame, but she knew they would meet again. 

“Who’s coming with us to the motherfucking bar?” Greta shouted out in the locker room as the gals were drearily getting dressed after the loss.  

“You know I’m in.” Carson smiled. “You coming, rookie?” She looked to Rio. 

“Sure, why not?” Rio answered cooly, pulling her dress on. She wasn’t a big drinker, but she remembered that Jen said team bonding was just as important as the playing part. She expected a night of dancing and casual conversation. 

What she didn’t expect was to face Agatha again so soon. She looked different. She looked good. The fabric hugged her hips and the neckline was suggestively low. 

Rio tried not to look, not to stare. She was here to play baseball. She wasn’t here for…well anything else, especially not this, not again, never again. 

But as Agatha moved across the dancefloor with a hopeless chump attempting to lead, Rio was helpless. 

She watched the way the fabric spun out around her knees. She admired the grace with which she twirled to the music. She stared at… no . She stared down at the beer bottle in her hands instead, casually playing with the drops of condensation, trying anything to distract herself from the thoughts that she couldn’t want. 

When the object of her fascination stormed over and yelled at her again, she should have shrunk away. She should have felt embarrassed. But she didn’t. She felt heat rising along the tight collar of her dress, and it wasn’t anger this time. 

“Jen…” Rio stood at the payphone near the practice field. She didn’t like using the phone at her host home due to the long distance charges. 

“Rio? What’s wrong? Did something happen?” Jen was worried. Rio was usually self-assured and confident in her words. 

“No, nothing like that. I am in trouble though.” 

“Why? Did you hit someone? Rio, I told you to be careful with stuff like that. You could get suspended or worse...thrown out of the league. And then what? Do I need to come get you? Oh god, I’ll have to get on a train. If they even let me on. How am I going to get time off work? I can’t-”

“Jen! Calm down, it’s not that. Jeez. It’s…well…we might have another…Rosie situation.” 

The line went silent and then Jen finally uttered out, “Who is it?”

“Do you remember that picture from my locker? The beautiful batter?” 

“No, Rio, no! Is she on your team?” 

“She’s on the Comets.” 

“Okay, so you won’t have to see her much. It won’t be a problem, right?” Jen had a tone of warning. “You know what will happen if you go down that road. I can protect you from a lot, but not everything, not that.”

Rio unlocked the box in her mind for just a second. The one that she kept on the highest shelf, hidden from view. The one she had to purposefully choose to ignore. 

Rosie Morales. 

Her best friend through high school. Until she wasn’t. 

“Rio?” Jen asked. 

“Sorry, I know. I know. I’ll try to keep my distance. I’ll be fine, I promise. She’s probably married anyway. She’s got a temper like your father.” Rio leaned against her forearm that draped across the phone booth. 

“I know you like them with some fire.” Jen snarked. 

“I’m here for baseball.”

“You better be. If you find yourself slipping, call me immediately. I’ll talk you off the ledge.”

“Thanks, Jen. I have to go but thank you. Talk soon.” Rio hung up after hearing the positive affirmation from her oldest friend. Jen always knew how to knock some sense into her. 

“She’s so good, Jen.” Rio was back at the payphone after her second game pitching. The stadium had cleared out, but she had to make this call. 

“You better mean good on the field.” Jen said. 

“Obviously. You should see her arm. She threw me out trying to steal. I couldn’t believe it. The throw was literally inch perfect.” Rio tried and failed to hide her amazement. 

“And? These are professionals, not just the back-alley chumps you’re used to playing with.” 

“She’s different. A different level than the rest. It’s like she’s constantly trying to get at me and throw me off, so I’m forced to give it right back.”

“Tell me about anyone else on your team or any other team.” Jen tried to redirect her. 

“Oh, ummm. There’s Greta on my team. She’s got a mouth on her, curses like a sailor! But she can really hit, and she’s probably one of the best first sackers in the league. There’s Carson too. Those two are like peas in a pod. Carson’s our catcher…not as skilled as Agatha, mind you, but she’s a real leader for the team. Everyone follows her lead.”

“Okay, follow them. You need to focus on something else, anything. I know how you get.”

Rio swallowed her protests. Jen was right. “We’re heading out on the road soon anyway. I probably won’t see her for weeks.”

“Good. Get closer to your teammates. They are your family for the season. Keep your head in the game above all. Your uncle told me to tell you that everyone misses you.”

“I miss them all. Tell them I’m okay and having the best time. I might not be able to call for a while. See ya!” Rio picked up her bag off the ground at her feet and strolled home alone. 

In the hotel in Racine, Rio was rooming with Carson. She liked her company and was incredibly impressed by her detailed note cards on their opponents. The catcher liked to write out tips for the pitchers on every single batter. She knew their strengths, their weaknesses, and which pitches worked best for all different scenarios. It was invaluable information.

The night after the first game against the Belles, Rio went out to dinner with Jess and the outfield girls. They were all craving burgers, so she tagged along. They invited her for drinks at a local bar, but she was feeling way too full and way too tired to join. 

“I’m going to head back. Thanks anyway!” Rio waved them off. 

She decided to take the stairs up to the third floor where her room was. She turned the key and opened the door in a quick, fluid motion. 

Rio froze in the doorway. 

Greta Gill was straddling Carson in one of the beds, both of them in their underclothes and engaging in what could only be described as intense necking. 

Rio hastily turned and shouted, “Sorry!” as she backed out of the room and shut the door. She stood in shock in the hallway for a minute as she heard her teammates fumbling around in the room and whispering at each other. 

The door reopened and Greta popped out in a lopsided dress, the buttons not quite done up correctly. Her usually perfect hair was sticking out in the back like a tumbleweed. 

“Rio…” She put a hand on Rio’s forearm. “You won’t…you won’t tell anyone will you?” Greta worried at her lip and looked up and down the hall. 

“No, I won’t, really. I won’t say anything.” Rio did her best to reassure her. 

Greta narrowed her eyes and assessed the pitcher. She seemed to trust her words, “I’ll see you at practice in the morning.” She practically ran off to her own room on the floor below. 

Rio hesitated before opening the hotel room again, but finally walked in and saw Carson leaning back on the bed, staring up at the slow-moving, creaking fan.

The catcher sat up and scooted over to the edge, “Hey, sorry…I thought you were going to be out late.”

“I should have knocked.” Rio offered and shrugged her shoulders. 

“Are you gonna…you know?” Carson lifted her brows and winced.

“What?”

“Report us.” 

“Why would I do that?” Rio sat down on her bed opposite Carson. 

Carson’s mouth turned down in a frown, “What do you want?” 

“Huh? I don’t want anything.” Rio shook her head. 

“Rio, you saw us kissing. Two women. Most people would be aghast, offended, shocked. Are you going to hold this over us or something? Blackmail us to the coach?”

Rio pushed up against her own thighs and stood carefully. She walked back and forth in front of the beds. 

“Why are you pacing?” Carson asked, watching the pitcher walk the same line over and over again. 

Rio battled internally with herself. She was here for baseball. She was here for baseball. But…she had never met anyone else. Anyone like her. 

She stopped. 

“I get it.” The words climbed out of her mouth. 

“You get what?”

“Being…” Rio nodded suggestively, hoping she wouldn’t be forced to say it but Carson was just looking at her with pure confusion. Rio groaned, “...queer.”

The whites of Carson’s eyes appeared like headlights and a salacious smile cut across her face, “You?” She pointed. 

“Yeah.” Rio admitted for the first time in her life to Carson’s over-dramatic gasp. 

“The Comets are playing their last game against the Blue Sox. You two want to check it out?” Greta proposed after they finished their game. 

They took Greta’s car for the short ride. Now that Rio was in on their secret, Carson and Greta were able to hold hands for the entire trip. It was tooth-achingly sweet. 

“When did you know, Rio?” Greta asked. “That you were…like us?”

“There wasn’t a singular moment. It didn’t flip on like a lightbulb. I think I’ve just always been like this. I lost my parents when I was really young, so my aunt and uncle raised me. They have seven boys, so I was always around them. Everyone would mistake me for a boy because I just wore their hand-me-downs. 

I didn’t have many girl friends until high school besides my best friend, Jen. Boys used to try to kiss me or ask me on dates, but I wasn’t interested. Until this new girl walked into my English class. I damn near fainted when she walked in the door. I had never seen a more beautiful person in my entire life. All I wanted to do was spend time with her, impress her, and be around her.”

“Awwww, baby’s first crush.” Greta teased. 

“Basically.” Rio smiled at the memory. “I didn’t know that at the time. Nothing would have ever happened if it had been left to me, but one day, after school, we were walking home together and she asked me if I ever kissed anyone. I said no, and she asked if I wanted to practice.”

“Clever girl.” Carson said. “What a classic move.” 

“I was putty in her hand, and she knew it. We started having kissing practice every moment that we could find alone. I had never felt anything like that before. God, my heart still races thinking about it.”

“What happened? I’m guessing you guys didn’t live happily ever after?” Greta asked. 

“We were caught at school in the janitor’s closet by a teacher. We tried to deny it, but she had seen us. They called my aunt and her parents. And I never saw her again.” Rio looked out the window forlornly. 

“Did you get in trouble with your family?” Carson turned around and looked at her. 

“I lied. It was cowardly but…I was scared. I blamed her and said she forced me to. My aunt believed me, but I could tell she doubted it a little. She kept a real close eye on me after that.”

Carson nodded in understanding, “Where did the girl go?”

“No idea. She was pulled out of school immediately, and I was too scared to go near her house. I waited a few weeks and walked by it, but they had moved.”

“Sorry, Rio. That’s tough. Have you been with anyone since?” Greta asked.

“Women? No. I didn’t know if I could go through that again. That shame, that all-consuming shame. It’s soul crushing. I dated a few fellas from work, but well…you know how that is.” She laughed. It felt so freeing to be able to talk about this with other people that weren’t Jen. 

“You know it's not just us two, right? The league is swimming with other queers. Most keep it way underwraps, but if you’re in the know, you can find someone easier than you think.” Greta explained. 

Rio’s mind swooped immediately to a certain catcher and Carson caught the pause, “Someone making you dizzy?”

“Ummm…” Rio didn’t really want to reveal all her embarrassing secrets but at this point, what the hell? “The Comets. The catcher.”

“Angel?! You’re carrying a torch for Agatha Harkness??” Greta nearly swerved off the road, and they all grabbed for something to hold onto. 

Rio tried to readjust herself back in the seat, having flown across to the other side, “I’m not going to act on anything. I think she hates me anyway.”

“Wow, no. Sorry, I’m just shocked. Angel is notoriously spiky. She’s not very personable and can be hard to understand. A complicated past, I heard. Why her?” Greta asked. 

“Why anybody? I don’t know. There’s something about her. She’s a puzzle I would love to solve.” 

“You’re dreaming if you think you have a chance. I swear she’s as straight as the day is long.” Carson added. 

“So were you, darling.” Greta quipped and gently put an arm over her lover’s shoulders. 

“Want some, uh, some extra time with Angel?” Greta stumbled over the question. They were a few drinks in after the game finished. A few fans in the stands had kept the drinks flowing when they found out the three of them were also ballplayers. 

“Careful, Greta. Maybe we should go back.” Carson suggested, wary of the look in Greta’s eyes. 

“Let’s propose a wager. You know they won’t be able to resist. We could get some cash for a little getaway, toots.” She winked and saw Carson relent. 

Greta saw Agatha and other Comets players leaving the ballpark, “Hey Butterfingers! Nice hitting tonight.”

Rio’s mind was dragging along one or three or five too many beers. Suddenly, she was sitting in the outfield and finishing off another drink. Someone asked why she was there and she somehow managed to piece together a coherent response of “The only way to get better is to play against the best.”  

The next moments came in broken flashes. 

Jumping the fence onto the dark, empty field. 

Taking the mound, barely able to even see the catcher’s mitt. 

Pitching to two batters like it was nothing. The repetition in her bones.  

Seeing Agatha come up to the plate, looking as fiercely determined as ever. 

Hearing the loud crack of the bat as she hit a no-doubt homer. 

Feeling Anger tap at her back and take control of the wheel. 

Saying something awful to Agatha and watching her face break at Rio’s terrible insults. 

The pain of a slap across her face. Tackling, pushing, shoving, limbs flying. 

Then the agony in her hand as she connected with the beautiful face that haunted her dreams. 

Shame, embarrassment, disappointment, running, car ride, ice on her hand, falling asleep. 

“I think you may have fucked that up.” Greta said over breakfast the next morning. 

“Ya think?” Rio deadpanned back. She was struggling to even grip her fork to pick up the scrambled eggs on her plate. 

“What possessed you? Why’d you serve up that knuckle sandwich?” Greta asked. 

Rio tried to call back the memories, but it was like her mind was blanketed in fog. It was such a blur. “I didn’t mean to hit her. I was trying to get that other gal who slapped me. It was an accident.” 

“We’re going to drive over and give them their winnings in a few hours. Do you want to come? You could apologize in person.” Carson said. 

“I don’t think I can look her in the eyes. I’m mortified.”

“Suit yourself. Well, we’re playing them again in a few weeks, so you’re going to have to face her again whether you like it or not.” Carson wiped her mouth with the thick hotel napkin and pushed back to stand. Greta finished off her toast and left Rio alone at the table. 

They wouldn’t be playing the Comets in a few weeks. They wouldn’t play the Comets again. The next day back in Springfield, the coach gathered the team at the field in the morning and made the announcement. 

“The league is closing us down. The Sallies and the Colleens are done. They are exploring options to send us on the road together as a barnstorming duo, but nothing is determined yet. I’m so sorry, girls. Don’t make any arrangements yet. The top brass said they would let me know the next steps in a few days.”

“But Coach…what are we meant to do?” Greta stepped forward and asked what was on all their minds. 

“We wait. Season’s almost over anyway so not much we can do. I’m sorry.” He frowned and tossed his hands up in defeat. 

“We can’t fight this? What if we fundraised or-” Greta tried again. 

“Decision is final. The teams are hemorrhaging money and not earning it back. I’ll send word when I have news.” He grimaced and walked off the field without fanfare. 

“Fuck!” Greta shouted and kicked the dirt in frustration. 

“This is bullshit.” Jess shook her head and chewed on a toothpick furiously. 

“I was just getting started…” Rio lamented. 

To her surprise, it wasn’t the coach who called a few days later. Their chaperone, Norma, gave her a ring in the morning. 

“I just spoke with management, Rio. A team has made an offer to buy out your contract. They want you for the remainder of the season.”

“Wow, that’s incredible. What team?” 

“The Comets.” 

Rio’s heart sank. Agatha’s team. Why’d it have to be her team? “Is that the only offer?” She asked carefully. 

“Is that not good enough?” Norma snapped back, “No other girl on the team has gotten an offer.”

“Sorry, I was just curious. I’ll take it. Of course, I’ll take it. Thank you, Norma.” Rio would be crazy to pass up the chance to join a strong team like the Comets. Personal issues aside, they were in serious playoff contention, and many were tipping them to win the whole shebang. 

“I thought so. Give me a week or so to sort out the paperwork and finalize the transfer. You’ll be on the next bus to Kenosha in no time.” 

When she walked onto the Comets field for the first time, Agatha looked at her like she had seen a ghost.. 

She expected a frosty reception but imaging it and experiencing it were very different. Throughout the practice, she caught Agatha’s glares and stares. It was like she was trying to wish her away. 

She watched the catcher follow the coach off the field and could only assume what she was going to say. Rio was meant to meet with Coach Grant to discuss the upcoming games, so she trailed after them. 

Bits and pieces of the argument filtered through the door. 

“...too green…she’s dangerous…”

And then, clear as day, “How am I going to play with her?”

Rio was leaning against the wall when Agatha was suddenly standing in front of her. Irritation was clear on her face. Her eyes were the most beautiful blue color. 

When the catcher said “eat my ass”, Rio almost collapsed in a belly laugh. It was so unexpected, so raw. The visuals that shot into her brain would give a nun a heart attack. She grappled with her rising pulse. 

Talk of killing each other shouldn’t have made her palms sweat, but that was a peculiarity to examine another day. 

Agatha called her “superstar”, and she felt like she won something.

Chapter End Notes

Oh yeah, Rio has been down bad since the very beginning :)

Chapter 11: Seventh inning stretch

“You know, the league years were a tough time, but they were a great time. You’d have lonely hours on the road in hotels or at home in your room all alone, and then you’d have fantastic times with the games and the other girls and the fans and the outings.” Marge Cryan

July set and August rose, announcing itself with early mornings and warm, humid nights. Baseball was a constant throughout, and the Comets were racing to the finish line in spectacular form. 

They swept the series against Racine and had an uncharacteristic three day break before the next run of games against the Rockford Peaches on the road. The team was currently standing at 56 wins, 28 losses. 

They were first in the league. The Blue Sox were nipping at their heels though, so they needed to stay strong for the rest of the month. The playoffs started at the beginning of September.

With an extra long weekend on the horizon, Horsey had a proposition to the team after their last win against the Belles, “My parents said I could invite everyone to their lake house. It’s a great spot on Fox Lake, about 45 minutes away. We’ve got canoes, a fire pit, and the swimming is swell.”

“No kidding! Count me in!” Peggy jumped at the chance. 

“Can the twins come too?” Wanda asked, and Horsey nodded enthusiastically. 

“Sure, there are four bedrooms, plus an entire finished basement and plenty of space for everyone.” 

“I could use a vacation.” Agatha said, joining the chorus of affirmations from the entire team. 

“I’ve never been to a lake house before.” Rio said excitedly. 

Just four players couldn’t make it. Shirley was afraid of water and warned them all about flesh-eating bacteria, Fossey wasn’t feeling very well and didn’t want to put a damper on the fun, Sugar’s boyfriend was in town, and Betty still lived at home and didn’t think her parents would approve. 

“Alright, there’s plenty of parking. Tell me if you’re driving, and I’ll write down the address. Bring a sleeping bag, swim clothes, and a good attitude. There’s a grocer in town, so we can get any food there. I’m so excited!” Horsey clapped her hands. 

“Hey Dottie, can Peggy and I ride with you tomorrow?” Agatha asked before anyone else had the chance. 

“Sure, Angel. Helen’s coming as well. I can probably fit one more.” Dottie said. 

Buckets put her hand up immediately to take the last spot. The other gals sorted out the car arrangements, and they were all set. 

The next morning, Agatha and Peggy quickly scarfed down the generous plates of waffles. 

“Thanks for letting us borrow your sleeping bags. We’ll bring them back safely.” Agatha said to their hosts. 

“You better. Those were expensive!” Pearl admonished. 

“Be careful in the water, girls. You don’t want to get hurt right before the playoffs begin.” Gertie warned them. 

“We’ll be good, mums.” Peggy smirked and downed the last of her orange juice as they heard the loud beep from Dottie’s car out the front. 

Agatha jumped up and thanked them for the meal before throwing her duffel bag over her shoulder, “See you on Monday!” 

They scooted into the backseat together with Buckets. It was a tight squeeze but not uncomfortable. It was not a long drive. They distracted each other with chat about the upcoming games. 

“What’s the verdict? How many do we have to win to top the league?” Helen asked the car. 

“We have to win at least half of the remaining 20 games, and the Blue Sox have to not win every single one. If they go on a crazy streak, they will snatch the top spot.” Agatha had written down all sorts of variations in her journal. The most likely scenario was the Comets won, but there were several edge cases where that didn’t happen. 

They could somehow lose all the upcoming games and drop to third or fourth. The Peaches and Blue Sox could both go on streaks and push the Comets down the table. Baseball was as unpredictable as a bucking bronco sometimes. 

“I have a feeling we’re gonna win.” Dottie declared. She had been on the team for three years and had yet to advance past the first round of the playoffs. 

“With Rio on the team, I think we could do anything.” Helen added.

“Amen! As long as our bats are hot, we’re going to be unstoppable.” Dottie said and the rest agreed. 

Agatha stared out at the passing landscape and daydreamed of all that was to come. They were good now, but the pressure of the playoffs was a whole different story. She had seen amazing teams crumble as if they were made of sand. She had watched scrappy, low ranking teams turn to diamonds and upset everyone. She had been around long enough to temper her expectations. 

“Let’s just get through August. One game at a time.” Agatha said.

Peggy booed her to the laughter of the rest of the gals. “Don’t bring us down, Angel. Have a little faith!” 

Dottie pulled down the long driveway to Horsey’s family lake house. 

“Golly, what a place!” Helen exclaimed as they took in the large cabin overlooking the water. It wasn’t flashy or sprawling, but it was impressive nonetheless. Based on the number of cars there, everyone else had already arrived. 

Agatha walked in first and immediately saw Billy and Tommy chasing each other in circles around the living room. 

“Welcome! Glad you made it okay, you better grab a bed while you can!” Horsey called out and pointed down the hallway. 

Agatha didn’t need to be told twice. She immediately rushed and looked into every room. The first door she opened was obviously the twins’ as the middle of the floor was littered with a pile of toy planes and cars. 

Nope, definitely not. She closed the door immediately. 

The next room was two sets of bunk beds. No way. 

Directly across from that was a small pink room. There was only one single twin bed that Buckets was sitting on. “There’s room on the floor for a sleeping bag.” She offered but Agatha had already shut the door. 

Running out of options, she approached the last door at the end of the hall. It was the biggest room by far but only had a large king bed. Lupe was opening her bag near the dresser. 

“I’ve already called this one with Horsey. Sorry!” She shrugged. 

Agatha walked back out and saw the other girls picking from the choices she turned down. 

“Hey Horsey, where else can I sleep?” She asked the hostess. 

“I think Rio has set up shop in the basement. You can join her down there. The couches are comfier than they look.” 

Agatha grimaced and faked a smile. Of course it had to be Rio. There were eleven other teammates here and somehow, some twisted universal force was determined to make her suffer. 

She walked down the wooden stairs off the kitchen into the basement. It was less a basement and more just a lower floor of the house. The room was wide open, wood-panelled like the rest of the place, and carpeted. There was a row of bookshelves covering the wall next to an old fireplace, and three floral sofas arranged in a u-shape in the center. Various other armchairs were arranged around the space, along with stacks of board games and piles of sporting equipment. 

The hero of the room were huge picture windows across an entire wall facing the lake. 

Agatha sucked in a breath, “Whoa.” She whispered. 

“It’s something, right?” A voice said, emerging from behind a sliding pocket door that looked to be a spare bathroom. 

Agatha had wondered where Rio was, “Yeah, what a view. Don't know why everyone else chose those rooms upstairs.”

“Probably not used to slumming it on a couch or the floor.” Rio said, moving to sit on one of the sofas. 

Agatha placed her bag under the window and stared out at the water. The lake was right in their backyard. She could see a long pier and other people already out in boats or swimming along the shore. “And you’re used to ‘slumming’ it?” 

“I grew up in a house with three bedrooms and ten people. So, you could say that, yeah.”

“How did you all fit? Agatha turned and sat on the other sofa to Rio’s left. She sunk deep into the cushions and tried to adjust herself. They both held in their laughter as she awkwardly maneuvered herself into a normal sitting position, fighting the flowery design that seemed intent on sucking her in. 

“You good?” Rio asked and Agatha shot her a thumbs up. “I shared with my three youngest cousins, and then as the oldest moved out, we’d shuffle. I usually just spent the summers sleeping on the screened-in back porch.”

“You slept outside?” Agatha was born and raised in the north east. She could only imagine freezing to death in the backyard. 

“Have you been to Florida? The humidity is punishing. At least outside, you can pretend you feel a breeze or pray for the afternoon storms to cool you down.” 

“We had spring training in Cuba last year. It wasn’t brutally hot, but I don’t know how you could live in that humidity. It was suffocating!” Agatha held her throat, pretending to choke. 

“Cuba? No kidding. I’ve always wanted to go - how’d the league get permission for that?” Rio asked curiously. 

“They have their own women’s league, so we played them in a few friendly matches. The Brooklyn Dodgers were touring the island at the same time, so the league tagged along. I think our games drew a bigger crowd. Those Cubans love baseball!” 

They heard shoes clomping down the steps then Dottie appeared, “We’re setting up the volleyball net, do y’all want to join?” 

They looked at each other and pushed up off the couches. There was a sliding door that led right into the backyard. 

Peggy was stringing up the net, while Lupe and Flo were hitting the volleyball back and forth. Wanda was slathering sunscreen on a whining Billy, and Helen was cracking open a Coke. 

“We’ve got two more takers!” Dottie ran over to help Peggy. 

Horsey walked over, “Schoolyard pick, line up. I’ll be one captain, Wanda you’re the other.”

They got into a line facing the two appointed captains. Wanda flipped a nickel and earned first choice. 

“Agatha.” She chose to nobody’s surprise. The catcher swaggered over and waved at the poor unchosen. 

“Fine, Rio.” Horsey didn’t want those two on the same team. Rio joined her side. 

“Dottie.”

“Peggy.”

“Lupe.” Wanda pointed to the pitcher. 

“Come on over, Flo.” Horsey called to the last girl standing. They had their teams, four on four.
