“Medical research. I’ve worked at the CDC in Atlanta for the past five years on the development of vaccines. There’s always more to learn though.” 

“Far out. That’s a long way from baseball. Do you talk to anyone from the ball club?”

Rio shook her head, “No. Haven’t heard from anyone since. I played one more season after your mom retired. But then, I moved back to Florida and enrolled in school.”

“So you haven’t heard from Agatha?” William thought of Agatha a lot. She was the only one not in the team photograph, but she stuck out the most in his mind. 

Rio spilled her coffee all over her shirt, “Damn!” She frantically tried to dab at it with a napkin. 

“Here, take mine.” He passed over his cloth napkin as she swiped up the drops from the table. 

“Sorry, I just…haven’t heard that name in a while.” Her lips turned down in a sad frown. William could hear the melancholy tone in her voice. 

“I used to call her Aunt Aggie. I don’t know what happened. I was just a kid. It felt like losing a relative, and we didn’t have many to begin with. I’m sorry for bringing it up.” 

“No, you didn’t do anything wrong. I’m fine. I haven’t seen her since, umm…it’s been…” Rio looked down at the table in deep thought, “fifteen years.” 

“Didn’t she used to live in Massachusetts?” 

“Salem, yeah.”

“Why don’t we try to find her?” 

“What? Why would we do that?” Rio gave him a look like he was insane. 

“Aren’t you curious?” 

“I’ve moved on. That was a lifetime ago. Look, it was nice to reminisce, but I need to go. I forgot my uh, my notebook in my apartment.” Rio stood abruptly. Her shirt was completely stained, her vest was disheveled. She looked overwhelmed as she made for the door. 

“Wait! Can I get your number?” William didn’t want to lose her now. 

Rio sighed but held out her hand and motioned for a pen. He scrambled into his bag and pulled out a pencil. She scribbled her phone number onto a scrap of paper he had in his pocket. 

“See ya, kid.” She said and was gone. 

—---------

Rio was not okay. It was like someone had turned her world upside down.. She didn’t know how, but she made it back to her apartment in one piece. 

She fell onto her bed and shoved her head into the pillow. 

How could one name set her off? She was 41 years old for god's sake. She was at one of the top colleges in the nation. She had a beautiful, supportive family. She was successful in her career.

She was happy. 

She was meant to be happy. 

One mention of her and I’m a mess all over again. It’s pathetic. She hates me, remember? She’s never reached out. She’s never contacted me. She doesn’t care. I don’t care. 

Rio rolled off the bed and shuffled to the closet. She moved aside her winter clothes and searched the floor. Under two knitted blankets, she finally found her old travelling suitcase. The outside was well-worn, having been tossed around on many a bus trip, but the casing was strong and didn’t have a single dent. She yanked it out by the handle. 

She pulled it on the floor and sat down with her back against her hope chest at the end of the bed. She clicked the latches and opened it for the first time in years. 

Her old cap was right on top. The green hat was like an old friend. Her uniform was folded beneath it. She could see all the stitches and repairs from the two years of wear. Her leather mitt looked lonely at the bottom. She put it on and opened and closed her hand, relishing the familiar feeling. 

I miss baseball. I forgot how much I missed it.

The adrenaline rush was unlike anything else since. The pressure was everything. Rio punched into her glove and remembered the thrill of pitching in front of a crowd of thousands. She didn’t think anything else would ever compare. 

The bottom of the case was littered with newspaper clippings about the Sallies and the Comets - their championship win in her rookie year, her perfect game, the play-off run in her second season. She dug through the unorganized mess in search of the thing she was trying to find. 

Under a pennant flag, she found it. She pulled it out like a treasure hunter carefully extracting a chunk of gold. 

It was just as beautiful as she remembered. 

The photo of Agatha from LIFE magazine that she used to keep in her locker. The photo that had inspired it all. It was marked with folds and sun damaged, but her face was as stunning as ever. Rio ran her thumb along the cheek of the young Agatha. 

Rio had dated a few others over the years. It’s not like she had held a candle for her short-lived fling. There was an unfortunate obsession with an older, brunette professor in undergrad at the University of Florida that amounted to nothing but a crush. There was a drunken fling with her roommate senior year, which ended in the cold shoulder for the rest of the semester. Ouch. 

There was a year-long something with Gloria, the hot statistician in the public health data department at work. It had been good until Gloria agreed to marry the boring engineer that promised her a backyard and a life that Rio could never provide. 

“I thought you had class all day?” Alice popped her head in. The law student was the best roommate - she mostly kept to herself and had a rad record collection. “Whoa, are you alright?”

Rio was sprawled on the floor, staring at the old photo of her long-lost lover. She was far from alright. 

Alice stepped into the room, “What is all this stuff?” She picked up a photo of Rio pitching on the Sallies. 

“I used to play professional baseball in the ‘40s.” Rio rocked up to her knees and stood up. She started packing everything back up. Hidden where it belonged. 

“What the hell? You never told me that! I didn’t know women played pro ball.” 

“The league only lasted nine years. It was during the war when the baseball owners lost all their best players to the draft. It took a global tragedy for them to finally take a chance on women’s sports.” Rio pocketed the photo of Agatha and piled the rest of the things in the suitcase. 

“Were you any good?”

Rio smiled, “Yeah, I was.” She snapped the case closed. 

Her life kept on rolling as it had been. The encounter with William had been a small detour, but she was back on track. She continued her research, attending her classes, and absolutely not admiring the photo of a certain someone every chance she got. 

It was about two weeks later that her phone rang on a Friday night. 

“This is Rio.”

“We found her!” 

“Who is this?” She asked. 

“It’s William, of course. And Eddie, my boyf…my friend.” He stuttered over that last word. 

“What are you talking about?”

“Agatha! I’ve been searching ever since we talked and Eddie’s been helping me. It’s been really fun.”

“Shouldn’t you be studying or…wait, it’s Friday night, should you be out or something and not playing private investigator?” Looking at a picture was one thing. Rio didn’t feel ready to see the real Agatha. 

“Don’t you want to hear what we found?”

Rio chewed the nail of her thumb. Before she could regret it she said, “Fine, out with it.”

“Okay, well the only thing I knew was her name and that she lived in Salem at some point. So last weekend we drove up there. It’s only 40 minutes away. First, we checked the phone book, but there was nobody listed under Harkness. Eddie suggested that maybe she got married.”

Rio felt her stomach drop at the thought. Of course she would get married. She was the most beautiful woman Rio had ever known. There’s no chance that she was still single. 

William continued, “So we went to the Salem Library and trawled the newspaper records for wedding announcements. Nothing! We must have stared at those papers for hours.”

Rio released a heavy breath. Why did she care at all? Why did her body tense up at the thought of Agatha marrying someone?

“Eventually, we were going to throw in the towel. But, I decided to ask the librarian if she knew anyone with the last name Harkness. Salem is a pretty small town. She seemed to shiver at the mention, and then said ‘Good riddance’ and wouldn’t elaborate. Eddie checked the obituaries then and found a small mention in 1949 - the sudden death of an Evanora Harkness, survived by one daughter, Agatha.” William said. 

So her mother died. That might explain why she didn’t come back to play the next season, but where was she now? 

Rio waited but couldn’t hear anything on the other end of the line, “Well, what happened to her?”

“I asked the librarian if she knew Agatha, but she said Agatha had left town after the funeral. Guess where she thought she moved?” He paused for dramatic effect. 

“I’m not in a guessing mood, kid.”

“Boston!”

“Are you serious?” Rio felt sick. She’d lived in Cambridge for a year, and Agatha was twenty minutes away the entire time?? She would be lying if she said Radcliffe’s location in Massachusetts hadn’t played a small (or massive) role in her choice of university. 

“I checked the phonebook, and there is only one A.Harkness listed. 18 Fairfield Street in the Back Bay neighborhood. Do you want to come with us tomorrow?”

Her heart felt like it was ripping the skin of her chest to scream out “YES” but her head was infuriatingly more logical and realistic. Agatha had made her feelings crystal clear all those years ago. She wanted nothing to do with Rio then - why would she want to now?

“Billy, sorry William, I don’t think this is a good idea. You were too young to remember, but things didn’t end well for Agatha in the league. She probably doesn’t want to be reminded of that time. I think you should let this go.”

“But…”

“Listen, you can talk to me about any questions you have, but let Agatha live her life. Trust me.” Rio tried to convey her seriousness. 

He paused in frustration, “Fine, if you insist. Can we have coffee again soon?”

“Sure. Go enjoy your Friday night.” She hung up and knew immediately she wasn’t going to sleep well. 

Rio tossed and turned in bed. The sounds of Peter, Paul, and Mary blared from the record player in the living room as Alice entertained a selection of other stressed law students. She would normally mingle, but now her thoughts were an avalanche of Agatha. 

She travelled deep into the cages she kept in the back of her mind. The memories that carried the warning “Be careful - will bite.” She unlocked the one filled with yearning and pure, intense heartache. 

And then, she was crying. 

Time hadn’t softened this wound as she’d simply pushed the hurt so far down into her subconscious that she hadn’t processed a moment. 

Rio’s eyes burned as she remembered their whirlwind meeting, their rivalry, their first and last kiss, the unexpected end to it all. Her nose started to run embarrassingly as the tears stained her pillow. She was too old to be crying over an ex. She wasn’t even an ex! They weren’t girlfriends, they weren’t married.

Agatha was like a camera flash - she burned as bright as the sun and disappeared with the blink of an eye.  

The clock on the wall ticked past midnight. Rio was meant to work on her dissertation this weekend. After another fifteen minutes, she gave up and clicked on the bedside lamp. She knew who she needed to call. 

“Hello?”

“Jen, I can’t sleep.” Rio muttered through the phone. 

“Rio? It’s midnight, you’re so lucky that I’m working the night shift this week.” Jen was a midwife at a large hospital in Brooklyn. She’d moved out of Florida after finishing her medical training and was thriving in the big city. 

“I knew you’d be awake. I need your sage advice.” 

“What woman is terrorizing you now?”

“Wh-what are you talking about?” Rio sputtered. 

“Come on, you only call me this late when you have girl problems.” Jen laughed at the obvious observation. She’d counselled Rio through a conveyor belt of beautiful but ill-suited suitors. 

“Oh, I guess you’re right. Well, at least this isn’t a new one…”

“Who? Please don’t say Beverley. She was such an evil bit-”

Rio cut her off and blurted out, “Agatha.”

“No.”

“Yes.”

“Nooooo. Rio, not again. That girl broke your damn heart into pieces. No, she didn’t just break it. She ripped it out, stomped on it with her spikes, tossed it in the air, smacked it with her bat, and sent it over the outfield fences.” Jen certainly knew how to paint a pretty picture. 

“She’s in Boston.”

“How do you know that?”

“Uhhh, long story.” Rio didn’t have the energy to recount the Billy story. 

“Have you seen her?” Jen asked. 

“No…”

“Then don’t. She left. She was cruel. She never came looking for you, did she?” Jen remembered all too well when Agatha had broken Rio’s heart. The Comets won the championship, but it didn’t even register for Rio. Jen was the one who wiped her tears, the one who helped pack up her bags, who rode with her back home, who nursed her back to life after weeks of catatonia at her aunt and uncle's home. 

If nothing else, it showed Jen how much she enjoyed taking care of others and inspired her career change. But it had been awful to watch Rio struggle to regain her confidence and feel worthy of love again. She wasn’t actually sure if her friend had ever fully recovered. She seemed to push every potential lover away as soon as things got too serious. 

There are some heartbreaks that never leave you. 

“What if I just go see her once?” Rio bargained. She couldn’t get the idea out of her head. Agatha was so close. It seemed crazy not to go see her. 

“Rio, we’re in our 40’s. You don’t need my permission, you know?”

“So it’s not a terrible idea?”

“I didn’t say that. I personally think it would be catastrophically awful, but I know you. If you’ve gotten it in your obsessive little head that you need to do this, then you need to do it. Can you promise me something though?” Jen asked. 

“Depends.”

“Promise me you won’t get your hopes up. She might not even want to see you. She might flat out refuse. She might throw a drink in your face. She might yell or scream or tell you to fuck off.” Jen knew she’d be the one to deal with the fall out. 

“I know. I don’t think she’ll want to pick things up again with me. She’s probably dating some guy named Greg and is the head of her neighborhood watch or some other domestic bullshit.” Rio had no clue what Agatha could possibly be doing now. She could only imagine the hardcore, take-no-prisoners catcher from her memories. 

“Well, I expect a phone call update as soon as possible.” Jen said. 

“Of course. Goodnight, Jen. Thanks as always.” Rio hung up. Her research could wait. 

She was going to Boston tomorrow.

Chapter End Notes

Welcome to the '60s!

Chapter 20: Caught in a pickle

“Just about the time I was ready to be a good strong veteran for four or five more years, four or five more years weren’t there.”

Mary “Wimp” Baumgartner, catcher, 1949–54

Morning crept up on her, and Rio rolled out of bed with a groan. She glanced at herself in the mirror and cringed. She looked even more tired than she felt - not exactly the way she wanted to present herself. 

“Alice!!” She raised her voice and heard her roommate walk over from the dining room. 

“What?”

“Can you help me with my makeup?” 

Alice leapt into the air in the doorway, “I thought you’d never ask! Come to the bathroom, I’ll get my stuff.”

Half an hour and an extensive amount of eyeliner later, Rio admired the final product. Alice had a real artist’s eye, and Rio looked way cooler than she felt. 

“What’s the occasion?” Alice asked. Rio rarely cared much about stuff like this. She’d never seen her roommate go out on a date or bring anyone home. 

Rio fussed with her bangs, “Seeing an old friend.”

“An old flame?” Alice teased. 

“Things didn’t end on a good note.”

“Ohhhh, so you want to show him what he missed out on? Let me pick your outfit.” Alice walked to her closet and searched the rack. She loved to collect coats and jackets. 

“No, it’s not like that. I just want to look…confident.” 

“What about this?” Alice held up a dark red sleeveless sheath dress. 

The corners of Rio’s mouth curved up on sight. She nodded. 

“I know what would look killer with this.” Alice flipped through her clothes until she landed on what she was looking for. 

She pulled out the black leather greaser jacket. Rio had always eyed the piece with envy. She put it on and loved the feeling immediately. She felt strong. “Thanks, Alice.”

“This guy would be crazy not to come crawling back once he sees you.” Alice ogled her friend. 

Rio wasn’t about to explain that it wasn’t a man she was going to see, “Can I borrow your car?”

Alice tossed her the keys with a smile, “Go get’em, tiger.”

Rio checked the map a few times and plotted the course to Back Bay. There wasn’t much traffic this early in the morning. The city sang with early risers and the promise of a new day. Rio bathed in the sunrise and drove with the windows down. 

If nothing else, I just want to see her again. Even if she doesn’t want to talk to me, I just want to see her face one more time. 

She hadn’t planned what she was going to say or what she even wanted out of this. All she wanted was some indication that Agatha existed. That she hadn’t imagined it all. She had disappeared from her life in a snap. Rio wanted some closure. 

The tree-lined streets felt like something from a different time. The buildings were all historic, built in the 19th century, and incredibly well-preserved.

The car pulled up in front of the address that William had given - 18 Fairfield Road. Rio checked the number twice. This couldn’t be right, could it? The brick townhouse looked like a house a Kennedy would live in, not a single woman from Salem. 

This was a mistake. She’s married. Definitely married. What was I thinking?

Rio parked along the curb and sat in the car staring out the window at the house. She couldn’t do this. She imagined ringing the door and some guy opening it. What would she say? How would she explain who she was and how she knew Agatha? 

She sighed and shook her head. I should just go back to school. I don’t need this stress. She turned the keys in the ignition and felt the engine stutter to life again. As she began to shift into drive, a man and woman walked right past the car and up the steps to the house that supposedly belonged to Agatha. 

Rio paused. She recognized that face, even with the grey streaks in her hair. She unclipped her seatbelt and jumped out of the car. 

“Peggy?” She called out. 

The woman on the steps turned right before knocking on the door. She looked down to the street and shaded her eyes to get a better view. It took a moment. 

“Rio?” Peggy skipped down and rushed over to her old teammate. “Is that you?”

“It’s me. What are you doing here?”

“What are you doing here?” Peggy’s eyes flicked up to the house. The man she was with was casually leaning against the iron railing, watching with interest. He was holding a bouquet of beautiful wildflowers. 

“Ummm, I don’t know. I thought this was Agatha’s house.”

“I think you should go.” Peggy said firmly. 

“Is this her house?”

“I don’t think she would want to see you. I’m sorry. I don’t know why you’re here. I don’t know what you want, but she’s finally in a good place. She’s finally doing well. I don’t want anything to mess that up.”

“I…I…” Rio stuttered. She was so close. She had waited years for this. 

They heard conversation behind them. A man’s voice and a woman’s greeting. They exchanged a few words and then a voice hollered towards them. 

“Peg! What are you-” 

Crash

The sound of glass shattering made everyone on the street turn. Agatha was standing in her doorway. The vase she was holding was now a broken collection of glass at her feet, the flowers lying in wake down the steps. 

Rio peered behind Peggy and saw Agatha staring straight at her. Their eyes locked. She felt like a wild bull being lassoed by a cowboy. Agatha’s eyes were reining her in and constricting around her, cutting off her breathing, cutting off all other thoughts.  

“Hey, butterfingers.” Rio smirked and waved. 

Agatha was frozen. Her foot was bleeding. Glass was covering her entire landing stoop. And she didn’t care. She couldn’t care about anything. 

Rio was here. 

“ARRHGHHH!!” Agatha flew down to the street ready to pounce. Her arm was raised to strike before Peggy held her back. 

“NO! Agatha calm down!” Peggy shouted, wrapping her arms around Agatha to steady her. 

“Why are you here?!” She spat, almost screaming in the street, forgetting all semblance of propriety. 

“Maybe we should go inside…” Peggy suggested as the neighbors started to whisper and stare. 

“I don’t want her in my house.” Agatha shook her head. 

They were at an impasse. Everyone stared at each other. Rio was the first to break the silence, “I’ll go. I’m sorry.” 

Agatha enjoyed the hollow victory. She didn’t say goodbye. She turned and asked Steve to help her back into the house. She went to grab the broom from the hallway closet. As she came back out, she saw Peggy still talking to the unwelcome visitor. 

Eventually, the car started, and Rio was gone. Agatha finally released the breath that sat in her lungs. How could she still harbor this anger after all this time? How could she still affect her like this?

Peggy walked up and grabbed the dustpan. She held it as Agatha swept the pieces up. Steve gathered the flowers. 

“Do you want to-” Peggy began to speak but Agatha made a noise of protest. 

“Let’s just go to breakfast like we planned.” She sighed and went to find another vase inside. 

At the Parisian Coffee House, they sat at their regular table near the pastry case. Agatha was uncharacteristically quiet. 

A waitress poured some coffee in each of their mugs. 

“Okay come on, tell me you’re not at least a little curious?” Peggy threw her hands up. She couldn’t believe Agatha was acting like this. 

“I don’t want to talk about her.”

“It’s been fifteen years!” 

“And?” Agatha couldn’t process what was happening. 

“She’s studying at Radcliffe. I always knew she was athletic, but she’s got brains too? Clearly God has favorites.”

Radcliffe. She lives minutes away. Minutes. 

“Wasn’t she the pitcher on the Comets?” Steve asked innocently - feeling lost in the subtext. 

“Can we talk about literally anything else?” Agatha dove into the menu despite knowing she was just going to order her usual - pancake stack with a side of hash browns. The coveted carbs on carbs way to start the day. 

“No way! I’ve let you ignore this part of our past for long enough. Time to grow up.” Peggy said. 

Agatha let her head drop to the table dramatically. 

“Ready to order?” The waitress followed the unwritten service rule - always show up at the most inconvenient time possible. 

They placed their breakfast orders and sat in an awkward silence as Peggy stared at Agatha, waiting for her friend to give in. 

Steve busied himself stacking the sugar packets on the table. He had been around these two long enough to know not to get involved. 

“FINE!” Agatha finally broke. 

Peggy grinned devilishly, “She gave me her number. I think she just genuinely wants to talk to you. She seemed just as shocked to see you, like she didn’t expect it.”

“What could we possibly have to talk about? She broke whatever we had, and I have no desire to reminisce or relive the good ol’ days with her.”

“She looked good though, right?” Peggy knew that would get a reaction. 

Agatha felt heat start to rise to her face. She wasn’t blind. Rio was somehow more gorgeous today than she was over a decade ago. It really wasn’t fair. She blurted out, “Stop.”

“You know that the coach would have pulled you from the game anyway. You can’t keep holding Rio responsible for that one decision. Any other pitcher would have made that same call.” Peggy explained.

Agatha couldn’t untie her feelings for Rio, the pain of the injury, the hurt of the betrayal, and the end of her baseball career. It was a jumbled spaghetti mess of steaming hot emotions. There was no rationality or logic. It was chaotic, temperamental dynamite. 

“I know that. I can know that’s true and still have trouble accepting it.” Agatha said. 

“Maybe it’d help to meet up with her. You don’t owe her anything, but I think you owe yourself something.” 

“If I say yes, will you shut up about this?”

“Yes.” Peggy handed over the ten digit number smugly. 

“No promises. I probably scared her away today anyway. Now, tell me how the kids are doing.”

Agatha let Rio’s number decay on the kitchen counter. 

She eyed it when she cooked dinner that night. She monitored it when she got a glass of water before bed. It haunted her in the morning when she heated up some oatmeal for breakfast. She finally had to move it under the fruit bowl during lunch as she sat at the dining room table doing some essay grading. 

The house was suffocating. For the past few months, the walls felt like they were too tall, too foreign. She hated the emptiness, the unending emptiness. She’d run away from this before, and the urge to up and run still pulsed through her veins. 

Agatha pushed the fruit bowl aside and yanked the number out. She stomped to the phone in the living room and dialled her one-time enemy / part-time lover. 

“Rio, speaking.”

That voice slashed through her defenses. She felt like a lovesick ballplayer again, “Hey.”

“Agatha.” She spoke her name like a devotion. 

“Rio.”

“I didn’t think you’d call.”

“Neither did I.”

They didn’t speak for a few seconds, content to just listen to the other’s breathing alone. Rio’s curiosity got the better of her, “How have you been?”

Agatha laughed at the absurdity of the question. How could she capture fifteen years of her life in a single response? She went with the stock standard, “Good, how about you?”

Rio chuckled too. They were playing pretend. “Good. I missed you.”

“I hated you.” Agatha didn’t want to keep up the facade anymore. 

“Past tense?”

“Huh?”

“You don’t hate me anymore?”

“I don’t know you anymore.”

“You know me.” Rio asserted. 

“You don’t know me.” Agatha confessed. A lot had changed. She wasn’t the same person that had fallen head over heels for a cocky pitcher in the heat of the summer. 

“I want to.” Rio said. 

“Want to what?”

“I want to know you.”

Agatha’s heart thawed slightly - the dripping shards ached in her chest. Rio always had the words that made her melt. She hesitated and murmured,  “What if you don’t like what you find?”

“Let me take you to dinner.” 

“Rio…”

“Please, one dinner.” 

“One dinner. Then you let me go.” Agatha relented. She was already preparing for the inevitable ending. She didn’t have any hopes left to get up. 

“Friday night? Can I pick you up at 6?” 

“Friday at 6. See you then.”

“Bye, Agatha.” The line went dead. 

Work dragged that week. Agatha felt every minute of her classes. The impending date (was it a date?) weighed heavy on her shoulders. She could feel her energy waning by Wednesday. 

“Ms. Harkness? You forgot to pass out the worksheet.” Ann, the class brown noser, raised her hand yet spoke aloud anyway. 

“Oh, sorry. Take one, pass it along.” She handed the stack to the student in the front row and returned to her desk.

I should call Rio and cancel. This was a mistake. Yeah, I’ll call her tonight and cancel. 

She finished the day and went home to change before heading to Peggy’s for dinner. She typically visited her friend’s house at least once a week when she couldn’t be bothered cooking. The Rogers house was only one block over on Exeter Street. 

She walked straight in the door to their 19th century brownstone that was similar to her own. 

“Mom! Aunt Agatha is here!” Sandra yelled from her spot on the couch. 

Peggy popped out from the kitchen, “Hey doll, we’re having pot roast.” 

“Perfect, do you need help?” Agatha asked as she stepped out of her pumps. 

“No, I could just use some company.”

“Well, I’m good for that. Where’s JJ?” Agatha looked around for Peggy’s eldest. 

“He’s out with some of the other boys from the baseball team.”

“Ohh, little bird is flying the nest!” Agatha tried to keep an eye on the boy at school. He was in his first year of high school, and she saw him around the halls. He was small for his age, but it looked like he had inherited his mom’s athletic ability. 

“Don’t remind me. I don’t know how I’m going to deal with him leaving for college. I’m dreading the day already.” Peggy checked the oven, and the smell made Agatha’s mouth water. 

“So…you know how we saw Rio the other day…” Agatha started. 

Peggy snapped to standing and whipped around, “Oh my god, did you call her?”

“Yes. We’re going to dinner on Friday.”

“I would kill to be a fly on the wall for that.” 

“What? Don’t be weird.”

“It’d be even better than the Dick Van Dyke or Andy Griffith Show! Two former rivals, who became…” Peggy mouthed the word ‘lovers’ to avoid her daughter overhearing, “Then, were separated for years and years and are now reuniting. It practically writes itself! I’d watch the hell out of that show.”

“I was going to cancel.” Agatha said, nervously running a hand through her hair. 

“No!! I need this.”

“ You need this?”

“I’m so bored, Agatha. If I have to attend one more parent’s bingo fundraiser, I think I will throw myself into the river. I need some exciting gossip in my life again.”

“You should get a job.”

“Who’s gonna hire a housewife? It’s not like we need the money. Steve makes more than enough.” She was proud of her husband, who was a successful artist. 

“Things are changing, Peg. Women are getting all sorts of jobs these days. One of my student’s mothers works for the police department! Remember when you wanted to be a detective one day?” 

“Really?”

“Why not? You could do anything you want. You’re brilliant.” Agatha knew that Peggy was struggling with a sense of purpose lately. She wanted her to pursue whatever goal she had. 

The timer went off, and Peggy pulled the huge roasting tray out of the oven. It looked divine. 

“Alright. Sandra!” She yelled out, “Set the table, please!”

Then, Peggy placed the roast down and turned to Agatha, “Go help the kid, and then we’ll eat. And you will not under any circumstance cancel your dinner with Rio. Got it?”

Agatha saluted her. Peggy would have made a hell of a drill sergeant in another life. 

Rio let Alice do her makeup again. She went to Filene’s in the city and bought a brand-new outfit. She decided to go with tight grey plaid tapered pants and a complimentary black sleeveless turtleneck sweater.  

“Careful the smoke alarm doesn’t go off because you look flaming hot, Rio!” Alice whistled low as she took in the entire outfit and look. 

“What’s that saying…” Rio began as she twirled in the mirror and admired the view. 

“Dress to impress?” Alice guessed. 

Rio smiled widely. 

She was about to show Agatha what she had missed out on, “No, revenge is a dish best served cold.” 

She pulled up outside Agatha’s house five minutes early in Alice’s sleek black ‘56 Dodge Lancer. The clear sky was painted in reds and pinks as the sun began to set. A light breeze rustled her hair as Rio walked up the steps and knocked on the door. 

The white wooden door swung open. 

There she was. 

Agatha was in front of her. She was a vision in a shirt-waist green striped dress. 

Rio didn’t know how she was more enticing now in her 50s than she had been in her 30s. The fullness of her face had subsided, and her hair was styled in the popular bouffant that most women preferred these days. There were more wrinkles around her eyes, her mouth, her neck. Based on what the magazines said, that should add up to a woman leaving her prime. But despite the sexist beauty standards, Agatha was as radiant as ever. 

“Wow, Agatha. You look…you look so beautiful.” Rio felt starstruck. She forgot every sarcastic joke and cutting barb. All she could remember was why she had fallen for this gal in the first place. Damn it. 

Agatha’s eyes wandered up and down Rio’s body, “How do you still look like this?”

Ah, there was still something there. “Good genetics, I suppose.”

“Still as humble as ever I see.” 

“Some things never change.” Rio offered a hand to help Agatha down the stairs. The older woman ignored it and led the way on her own. 

“Suppose they don’t.” Rio said to herself and hustled to catch up as Agatha was already opening the passenger seat and getting in. 

“Is this your car?” She asked. 

“My roommate’s.”

“Knew it was too nifty for you. Where are you taking me anyway?” Agatha sat her purse in her lap and rolled down the window. 

Rio started the car, “Cantina Italiana. Have you ever been?”

“Can’t say I have.” Agatha looked over and saw Rio staring at her, blatantly admiring her profile. “Enjoying the view?”

“Sorry, yup. Let’s go.” 

“So, um, how long have you been at Radcliffe?” Agatha asked, trying to keep the conversation light as they exited her neighborhood. 

“This is my second year. How long have you lived in Boston?”

“I lived in Salem for a year after my last season. My mom died the following year, and I was stuck dealing with her estate for another year. I got so sick of that town. I sold my house and moved in 1950. So I’ve been here for about 13 years.”

“Sorry about your mom.” Rio said. 

“I’m not. She left this complicated will for me. One final fuck you from beyond the grave. I couldn’t even find peace with her death. It’s like she was still hovering behind me, laughing.” 

“Sorry…” Rio didn’t know what else there was to say. 

“I don’t need your sympathy.” Agatha snapped then shook her head, “I mean, thanks. Sorry, it’s just…strange. Being here with you. After everything that happened.”

“No, I get it.” Rio knew that Agatha probably didn’t even want to be here. 

They arrived at Hanover St, and Rio parallelled parked. The host sat them at a quiet table near the back of the restaurant. It was busy and all the surrounding tables were full of other couples or groups. 

Rio picked up the menu and started scanning the offerings, but Agatha just stared at her, “Rio, what are we doing?”

“Having dinner?”

“Obviously. I mean, what are you doing here? How did you find me?”

Rio folded the menu back up and set it down. Okay, they were jumping straight into the deep end. “Do you remember Billy?”

“You’re gonna have to be a lot more specific.”

“Wanda’s son.”

Agatha’s eyebrows raised in surprise, “Billy and Tommy.”

“Yeah. Well Billy…he goes by William now…goes to Harvard. He recognized me out of nowhere a few weeks ago.”

“No kidding. He’s in college now?”

“He’s 20, if you can believe it. Anyway, he saw me and was so desperate to talk to someone about the league. Apparently, Wanda is very hush hush about it all. He asked about you.” Rio explained. 

“Does he know what happened?”

“No. His memory seems very limited. It’s thanks to him that I'm here at all. He’s the one who went to Salem, found out you had moved to Boston, and then found your address. The kid’s got a one-track mind. I had to beg him not to come surprise you. I decided to do that instead.” Rio smiled and Agatha returned it.

They all have added to supporting girls and women in sports throughout their lifetimes.”

Jeneane DesCombes

It was the fifth game of the year for the Excel High Knights. Agatha had taken over the coaching gig in her third year teaching when the old gym teacher retired and nobody else wanted the job. She didn’t expect much, but coaching these girls was now her favorite part of the year. 

She loved taking nervous freshmen and molding and shaping them until they became killer ballplayers. The girls both loved and feared her in equal measure. Coach Harkness was notorious in the state for her intensity and her results. Excel won the Massachusetts state championship for the past four years in a row, and they’d won two other times with Agatha at the helm. They lost once in ‘58 due to a food poisoning outbreak that struck half the team on the last game of the playoffs. Don’t bring it up to Agatha if you want to live. 

“Hustle Susie! Come on, get your mitt!” Agatha yelled at the second baseman who was running from the parking lot. She was perpetually late and arrived as the team was already warming up in the field prior to the game. 

“Alright, toss the ball around to get your arms warm. We’re up against Brighton today. They’re about as dangerous as a field mouse, so I expect big things in terms of score. If I don’t see at least five runs on that board, you better hope and pray that Friday practice gets cancelled.” Agatha addressed her team. She wasn’t above using threats. 

The coach checked her clipboard with the roster and batting order. She was so busy re-ordering the starting lineup that she didn’t notice someone gliding right in beside her. 

“Do you think you could use that ‘coach’ voice in the bedroom?” 

Agatha gasped and saw Rio smiling next to her, looking confident in a pair of long shorts, blouse, and cardigan. 

“You came.” She opened her arms and hugged her tightly. 

“Of course. I’ll always come when you ask.” Rio stepped back and looked out at the practicing players. “They’re good. I can tell you’ve taught them well.”

“I don’t think they could stand a chance against the Comets back in the day, but they’re not bad for a group of wily teens.” 

“At least they don’t have to play in skirts.” Rio joked. 

“I fought hard to make that true. You should have seen the stink I kicked up at the school board meeting when they tried to implement dress uniforms.” Agatha wasn’t going to make young girls have to slide on skin like she had to. 

“I can only imagine.”

“Do you want to meet them? Can you still pitch?” Agatha eyed her arm. 

“It’s been a while…a long while. I don’t think I have that fast heater anymore, but I could probably still hurl a good curve. Do you have a mitt I could borrow?” 

“Jane, come here! Give me your glove.” Agatha caught it as the girl tossed it over. Then whistled the rest of the team over. “Alright, get your bats. We’ve got a very special guest today. This is Rio Vidal. We used to play professional ball together back in the day. She had the finest arm in the league and threw a perfect game once. She’s agreed to throw batting practice for you, and you’ll thank her for it. She only throws overhand, but it’ll be good practice for you to work on your bat speed, timing, and identifying different pitches. Get ready!” 

Rio put on the mitt. Design had come a long way from the big, beefy gloves they used to wield a decade ago. The new mitts were much lighter and streamlined. She flexed it open and closed. Agatha passed her a softball. It was way bigger than the ball she was accustomed to, but she could still get a decent grip on it. 

“Will you catch for me? Like old times?” Rio asked, giving her best puppy dog eyes to the coach. 

Agatha pursed her lips and considered it, “My knees are not what they used to be.”

Rio leaned in, whispered and patted her shoulder, “It’ll be good practice.”

Agatha pulled at her collar as she felt sudden heat crawling up her neck at the flashback to what happened in that yacht club office a few days ago, “I’m going to send you my doctor’s bill!”

“Go get your mask and gear on.” Rio ordered and walked to the pitcher’s mound. She hadn’t been on a field since she retired from the league. The red dirt welcomed her like an old friend. The teenage players gathered in their dugout and lined the fence to watch. Agatha eventually jogged out in the catcher’s gear, which didn’t quite fit right but would protect her well enough. 

Before dropping behind the plate, she called out to her starting pitcher, “Donna, watch closely. Rio’s got a unique wind up.”

Rio took a big breath in and put her hands together in the mitt, lifted them above her head, kicked her leg out (ignoring the tight stretch in her hamstring), leaned back, and threw a down the middle fastball. She took a ton of speed off it to allow her arm time to readjust to pitching, but the placement was still as perfect as ever. 

The chattering girls were stunned and silenced as the ball smacked Agatha’s mitt. Rio felt that exhilarating energy that only comes from pitching in front of a crowd, even the opposing team stopped their practice to turn and watch. She threw a few more pitches, before Agatha called over the first batter. 

“Remember Joyce. Eye on the ball. Rio’s not going to hold back, so if you’re going to swing, commit early.” Agatha advised the batter. She looked small and nervous from Rio’s point of view. She’d normally eat batters like this for breakfast but knew that wasn’t the point of this exercise. 

Agatha dropped a two - sign for a curve ball. Rio adjusted her grip and threw a sweeping curve that came in high and then dropped. Joyce swung and missed. She was close though. Agatha called for the same pitch. Rio took some power off it and threw the curve. The bat connected and sent the ball bouncing towards third base. The team cheered for their friend, who was grinning with all her teeth. 

Agatha pushed her mask up and walked out to Rio, “Don’t steal her struggle.”

“What?” Rio grabbed another ball from the bucket beside her. 

“I know you let her have that one. Don’t go easy on them. They’ll never get better if you hold back.”

“Look at her. She’s so happy.” Rio pointed to the girl who was still smiling as she sat on the bench. 

“Imagine how much happier she’d be if she’d really hit one off you.” 

“I thought this was batting practice. You’re a real hard ass, you know?” 

“I’m aware. Now pitch like I know you can.” Agatha said before heading back to home plate. Rio felt like they were back in time. It was all so surreal being back on a diamond with Agatha. 

The next girl put on her helmet and came out. She was taller and had the aggression that makes for a talented athlete. Rio’s eyes connected with Agatha’s blues. That unspoken understanding was still fresh between them. Agatha dropped a single finger for a fastball. 

Rio felt like her arm was warm. She wound up and fired. 

The batter didn’t even swing. Rio saw her eyes widen and by the time her brain told her body to move, the ball was already safe in Agatha’s mitt. 

“How am I meant to hit that?” Vicky asked, still in shock. 

Agatha threw the ball back to Rio, “Swing earlier.” 

Rio threw two more fastballs and Vicky still couldn’t get a piece. Agatha called for a changeup instead so as not to wear out the pitcher too quickly. Vicky waited on it and sent it flying to the outfield. 

The rest of the game lineup took their turns. Only two hitters were able to get a piece of Rio’s fastball. Agatha was right though. Rio saw the immense sense of satisfaction in the batters and the way their team celebrated their well-earned success. 

“Great batting practice, team. Say thank you to Rio.” Agatha said. 

“Thank you, Rio!” They all chanted at her. Rio smiled shyly. She wasn’t used to working with young people and being on the receiving end of their gratitude. 

“Game starts in ten minutes, so run to the bathroom or the water fountain if you need to. We’re not wasting a timeout again!” Rio watched as Agatha shot daggers at a brunette standing near the back, who was suddenly looking very interested in the clouds. “Go stretch!” The girls dispersed leaving Agatha and Rio standing outside the dugout. 

“They should have frozen your arm in time. It belongs in a hall of fame, not on some research scientist sitting in a lab.” Agatha said. 

“Hall of Fame? As if, they’ll ever let a couple of dames in one of those! I’m glad you value throwing a fastball over life-saving vaccines.” Rio snarked back. 

“Both are important. Baseball saved my life more than once.” 

“It saved mine too.” Rio agreed. She couldn’t imagine where she would be if she hadn’t earned the money for college during those two years playing ball. She would probably be stuck at the docks or married to some chump from her hometown. 

“Are you going to stick around? I could use a first base coach. Normally, one of the girls stands in.” Agatha asked, trying not to get her hopes up. 

“Sure, it could be fun.”

Rio helped the base runners with her directions and encouragement. She gave tips to the batters when she was in the dugout. She advised the pitchers on technique and strategy. It felt good to be back in the sport, like she had never left. 

Most of all, it felt good to be around Agatha again. The coach was a machine on the field. She inspired something in the players like nothing Rio had ever seen. She was tough when she needed to be, yet she had this unshakeable softness with all of them. It was obvious she cared so deeply for each and every player. 

Her competitive drive was like a tornado - it pulled in all those around her. Rio couldn’t stop watching her throughout the entire game. She was more attracted to Agatha in her track pants, t-shirt, and ballcap than she was in her finest dress. She couldn’t stop her heart pounding when she watched Agatha lay into the cowering ump who made a questionable call, or scream for joy at a no-doubt homer. 

She was perfect. 

The game was a blowout. Agatha’s team was miles better than their competitor, and the game ended in the fifth inning due to the mercy rule. The Knights were winning by 12 runs, so the umpires called it there. No need to embarrass the other team further. 

“Do you want to get a drink?” Agatha slung her equipment bag over her shoulder. “The game finished so much earlier than I thought.” It was only 7. 

Rio nodded, “I’ll follow you.”

Agatha drove to The Hidden Gate, a local upscale bar near the college campus. They grabbed a booth and Rio ordered two beers. She carried the glasses over to the table and set them down carefully. 

“Thanks again. The girls were so impressed with you. The pitchers wouldn’t shut up about it. You really made an impression.” Agatha held up her beer and Rio clinked her glass before taking a drink. 

“Thanks for inviting me. I liked seeing this side of you. You’re the same.”

“I’d like to think I’m a little different, a little better.”

Rio smiled to herself, “You are different, but you still have that diseased need to win at all costs.”

Agatha shrugged, “These girls don’t have a lot of opportunities to compete in their lives. There is only one softball team and one volleyball team at my school. There aren’t enough girls for any other sports. So I try to make sure they succeed.”

“More proof that Agatha Harkness has feelings.” 

“Hey, don’t go spreading that around. I’ve got a reputation around here.” Agatha playfully glanced around her shoulders. 

They each took a drink of their beers and simply stared at each other. Both were unsure where they wanted this conversation to go. Their relationship was in such a peaceful state, yet to Rio it felt more like balancing on the edge of a cliff. 

She decided to take a leap of faith. She didn’t want to keep laying awake for another night wondering, “Do you want to be with me?”

Agatha’s eyebrows shot up at the boldness of the question. There was no misinterpreting this. “Rio…”

“Don’t tell me you can’t feel…whatever this is between us. That you didn’t enjoy yourself the other day. I need to know.” Rio pleaded. 

“Nothing’s changed. In the world, I mean. It’s just as taboo to be a lesbian today as it ever was. Most people still view it as a mental disorder! They still arrest people in some states.”

“We will always be persecuted and demonized, but I’d rather hide in the shadows with you than be in the sunlight alone.” Rio didn’t want to watch Agatha slip through her fingers again. Being with her on the field again had just solidified all her wants. She knew she couldn’t live another way.  

“I could lose my job. I could be run out of the neighborhood. I could be sent away for all I know. And then what? Would you still want to be with me in the loony bin?”

“I want to be with you in any way and any reality that you’d take me. I am going to want you until the day I die. I swear, Agatha. I only want you.” 

Agatha opened and closed her mouth. She was so blown away by the declaration. 

“Just let me fucking love you.” Rio added with a smirk. 

That got a laugh out of Agatha, “You drive me mad. You make me want to rip my hair out and rip my clothes off. It’s deranged! When I look at you, I want to fight you and then fuck you. What have you done to me? What spell have you trapped me in?”

“I think they call it love, sweetheart.”

“It’s been too long. You still have time.”

“Time for what?” Rio asked. 

“Time to find someone better!”

“I’ve already found you.” 

Agatha started to speak, but Rio put a hand up. “Just…don’t respond. I know I’ve sprung this on you. I know it’s not fair. I don’t want you to say something just because you think I want to hear it. Take some time. Think about this, then come find me. Okay?”

Agatha agreed, and they finished their drinks and parted ways for the night. 

As she drove home to Back Bay, her every thought echoed with shades of Rio. She couldn’t escape her grasp, and the more she thought about it, the more she realized…she didn’t want to. 

Rio spent the weekend in her room, at her desk, endlessly reading the latest medical journals. It was the only thing that kept her mind busy enough to keep out other thoughts. 

A knock at her door, “Rio? Are you alive in there?” Alice peeked her head in. She hadn’t seen her roommate come out in two days. 

Rio looked up. Her eyes were bloodshot, her hand was cramped from taking notes, her hair a complete mess. “I’m something.”

“God, let some light in here at least.” She crossed the space and yanked open the blinds. She threw the comforter over the bed and sat down. “What’s going on? You’re a hermit, but not normally like this.”

Rio stood up and stretched. Her back felt tight from sitting for so long. She hadn’t heard from Agatha. She was kicking herself for getting her hopes up yet again. What’s that saying about the definition of insanity - she couldn’t be bothered to remember.  

“I’m fine. I need to finish this reading before Monday.” 

“So the fact that you’re avoiding all human interaction after recently meeting your old flame is just a coincidence?” Alice guessed. 

Rio slumped back into her chair and sighed her heavy feelings out, “I thought this was meant to get easier as we get older.”

“Why would you think that? The older we get, the harder it is to deal with other people’s bullshit. When you find someone you want to spend your time with, it’s hard to let that go.”

“I just thought I’d handle this better. Yet here I am - the same pitiful gal, waiting by the phone for someone who won’t call.” Rio said. 

“I’m sure she will.”

Rio’s breath hitched at the pointed pronoun. “Wait, you knew?”

Alice laughed, “Come on, mack. You have a framed photo of Judy Garland on your wall. Your entire record collection is full of beautiful women. You’ve never so much as made a pass at any guy or talked about liking any of my objectively good-looking single male friends. It doesn’t take a genius like you to deduce it. Seeing that you were a pro ballplayer was just the cherry on top.”

“Hey, not all ballplayers are queer.”

“Sure, sure. And not all women who dye their hair with bright colors are lesbians…but some are.” Alice twirled her hair that she’d recently added bright red streaks. 

“You?” Rio pointed at her roommate. She’d known the girl for two years now and never guessed. 

“I dabble. Men, women. It’s so limiting to deny all of life’s pleasures.” 

“Aren’t you full of surprises? Do you have any sage wisdom on dealing with a repressed, older woman that is afraid to date you?” 

Alice hummed and looked at the ceiling as she considered the complicated question, “You have to let her come to you. Otherwise, you’ll be chasing her for your entire life.”

As she was contemplating how to chase Agatha through eternity, Rio’s phone rang. They both locked eyes in surprise. It couldn’t be…

“Hello?” Rio picked up the receiver. Alice was staring in rapt attention. 

“It’s me.” 

Rio gave a huge thumbs up to Alice, who was now jumping on the bed in excitement. “What are you up to?” Rio asked. 

“I’ve been thinking about you.” Agatha admitted. 

“Yeah, me too.” Rio waved Alice to get out of her room, much to her chagrin. 

“Sorry for the wait. I wanted to see if you were busy next weekend. I’m hosting a little get together for Peggy’s birthday. Nothing fancy, no need to bring a gift, just an appetizer to share. I’m making roast chicken.”

“On Saturday or Sunday?”

“Saturday for dinner.” Agatha responded. 

“Sure, will it be all your hoity-toity neighbors or will there be some normal people there too?”

“Excuse you, there will be a lot of teachers, artists, all sorts. You won’t be the only poor.”

“Wow, thanks. You’re a real charmer.” Rio laughed at Agatha’s blunt humor. 

“It’s what drew you to me isn’t it?”

“I thought it was the way your legs looked in that catcher’s position but…”

“Rio! You dirty dog.” Agatha cackled at the boldness that sometimes caught her off guard. “So I take it you’re in? You’ll be there?”

“Yes, ma’am. What should I wear?”

“Whatever you feel good in. We’ll be eating, dancing, and drinking.”

“Okay, sounds good. I’ll see you then.” Rio smiled. 

“Great, see you.” Agatha hung up. 

Alice immediately burst through the door. She had clearly been listening, “Did she ask you over to her house?”

Rio nodded, “Next weekend!”

“Score! Maybe your luck is changing.”

“Maybe.”

Chapter End Notes

Sweet, heartsick Rio, my beloved.

Also, you may have noticed that I (hopefully) finalized the chapter count. Just a few more left of these two baseball-loving, lovebirds. It's been so fun writing this - thanks as always for reading!

Chapter 23: Walk-off homer

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“I was just getting into my prime. . . . I thought it would last forever. . . . I prayed every day that it would last forever.”

Catherine Horstman

Rio prepared guava pastelitos on Saturday afternoon. Her aunt used to make them every Sunday before church. They were light, flaky, fruity, and so delicious. Alice was her willing taste-tester and gave her two thumbs up. 

“These are incredible. Why don’t you cook more?” She asked. Rio was more of a grazer in the kitchen and rarely cooked full meals. She never baked anything. 

“I should. My aunt was an amazing chef. I really miss her cooking - every time I try to recreate her dishes, it pales in comparison.” 

“How are you aunt and uncle doing?” Alice had only met them once, last Christmas when they came up to visit. 

“Good, they don’t know what to do with themselves now that all my cousins are out of the house. I swear my aunt is ready to lose her mind. They’ve adopted two more chihuahuas on top of the one they already had. My uncle said they follow him everywhere. He’s already planning his next trip up here to escape.” 

“Tell him to bring some more of that Puerto Rican rum if he comes before summer break. I’ve been craving it ever since they brought it last time.”

“Will do. Can I borrow your leather jacket again? I was going to wear it tonight.” Rio was planning to wear a pair of form-fitting plaid ankle pants with a sleeveless white shirt and thought the 50’s statement piece would go perfectly with it. 

“It’s all yours. Have a good time!”

Rio took a cab this time to Agatha’s house since she planned to drink and didn’t want to risk driving. She arrived a little early to help her setup. 

She rang the doorbell. 

“Did I leave the oven on or is that just you, hot stuff?” Agatha quipped upon opening the door and taking in Rio’s look. 

“Shut up. Here, I baked these.” Rio held up her platter of pastelitos that she’d carried here. 

“I didn’t know you could bake.”

“I’m a woman of many talents.”

“I know that’s true.” Agatha led the way to the kitchen, which was at the back of the house. The room was covered in a mix of freestanding and mounted cabinets and shelving. There were pots and pans hanging on the walls and a glorious hutch over the oven. It was clear this room had seen many renovations over its long history and was marked by each decade in different ways. 

“Smells amazing in here.” Rio remarked and took in the bubbling pots on the stove. There was also a mound of prep dishes already piled in the sink. Rio shrugged out of her jacket and went to hang it on the coat rack by the door. She then got to work washing the dishes. 

“You don’t need to do that. I’ll take care of it after.” Agatha said. 

“Don’t be too proud. It’s better to get it out of the way now.” 

Agatha smiled and went back to work seasoning the chopped vegetables in the roasting dish. “I didn’t expect you this early.” 

“Is it alright?” Rio asked. She hadn’t checked in to see if it was okay. 

“I like it.”

“Good.” Rio kept washing the assorted bowls and tools. 

After Agatha pulled off the boiling potatoes and put the remaining veggies in the oven, she untied her apron. “Do you want to see the rest of the house?”

Rio finished drying the peeler in her hand, “Yes please.”

Agatha led them through the other door in the kitchen, which flowed into the formal dining room. The table was extended to fit twelve chairs and had been beautifully styled with placemats, matching napkins, and fine china. 

“I thought you said this wasn’t fancy.” Rio remarked as she scanned the perfectly set table. 

“This is my casual dinnerware. The fancy stuff is reserved for Thanksgiving or Christmas. If you’re lucky, I’ll think about inviting you.” Agatha kept walking through the arched doorway to the living room. There were a few elegant couches around a television. A record player sat near the window, and a persian rug took up most of the flooring. 

“Did you buy most of this furniture?” Rio asked. 

“Some of it. Some pieces belonged to my husband or his family. Why? Not a fan?” Agatha explained. 

“It just seems so stuffy.”

Agatha laughed, “That’s the style for this neighborhood. Maybe you’ll like the second floor more.” She led them up the stairs. The stairwell was decorated in frames with art and photos. Rio took her time looking at each one. 

Agatha as a teenager in a school photo. Agatha holding Nicky when he couldn’t be more than two years old. Agatha and Peggy in their first year with the Comets. Agatha on her wedding day. She looked like a vision in the long white dress. Agatha and her husband in Times Square. Agatha and Peggy’s two kids when they were young. Agatha with each of her winning school softball teams. It was her life in snapshots. Rio wished she was somewhere on this wall. 

“Keep up!” Agatha shouted from the top of the stairs. 

Rio hustled up the last few and was in another long hallway. The first room on the left looked like an office or a reading room. Large bookshelves covered almost every wall. There was a very comfy, large recliner in one corner next to a floor lamp. Right across from that room was the main bathroom, which had a stunning clawfoot tub. 

“Apparently that tub has been in this house since it was built in 1889. Can you believe that?” Agatha asked. 

“Think of all the bare butts that have sat on that porcelain.”

“Rio! I love that tub - don’t ruin it for me!” 

Rio shrugged, “Just an observation.”

Agatha kept walking and opened the next door further down the hall. It was a guest bedroom, but there looked to be a large cage in the corner. 

“What’s that?” Rio asked, trying to see what could be in there. 

Agatha kneeled down and unlocked the cage door, reached in, and pulled out to Rio’s complete surprise a fluffy white and tan rabbit. “This is Señor Scratchy.”

“What…” Rio watched as Agatha affectionately snuggled the floppy bunny. 

“One of the class pets at school was pregnant and gave birth to a bunch of baby rabbits. They couldn’t find a home for this little guy, so on a whim, I adopted him. I don’t know why. It was not too long after Jimmy died. I think I just couldn’t bear another night by myself in this house.” Agatha brought the rabbit over and Rio gave him a small pet. He was extremely soft. 

“I’m glad someone could be here for you…even if it was just this guy.”

“Back to rest, Mister. I’ll bring you your dinner soon.” Agatha muttered as she put him back in his large enclosure. 

They saw one more guest room, and then there was just one last room. Agatha opened the door to her bedroom and let Rio in first. While the downstairs was relatively bland and elegant, this room was entirely Agatha. 

The four poster bed was all drama. The dark violet curtains immediately drew the eye and dominated the space beneath a completely over-the-top chandelier. There was a large walk-in closet full of every color of dress and gown. Another door must lead to an en-suite.

An ornate dressing table sat in the corner with a collection of perfumes and make-up. Jewelry was displayed tastefully in open, velvet-lined boxes. A handful of brooches lined the top of the table beneath the mirror for easy access. Rio could so easily imagine all the mornings Agatha spent getting ready right here. 

There was dark wood wainscotting along the walls, which matched the hallway, with cream paint on top. There were mementos and interesting art pieces everywhere - from Red Sox flags to antique paintings to postcards from Midwest towns. 

“Wow, it’s so…subtle.” Rio deadpanned as she twirled around to take it all in. 

“It’s been nice to have a space that’s completely my own.” Agatha had never been allowed to decorate her room under her mother’s strict eye, she’d felt obliged to both her husbands, and she’d lived in rentals while playing ball. 

“I’m just joking. It’s great. I really love it.” Rio approached Agatha from behind. The older woman was readjusting a slightly off-center frame. Rio slowly ran her hands through Agatha’s hair and received an unexpected moan in return. It sent a wave of goosebump up Rio’s arms. 

The loud doorbell rang throughout the house, cutting right through them. Agatha shook out her body to snap out of…whatever that was, and hurriedly made her way back downstairs. Rio tried hard to tamp down the arousal rising in her body. She couldn’t be feeling like this around Agatha’s friends. 

“Margaret, Kay, how are you both?” Agatha’s candy-sweet voice sounded so foreign. 

Rio walked downstairs and saw two women around Agatha’s age taking off their expensive wool coats. They must be neighbors. 

“Oh and this is an old friend, Rio.” Agatha introduced her as she stepped off the stairs. She could feel their prying eyes taking in her pants and youthful hairstyle, but they were polite as they greeted her. 

“Nice to meet you both.” Rio put up a hand to wave. 

The rest of the ladies arrived in succession. It really was a mixed bag. There was a teacher in her 60s named Irma that smoked out of an old-timey cigarette holder. Rio took a few hits out of curiosity and didn’t hate it. There were a few of Peggy’s friends, whose husbands were artists like Steve. One of them, Constance, was from Alabama and told Rio about the upcoming March on Washington that civil rights leaders were currently organizing. A young journalist recounted how she worked on the harrowing Boston Strangler story that had terrorized the city over the past year. 

As Agatha served up the staggering spread of food, the entire table was buzzing with interesting conversation. Rio wanted to be everywhere. It seemed like every person here had something to say. 

The hostess clinked her glass to get their attention, “Thank you all for coming to celebrate my oldest and dearest friend. Peggy, I can’t imagine my life without you in it. You have been there for me through it all. I’m so incredibly happy that we are still friends and that we are still in each other’s lives. I love you. I’m grateful for you. Happy Birthday!” 

The entire table raised their glasses and echoed the sentiment to the tearful birthday girl, who stood up from her seat, “Oh I thought I told you not to make me cry!” She pointed to Agatha, who just grinned in return. “I don’t know what to say. I never imagined that when I was in my 50s, I’d be living in America, a wife, and a mother to two children. It’s been a rollercoaster, but I wouldn’t have it any other way. Thank you all for being here, for being my friends, and thank you Agatha for all of this!

Rio watched the two friends admire each other. Decades of friendship swinging between them like a metronome. It was a beautiful outpouring of support and made her appreciate her invitation to witness it all the more. 

As the group retired to the living room for more drinks, Agatha brought out a beautifully decorated chocolate cake topped with lit candles.

“Who’d you pay to make that because I know it wasn’t you.” Peggy sniped at Agatha, who she knew couldn’t bake to save her life. 

“Mrs. Hart sends her regards.” Agatha chuckled before leading the group in the birthday song. 

After indulging in the rich dessert, everyone sprawled out onto the couches or floor. 

“So, has anyone got any good scar stories? I know this group must have some interesting ones.” Peggy’s friend, Anita asked. 

“Oooooooh, I love a gruesome injury story.” Irma rubbed her hands together. Rio almost spit out her water. 

The birthday girl stood up first and lifted her dress. She rolled down her stocking, revealing the discolored patch on her upper thigh, “Not particularly gruesome, but sliding on dirt for a few years has permanently messed up my leg. I don’t think this skin is ever going to look normal again.”

“Yikes! I can’t believe they let you girls do that back in the day. It seems unnecessarily cruel.” Marcia, a teacher, shook her head. 

“This country is nothing if not unnecessarily cruel to women.” Constance remarked. 

“Amen. Check this out.” Pat, one of the neighbors, lifted her hair up to reveal her neck. There was a noticeable, raised line cutting across her hair line. “I was 14. A group of older boys were in their car driving along the road by my school. I was walking home and they were throwing rocks at all the girls. I got hit right in the back of the neck. I needed ten stitches. The boys were identified and never punished.”

“What?!” Several women shouted. 

“Fucking typical.” Agatha added. It was not surprising but still disappointing. 

“I’ve only got a boring scar from when I got my appendix out.” Margaret offered, trying to lighten the mood.
