She looked so happy as the team jumped and celebrated around her - running circles around the field like wild rabbits. 

Agatha waved her over, and Rio stepped over the other parents to get down to the gate. She walked in and congratulated all the girls that passed her. The pitchers ran over and hugged her and thanked her so much for all her guidance. Rio did her best to accept the compliments despite her internal struggles. Agatha came over and wrapped her up in a quick hug. 

“Congratulations, my love.” Rio whispered in her ear for the second time in her life. 

Agatha was called over to collect the trophy for the team. Rio watched her lift it high over her head with the help of the team captain. The local newspaper snapped several photos. It was a beautiful celebration to end the season. Rio could not tear her eyes off Agatha, who was a crying, smiling mess. She gave her all for these kids - how could Rio not be smitten?

Once everyone had cleared out. All the parents had collected their children and all the friends had driven off into the night. Agatha and Rio were left alone, in the dugout as she packed up the team bats and catcher gear. Rio helped gather up the spare softballs into a bucket. 

“Another win to add to your collection. Has it lost its shine yet?” Rio asked. 

Agatha zipped up her bag, “I don’t think it ever will. Seeing their faces when they win - nothing compares.”

“Seeing your face when you win was pretty damn special.” Rio said. 

“Aww shut up. Can you grab those paper cups as well? The parks cleaner will have my neck if we leave any trash in here.” 

Agatha bent down to grab a forgotten pack of chewing gum. When she turned to throw it away, she almost fell over. 

Rio was standing right behind her. 

A black velvet box was sitting on display in her closed, outstretched hand. 

“Rio…what are you…” Agatha dropped the gum and covered her mouth. This wasn’t happening - surely not. 

“I know this might seem sudden. I know this is insane and not even recognized by any state in this country but…I don’t care. I don’t care that it’s only been a few months. I don’t care what anyone thinks. I don’t want to spend another minute on this Earth not married to you.” Rio opened the box to reveal a jaw-dropping diamond ring. The gasp that was pulled from Agatha’s throat was worth every penny. 

She continued, “I’ve been carrying this in my bag and trying to think of the perfect way to propose. I knew it couldn’t be in a restaurant or in a public place. I didn’t want this to be marred by the judgement of strangers. Then, I saw the elation you experienced here tonight and knew it couldn’t be anywhere else. 

The baseball field is where we met. It’s where we experienced the best and worst of our relationship. It’s where I fell in love with you the first time and where I’ve fallen in love with you again.”

Rio bent down on one knee. Agatha choked out a sob. She hadn’t cried at either of her two previous proposals despite wanting them at the time. This one felt different. It was the one she never saw coming, the one that her heart had been waiting for.  

“Agatha Harkness. Will you do me the honor of being mine for the rest of my life?”

Agatha laughed her signature cackle, “You impatient little…” She wiped her eyes and went over to rummage in her game bag. She was muttering to herself as Rio was left confused, still waiting on one knee. She could hear “ruined”, “god damn”, “should have known” being said under Agatha’s breath. 

“Sweetheart, you okay?” Rio asked worried about what was going on. 

Agatha came back and kneeled down in front of her. She revealed her own little box from behind her back. “You just had to get the jump on me.” She opened the box to present her own engagement ring to Rio. It was a thin silver band with a circular jade stone set in the center. 

Rio dropped her head forward with a laugh. This was ridiculous. This was perfect.

“I think you’ve waited long enough for me. So let me be the one to ask - Rio, will you marry me?”

Rio threw her arms around Agatha’s neck and kissed her. “YES! A thousand times, yes.” She leaned back and watched Agatha slip the ring onto her finger. It was as satisfying as the feeling of placing the final puzzle piece. 

Agatha (with some effort) pulled them both up to standing. She thought winning the championship had felt good, but that joy was only a fraction of what she felt right now. “Alright, show me my ring again.” She held out her hand. 

Rio pulled out the ring and placed it gingerly on her love’s finger, sealing it with a kiss like a charming prince out of a film. 

“Where did you get this?” Agatha held it up to her eyes to examine the beautiful gem. 

Rio shyly shrugged, “It’s a diamond from the ‘54 NY Giants World Series ring.” 

Rio knew that Agatha’s team was the Red Sox. But the Giants, specifically the 1954 Giants, were a very very close second, and only because they were the team to break the Yankees five-year World Series winning streak. 

Agatha’s mouth fell open, “What the actual hell?? How?!”

“One of the assistants in my biochem class. Her mom used to go steady with the back-up catcher on that winning team. She got to talking to me when I told her I used to play ball and said the ring just sits gathering dust on her mom’s office shelf. Apparently, he gifted it to her when they were dating and never came back to collect it! I asked if I could have it, even offered to pay, but she brought it to class the next day and gave it to me for free. I got it re-set at the jeweller and well, here it is.”

“I cannot believe you.” Agatha admired the ring. She liked the look of it initially, but now knowing what Rio did to get it and its history, she loved it. 

“And you remembered what I asked for…all those years ago. A jade ring.” Rio was beyond touched. 

“I wrote it down in my journal. I kept extensive notes on all those leagues years and have been re-reading them lately now that you got me feeling all nostalgic.” Agatha admitted. 

They hugged again and couldn’t believe they had both had the same idea to propose. It fit for them. Always in competition, always trying to one up the other. 

“Are we being crazy? Is it wise to marry someone you’ve only been dating for such a short time?” Agatha asked as they walked together to her car. She was still longingly staring at the ring, holding her hand in front of her to show it off. 

“It’s not like we’re strangers anyway. I’ve been wishing for this for well over a decade. I’m not some naive kid - you’re all that I want.” Rio opened the passenger door for her fiancée. Who said chivalry was dead?

Agatha threw her bag and the trophy in the backseat, then sat inside, “Peggy is going to lose her mind. Oh god, she’s going to go wild with wedding planning.” 

Rio closed the door and rushed around to the driver’s side, “She better not try to get me in a white dress.”

“If you try to get married in a Cubs jersey, so help me…” Agatha warned. 

Rio laughed heartily, “I hadn’t considered that but now that you’ve suggested it, it’s not a bad idea.”

“Rio.”

“Agatha, I’m only getting married once. Don’t stifle me!” 

The two held hands the entire drive back to their home. Rio relished the feeling of the ring sitting snugly on Agatha’s finger. They were together. They were happy. 

They were getting married.

Chapter End Notes

We're reaching the end. Just got to get these two down the aisle!

If you have any suggestions for what (or who) you want to see at their wedding, let me know :)

Chapter 25: Two out rally

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“I don’t think anybody in the league had any thought of women in the future while we were playing...but a good portion of the women have been in sports all of these years in one capacity or another, as teachers, instructors, or coaches. They all have added to supporting girls and women in sports throughout their lifetimes.”

Jeneane DesCombes

 

When Agatha broke the news of her engagement to her best friend that weekend, Peggy screamed so loud that the neighbor pounded on the door and asked if he should call the police. 

“Show me the ring! Show me the ring!” She hollered after finally convincing Mr. Preston that they were fine. 

Agatha explained the story, which caused Peggy to immediately break down in tears, “That is just about the sweetest thing I’ve ever heard. I knew I should have married a woman.” 

Jen also cried when Rio broke the news over the phone, even though she’ll never admit it. “I just have allergies!” She tried to say, but Rio knew her friend was overjoyed. She’d been listening to non-stop Agatha pining for months now and was glad that they were finally sealing the deal.  

Rio wished she could tell her aunt and uncle and her cousins but knew realistically, they’d never understand. It wasn’t worth jeopardizing their relationship, which hurt like hell but there wasn’t any alternative. Agatha would be described as her dear friend, and that would have to be enough. 

Peggy planned it all - from the date down to the font choice on the invites. It was billed as a ‘celebration’ but everyone knew that this was a wedding. They waited until the end of October when the weather cooled off to give Peggy enough time to arrange it all. 

For the venue, they chose the Commander’s Mansion along the river in Watertown - a Civil War-era building that hosted large events. It was beautiful, historic, and best of all, secluded. To Rio it looked like a few of the old hotels they used to stay at on the road when they played ball, when they used to sleep wrapped up in each other in squeaky old twin beds. 

Agatha didn’t want fancy catering, so she convinced Steve to grill up hot dogs and burgers for everyone instead. In lieu of gifts, the guests brought along a plate of their favorite ballpark snacks to share, so the reception was full of Cracker Jacks, peanuts, and bowls of candy. Peggy scoured old address books and went searching high and low to get out the invites to people from both of their pasts. The brides honestly did not know who she had invited until the day of.

Jen drove in from New York to help Rio pick her outfit and prepare. Alice was also on hand to assist with fashion advice. Rio had absolutely zero desire to wear a white dress like some Betty Sue. She went with a suit, which was quickly becoming a popular fashion trend. It had a dog tooth double-breasted coat that she wore over a pressed white shirt with black tie, a pair of slim black cigarette pants, and shiny loafers.  

Agatha had circled the dress she wanted in the Sears catalog weeks prior - a forest green, party dress with chiffon skirt and a lace top with three-quarter sleeves. It had a stylish belt with a rhinestone buckle that gave that hint of sparkle that she loved. She had done the boring bridal outfit twice before, now she was going to wear what she wanted. 

For the ‘something old’, she pinned her mother’s broach to her collar - the lock of Nicky’s hair was snug and safe inside. For ‘something new’, Rio had bought her a pair of pearl earrings to match the necklace she loved. For ‘something borrowed’, Peggy clasped on the silver bracelet that she had worn at her own wedding. And for ‘something blue’, Agatha rolled on the blue lace garter that she’d purchased as a surprise for Rio on their wedding night. 

William was the flower boy and tossed beautiful petals to the enjoyment of the crowd. 

They walked each other down the aisle. 

The weather decided to cooperate or maybe it just bent to Agatha’s will because somehow in the middle of fall there was not a cloud in the sky and no hint of rain. The leaves were beautiful reds and oranges as they stood outside on the lawn beneath the wedding arch.

As they made their way down the rows of chairs they spotted so many familiar faces. Dottie and Lupe from the Comets. Greta and Carson - still clearly holding hands and beaming. Steve sitting with his son JJ. His and Peggy’s daughter, Sandra, was taking the photos of the event. 

Wanda with a young man who must be William’s twin, Tommy. Jen and Alice. A handful of Agatha’s more progressive teacher friends from over the years. Some of Rio’s classmates who Alice knew were queer as well. To Rio’s surprise, her middle cousin, Chris, was there too - waving at her from the front row. It was a real hodge podge of people all coming together to support them. 

Peggy officiated. 

“We gather here today for the union of these two people, Agatha and Rio. The road here has been wild and weary, but they finally made it. There were plenty of detours, hazards, and long delays in this journey, yet somehow, the road always led them back together. 

I had the pleasure, or perhaps a better word is frustration, of watching these two first meet fifteen years ago when we were all playing baseball.” 

Dottie in the crowd gave a huge holler and shout at that. 

Peggy laughed and continued, “Yes, Go Comets! And although there was clearly an immediate attraction, it was not love at first sight. I used to room with Agatha and had to hear her bitch and moan about the pitcher who almost took her head off for weeks. It wasn’t until I gave Agatha a real push that she realized Rio wasn’t out to get her, Rio was actually completely in love with her. From then on, they were inseparable. I’m just glad I never walked in on them in any compromising positions.”

Agatha noticeably blushed and playfully swatted at Peggy, who kept on undeterred, “When they broke up, it was a right shame. Agatha was a mess for months, for years really. I watched her struggle and try to hide it, but I could tell she missed Rio, even though she was too proud to say so. I almost couldn’t believe it when Rio showed up out of nowhere earlier this year - looking as smitten as ever despite the years gone by. It’s been amazing to see my best friend happy again, truly, truly happy. I wish you both nothing but the best in the future. I believe you’ve written your own vows?”

Agatha nodded and composed herself. She didn’t think Peggy’s speech would affect her so much. She held Rio’s hands tightly and stared into her favorite dark brown eyes. 

“I always thought there were three loves of my life. Three irrefutable parts of my heart. Baseball, the Red Sox, and Nicky. I was grateful. I thought I had won, you know? Some people hope and pray for one love. And I got three. 

I stopped hoping for any more. Baseball has been the constant. It changed the entire course of my life and continues to surprise and delight me. The Red Sox will always let me down, but I’ll be there cheering and screaming until the day I die. My son went to sleep early but lives on in my memory. I thought that those three things were enough. 

Until you showed up out of the blue. I had learned to live without you. I had forgotten the way that you make me feel. But suddenly, you were back, and I was 35 and stupidly in love with the rookie pitcher from Florida all over again. 

You see me. You accept all my flaws, and I know there are many. You listen, you care, you are everything I’ve ever wanted in a partner. I thought I was lucky to have three loves, but now I know I don’t have three, I’ve won the lottery and have four. 

I love baseball. I love the Red Sox. I love my son. And I love you.”

Rio was crying freely now. It was unstoppable after hearing that. She didn’t know how she was going to get her words out but needed to say them now. She took a deep breath and unfolded the paper where she had scrawled her vows. 

“I knew I was different from the other girls very early. All I wanted for Christmas was a baseball mitt, and every other girl in my class was asking for a new baby doll or new dresses. I felt separate my entire life until I put on my first baseball uniform. I was part of a team. I felt like I belonged. 

Then you had to go and make me fall in love with you. I know it was more like hate at first sight from your point of view, but I was obsessed from the first time I saw you in a magazine. When we finally kissed after months of dancing around each other, I knew it was over for me. I was going to be addicted forever. I had finally found where I actually belonged. 

I hate the way things ended between us. There were many sleepless nights spent wishing I could have changed something, anything, to prevent hurting you. For fifteen years, I focussed on my studies and my work. I thought if I could pour myself into something productive the hurt would go away. I was kidding myself. 

You are my beginning. You are my end. I want to watch you coach softball games, host dinner parties, and throw snacks at the TV when your team loses. I want to be by your side for it all. I can’t promise I’ll ever cheer on the Red Sox, but I know we’ll have tickets to Fenway if they ever make the postseason. 

I loved you when you hated me. I loved you when you were gone. I love you now and forever.”

Agatha was a mess. She was so in love with this woman that she felt like she was going to burst. Her tears were the outpouring of all the pent-up emotions. Rio passed over her pocket square (which Agatha now realized was emblazoned with the Chicago Cubs logo), and she did her best to keep her makeup from smudging. 

As she passed it back, she leaned forward and moved for a kiss before Peggy stuck her hand in between them. 

“Ah ah ah, wait for the magic words.” She glared at Agatha. “First, exchange rings.” 

Jen stood up and handed the boxes to Peggy. They had picked out two simple gold wedding bands from the local jeweller. Agatha had a fun time lying to the shop owner that she was getting married to her favorite baseball player, left fielder of the Sox, Carl Yastrzemski. Rio let her have her fun. She loved watching Agatha ham it up and lie to the gullible.

As they slipped the bands onto each other’s fingers. Rio knew that she was never going to forget this moment. It was symbolic of everything they had gone through. Time was a circle, and no matter what, there was no end to her love for her catcher, her Agatha. 

“Okay, now, by the power vested in me by…uhh you two lovebirds, I now pronounce you wives. You may now kiss!” Peggy declared. 

They both stared at each other and grinned. Agatha yanked Rio’s hands that she was holding and pulled her wife towards her. Their lips moved together in shared abandon as they sealed their fate in one glorious kiss. The onlookers cheered until they separated and lifted their locked hands in joyful celebration. Sandra snapped a beautiful photo of the look of pure bliss as they exited the aisle together after the ceremony. 

The reception in the manor was joyous and filled with well-wishes for the happy couple. 

“What the hell took you two so long?” Greta shoved herself between them both and put her arms around their shoulders. 

“What the hell are you and Carson even doing here?” Agatha asked back. She hadn’t seen them since leaving the Comets.

“We got an invite in the mail and knew we couldn’t miss it.” 

“And you two have been together all this time?” Rio asked.

Carson nodded, “Hardly a day apart ever since I left my husband in ‘54 when the league shut down. I realized I didn’t want Greta only a few months of the year anymore. I wanted her 365 days a year.”

“Awwww, isn’t she adorable?” Greta swooned and moved to go hug her partner. “Now that open bar is calling our name - we’ll catch up later, yeah?”

“We’ll be around.” Rio agreed as they watched them skip away to the bar. 

“Angel!” A voice called out - now there was a nickname, they hadn’t heard in a while. 

“Dottie, how are you?” Agatha greeted their old teammate. 

“Right as rain, darling. How did I not know that you and Rio were going steady? Was I living under a rock?” 

“We tried to be discreet. What have you been up to? Are you married?” Agatha could only see the charming young twenty something from the old days. It was hard to reconcile the put-together, beautiful 40 year old in front of her. 

Dottie held up her hand to show off a big ol’ rock on her finger, “Got hitched when I was 26 to a boy from my hometown. Popped out three little anklebiters who are both the light of my life and bane of my existence at times. I work part-time at the local sporting goods store, which gives me a nice break from their constant needs.”

“And you don’t mind that Rio and I are…queer?” 

Dottie laughed, “Not all southerners are close-minded, ya know? Only God can judge and if he made you this way, then who am I to complain?” 

“Amen!” Agatha voiced her agreement - unused to religious acceptance in any form. 

Lupe approached and practically jumped on Rio, “Hey Reaper, congrats!!” 

“Thanks, Lupe. Never thought I’d see you again.” Rio hugged her fellow pitcher. 

“I don’t even know how Peggy tracked me down. All I know is I got a letter in the mail, and I sure needed a break from work.”

“What do you do?”

“Got a factory job at General Motors in Detroit. Long hours but the pay is tops.”

“You always were a hard worker. Got a special gal in your life?” Rio asked. 

“I never was a one woman girl.” Lupe winked and tipped her cap as she walked off. 

Peggy interrupted and pulled the brides over to the cake table. She had baked a two tier chocolate cake with white frosting. Someone had gotten crafty and painted the brides on top so their dresses resembled the Comets green uniforms - complete with Rio and Agatha’s numbers on the back. 

“Did Steve add his artistic touch?” Agatha pointed to the figures and asked Peggy. 

“I made him contribute something besides being grillmaster.” Peggy admitted. “Now grab the knife, let’s cut this cake.”

Rio and Agatha stood side by side and held the comically large blade to slice into the cake. They each took a small piece. Agatha had warned that if Rio tried to smash any of it into her face, she would pay for it later. 

Unluckily for her, Rio was a glutton for punishment and shoved the decadent frosting dessert straight into Agatha’s mouth, muffling her scream. 

Everyone watched with bated breath as Agatha stood stock still for a moment, Rio also frozen in instant regret, before her wife grabbed a fistful of cake and returned the favor. Peggy quickly stepped in to stop it dissolving into a full-on food fight and took over plating pieces for the guests. 

“I warned you.” Agatha whispered quietly as she passed over some napkins. 

Rio swiped her finger across Agatha’s chin to clean up some frosting and sucked it clean, “I can’t wait.”

“You are sick.” Agatha tried to lick up the cake stuck around her mouth. 

“Don’t pretend you don’t love bossing me around.” Rio grinned and showed off her chocolatey teeth. 

Her cousin, Chris, came over and grabbed a slice, “Never thought I’d ever see you get married.” He joked. 

“How did you get roped into coming?” Rio asked. She didn’t think any of family would be here. She’d told Peggy in advance that Chris was the only one who might be okay with it. They’d always been the closest. 

“Got a letter with your name on in inviting me up to Boston. Seeing as you don’t invite us up here for anything else, figured I should take the chance to finally see where you live.” Chris said.. 

“Are you going to tell the rest of la familia?” Rio couldn’t bear the thought of her family disowning her or refusing to speak to her. 

“Don’t worry little cuz - you’re secret’s safe with me. I think a few of the boys might be jealous if they saw how smoking hot your wife is.”

“Hey, don’t be checking out my girl.” Rio attempted to cover his eyes. 

He ducked out of her reach, keeping a steady hand on his cake. “Let’s just say I’m impressed. I didn’t know you could pull like that.”

“I’m flattered.”

“Attention, attention. Can the newlyweds please make their way to the main parlor for their first dance?” Peggy’s loud voice called out. 

Rio found Agatha, who was animatedly talking to some other teachers, and they walked together to the large front room that had a record player and speakers. It had been cleared of most other furniture to create a makeshift dancefloor. The guests lined the walls as Peggy dropped the needle on the record. 

You sigh, the song begins

You speak and I hear violins

It's magic

Rio thought she had already cried enough but hearing this classic Doris Day tune again transported her right back to a secret hidden bar in Rockford where they first danced together in public. Agatha held her close with her arms around Rio’s neck, their bodies moving slowly together around the floor. 

The stars desert the skies

And rush to nestle in your eyes

It's magic

“I used to turn the radio off whenever this song came on.” Agatha confessed. 

“I don’t think I’ve heard it since. I did purposely avoid Doris Day records at the store whenever I was browsing though.” Rio held tightly to Agatha’s hips. 

They danced together like nobody was watching, like it was just them alone with this song. 

Without a golden wand

Or mystic charms,

Fantastic things begin

When I am in your arms

From then on, they would spend each anniversary dancing to “It’s Magic” and be able to relive both their first dances together. 

Each year of their relationship was somehow better and better. 

1964

Rio graduated with her doctorate and was immediately snapped up by Merck as part of their vaccine research team. To Agatha’s dismay, the head office was located in Rahway, New Jersey, so they decided to sell the Boston house and move. 

Wanda had come around to the idea of them together after being at their wedding and was thrilled at the close proximity. She was a frequent dinner guest and a surprisingly good gossip. She kept them abreast of all of Tommy and William’s frequently changing relationships.

She currently hated the boy named Richard that William was seeing. Apparently, he had crashed William’s car and was refusing to pay for the damage. Wanda was ready to raise hell. Rio advised that William was old enough to handle it on his own. 

Agatha hated to leave her school in Boston, but teachers are needed everywhere. She was able to easily get a new job at the nearest high school and immediately started a softball team. 

They won the state championship her first year coaching. 

1967

New Jersey sucked. 

They lasted three years before Agatha finally broke and said she missed Massachusetts desperately. According to her, Señor Scratchy also hated Jersey, so they had two votes in favor of moving. Rio wasn’t enjoying the work as her entire team was a group of white men over 50 who dismissed her every suggestion. When her old professor at Radcliffe reached out with a job opportunity for an adjunct teaching position in the biochemistry department, it was an easy decision.

They moved back to Boston. This time to Jamaica Plain where they found a charming two bedder with all the historic charm that Agatha loved and the modern amenities that Rio needed. Agatha was back at her old school. Peggy was ecstatic and gifted them season tickets to the Red Sox if they promised to stay. Agatha would take that deal any day. 

The stands were half empty most of the time, so tickets were dirt cheap regardless. Fenway was as barren as a church on Monday. There’s only so much that fans can deal with before they give up on a team, and Boston had all but given up on the Red Sox. Agatha was not a fair weather fan though, and she gleefully dragged Rio to every home game they could make it to. 

They had seats five rows back from home plate just to the left. Agatha always preferred the catcher’s view of the game over anywhere else. Rio preferred to be right by Agatha’s side, so it worked out for both of them. 

In 1967, expectations were as low as ever. The team was coming off eight straight losing seasons, so why should this one be any different?

It was different. 

“Rio, they’re only three games behind the White Sox in the pennant race. We might have a real shot.” Agatha explained as the team continued their impressive performance into August. 

“Sure, dear - whatever you say.” 

A few days later as they watched the game on TV, Rio almost dropped a pot of boiling water in the kitchen when Agatha screamed, “FUCK!” so loud that she thought someone was dying. 

She quickly set the pot back on the stove and ran to the living room, “What, what happened??”

“Conigliaro was beaned!”

“What?!”

“The Angels pitcher hit him right in the face. He’s on the ground, not getting up!” Agatha paced in front of the screen as they watched the star youngster unmoving in the dirt. 

“Oh no…” Rio saw the kid get lifted onto a stretcher and carried off the field. 

“There goes our chances.” Agatha fell to the couch in a huff. 

Despite the setback, the Red Sox kept pushing. They were first in the pennant race for the first time in over two decades and showed no signs of slowing down. All Star Carl “Yaz” Yastrzemski was carrying this team on his back with his league leading home run and hitting records. The stands became packed each game as Red Sox fever swept the town. 

It was an impossible dream. 

In October, when the Sox beat the Twins at home to clinch the American League, they secured their first trip to the World Series since the ‘40s. Agatha was over the moon. 

At game one against the St. Louis Cardinals, Agatha and Rio marvelled at the sold out stadium. They could see people hanging off the billboards across the street to try to get a glimpse of the game. Every seat was filled. The noise was deafening. 

“Can you believe it? We’re here! We’re really here!” Agatha was buzzing with excitement as the first pitch was thrown. She never thought she’d get to see this happen. 

Rio enjoyed the game but mostly watched her favorite sight - Agatha watching the Red Sox. She went through every emotion as they watched starting pitcher Jose Santiago (a young Puerto Rican) give his best stuff to the batters. Rio was still undeniably a Cubs fan, but Santiago was no doubt impressive. Unfortunately, the Cards went up early by one, but Santiago got back at them with his own homer. At the end of the game it wasn’t enough, and they lost 2-1. 

Agatha’s spirit wouldn’t be broken. They got tickets to game two and screamed with the crowd as Yaz crushed two homers and the Sox won 5-0 to tie up the series. 

“Is this happening? Pinch me, Rio!” Agatha exclaimed as they walked out of the stadium on a high. 

The Cardinals won the next two games in their home stadium in St. Louis. Boston needed to win game 5 to stay in it. Agatha pulled the couch as close as possible to the TV as they watched it live. She’d taken the day off work and insisted Rio do the same. 

The team pulled out a win 3-1 to stay alive. Agatha dove on Rio when the final out was called, and they fiercely made out on the floor to Rio’s sheer delight. 

Game six was another win by Boston and the series was tied three a piece. It all came down to game seven at Fenway. 

“We’re going.” Agatha declared. Fuck work and any obligations. This was the final game of the World Series. She wasn’t going to miss this for anything. Rio nodded along and called her dean to say she wouldn’t be in. 

They sat in their usual seats and greeted the familiar faces around them. 

From the start, Rio could tell the starting pitcher, Jim Lonborg, was struggling. She noted his placement and pitch speed was not his usual, and the Cardinals were pouncing on it. The Red Sox were down 0-2 in the third inning. 

“Rio, hold my hand.” Agatha grabbed her wife’s hand and would not let go as they watched the impossible dream slip away before their eyes. 

Boston scored one in the fifth but it was 1-4 at that point. 

They needed a miracle. 

Instead, they got a nightmare. Rio watched in horror as Lonborg’s arm became weaker and weaker until in the sixth inning, he pitched into a three-run homer and was pulled from the game. 

Agatha cried with the other fans as they watched their championship hopes disappear. Rio rubbed the back of her neck and offered the usual condolences, but she didn’t know if the Red Sox would ever get this close again. 

1970

“Why can’t we go to Miami again?” Rio whined as Agatha packed their suitcases. 

“My love, we go to Florida every winter to see your family. I want a different sort of vacation!” Agatha folded Rio’s shorts and stacked them in the bag. She’d spent every Christmas in the sunny snowbird escape. She put her foot down this year. 

“But why are we going to New York? You hate that city!” 

“No, I hate the damn Yankees. I’ve only been to the city a few times in my life - I want to see a Broadway show.”

They arrived in the city at the end of June. It was sweltering amongst the steel and the concrete, but the hustle of the city was intoxicating. Rio was a happy camper as soon as Agatha bought her a street dog and a pretzel. 

As they walked up 8th Avenue, Rio spotted a familiar face, “Jen?”

“About time you visit my city!” Her best friend bounded over to hug her. 

“What are you doing here?” Rio asked, confused. 

“Your wife called. Said she got us all tickets to see Company. Glad she finally convinced you to give the Big Apple a try.” Jen explained. 

Rio narrowed her eyes at Agatha, who feigned innocence, “Oh, sorry I surprised you with your oldest friend! My mistake!” 

“I love you.” Rio shook her head and followed her two favorite people in the world into the theatre. 

After the show, at a moody bar down the street, Jen invited them to a big march the next day, “There is going to be a huge event to commemorate the Stonewall Riots that happened last year. You should both come. We’re marching from the West Village to Central Park. Some of my coworkers told me about it.”

“Is it safe?” Agatha questioned cautiously. She knew the reputation of the NYPD and the gay community. 

“I heard they are expecting thousands of people. Safety in numbers - they can’t arrest us all.”

“I’m in.” Rio agreed. 

“Alright, what’s that chant going around? We’re here, we’re queer…now somebody buy me a beer.” Agatha joked. 

“That’s not…you know what, fine, next round is on me.” Jen was just glad to have her friends join her. 

The Christopher Street Liberation Day march took place on June 28, 1970. It would be recorded in history as one of the first pride marches. The event took half the scheduled time to cover the 51 block route due to excitement and wariness. 

Rio held Agatha’s hand as they marched alongside the throngs of people. There were people of all ages and races. Folks in custom lambda t-shirts, wearing different pins and badges, holding bold signs and posters. 

“Wow, you forget sometimes that there are so many others like us.” Agatha marvelled as they followed the crowd. 

“Times are changing.” Rio smiled and raised their clasped hands to kiss the back of Agatha’s. 

1973 

“Coach, can you write a letter of recommendation for me?” Kimberley came into Agatha’s classroom after the last bell rang. 

“What do you need a letter for?”

“Texas A&M. They’ve just added softball to their divisional sports program. I might actually have a chance at an athletic scholarship.” 

The country had passed Title IX legislation the previous year to ban sex-based discrimination in higher education and require schools to offer proportionally equal gender sports programs. For the first time, girls across the country were going to have as much of a chance as the boys to get scholarships and play for top-tier schools. 

“Of course I’ll write you a letter. You’re one of the best outfielders I’ve coached in years - it’d be a shame to see that cannon of an arm go to waste.” Agatha wrote down in her to-do list to type up a letter as soon as possible. She was overjoyed that her girls were going to have more opportunities to compete in softball past high school. 

There were still no professional leagues but getting a free or discounted college education was a huge step forward. Hundreds of thousands of girls across the states would benefit from these programs. 

In the 1940s, only 5% of women in the country had a college degree. The women ballplayers of the All-American league were outliers - with over 35% going on to get higher education degrees after leaving the league. These women, like Agatha, would go out into the world and continue to inspire future generations as many became educators and coaches. 

By the ‘70s the percentage of women graduates had doubled to 11%, and that number would only grow as the years went on. The ballplayers were some of the first to set the template and show it was possible. 

1975 

“Her water just broke!” Peggy screamed into the phone. “I’m too young to be a grandmother!” 

“You’re 62. Sorry babe, that’s grandma age.” Agatha joked. 

“Shut up, and stay by the phone - I’ll call you when the baby’s born.”

Stevie Rose Walsh was born on September 13, 1975 to her overjoyed parents Sandra and her husband Lyle. She was named for her grandfather (and a certain witchy rock singer whose band was blowing up the charts). Peggy and Steve were as proud as any grandparents could be. 

Rio and Agatha were the first visitors to the hospital. 

“Look at her little nose!!” Agatha squealed as quietly as she could and admired the baby, who was peacefully sleeping in her mother’s arms. 

“Congratulations, Sandy. She is beautiful.” Rio added, staring lovingly at the fresh baby. 

“Thanks, you two. I thought you’d be watching the Sox game, Auntie Agatha.” Sandra looked at the clock on the wall. She somehow knew the game schedule by heart. 

“I’ll check the score in the paper tomorrow. How often do I get to meet your baby? And anyway I need to indoctrinate this kid early.” Agatha pulled out a Red Sox onesie from her bag and held it up to the baby. “Look, a little ballplayer in the making!”

“Don’t worry, I’ll make sure Sandy signs her up for softball as soon as she can walk.” Peggy reassured her friend.

The new mother tried to hush them, “Oh stop! She hasn’t even opened her eyes yet. She might not even like The Red Sox!” 

“She doesn’t have a choice.” Agatha wouldn’t hear any complaints. 

“I don’t want her falling in love with a losing team. It’s so hard to watch! You cursed me with this fandom - I don’t want my daughter to suffer like I have.” Sandy joked with her aunt and mother. 

“They’re good this year!” Agatha replied. 

“You say that every year. If they make it to the World Series again, I’ll raise Stevie as a Red Sox fan. If they don’t, I won’t.”

“Deal.” Agatha held out her hand. Sandy unfolded the blanket around Stevie and held out the baby’s delicate little hand. Agatha shook it gently. 

For only the 3rd time in the last 60 years, the Red Sox made it to the World Series in 1975. 

A new fan was born. 

1979 

Agatha retired in 1976 at 64 years old. She was tired of grading papers and dealing with rowdy teens. She got to be home in ‘77 to celebrate with Rio when they got the news that her research into the pneumococcal virus had contributed to the new vaccine that now protected against 14 strains. Her work was going to make a real difference in the world. Agatha couldn’t be prouder. 

She was restless at home though, so she started volunteering with a local gay and lesbian organization in her free time. The movement was gaining in both acceptance and political power. 

Throughout her career, she had been grateful to work for principals and with coworkers who were surprisingly not nosy, so nobody questioned her marital status or living situation. Many other teachers and people in every profession were not so lucky. 

There were no laws to protect against discrimination based on sexuality or in some states to protect against arrest and criminal charges. Employers were free to fire any employee suspected of homosexual behavior. Sodomy laws were still rampant across more conservative states, and police regularly raided businesses that catered to homosexual clientele. 

William was kicked out of his rental when another tenant reported him to the landlord for suspected “gay activity” (with his boyfriend of five years). Agatha knew several teachers who had been fired and barred from teaching when their sexuality was revealed. 

By 1979, the gays had had enough. 

“Rio, they’re marching on Washington.” Agatha informed her wife, who had just gotten home from work. She hadn’t even removed her coat. 

“Who?”

“It’s a national march for lesbian and gay rights. We have to go.”

“Book the tickets.” Rio saw the fire in Agatha’s eyes and knew they needed to be there. She knew it was a big deal when Agatha didn’t put up any fight when William invited himself along. 

Around 100,000 people marched on the National Mall, on the 10th anniversary of Stonewall, to demand equal civil rights and urge the passage of legislation. It would be decades before there would be any progress, but this was one of the first steps on a very long journey ahead. 

1986 

“Did you see that letter on the table?” Rio asked. 

“What?” Agatha yelled back. 

“The letter! On the table!” Rio raised her voice. 

“The kettle?”

“No! The letter!” Rio walked over and picked up the letter and brought it over to her wife, who was again refusing to wear her hearing aids.

“Oh, the letter. Why didn’t you just say so?” Agatha adjusted her glasses to read it. “The reunion is in Chicago again?”

“Yeah, I don’t know if we can make it. It’s Peggy and Steve’s 40th wedding anniversary that weekend.” 

“I hate to miss the reunion though. We go every year.”

Rose “Horsey” Horstman had started the All-American Girls Professional Baseball League newsletter in 1980 as a way to reconnect all the players after decades of time apart. The league had all but faded into obscurity. Nobody talked about it at all because nobody believed it had happened. When the gals tried to say they got paid to play baseball in the ‘40s, they were laughed at. 

However, several players kept the memory alive and thanks to meticulous record-keeping at the time, were able to track down and find the over 600 women who once played ball in the league. In 1980, they organized the first reunion in Rockford and decided to make it an annual event. Rio and Agatha tried their best to attend each and every one. 

“We’ll go next time.” Rio reassured her. Agatha was 74 and still running around like a jackrabbit, although more like a deaf jackrabbit these days. She was showing no signs of slowing down. 

Plus, Agatha said she wasn’t going to die until she saw the Red Sox win the World Series, and Rio knew how stubborn she was. 

Part of her wanted Agatha to finally see her team win, but the other part was happy that they kept losing because it gave Agatha an excuse to keep going - not that she needed one. She played in their community slow-pitch softball league each fall, she kept up volunteering with the Gay and Lesbian Task Force, and she helped coach youth teams whenever she could. 

In 1986, the Red Sox made it to the pinnacle event in baseball again. Rio was conflicted as always, and Agatha was cautiously optimistic. Maybe, just maybe. 

They were up against the NY Mets. Boston had a killer pitching bullpen with both Roger Clemens and Oil Can Boyd, and an amazing hitting lineup full of veteran talent and young blood. 

The Red Sox won the first two games away in New York. They were heading to Fenway for game three.

Agatha was flying, “Two more. Just two more wins and we’re there!” 

They were at the game when the Mets won 4-0. Rio had to help Agatha down the many steps to their seats, but she would never give up her tickets. The Sox just didn’t have it in them that day. Agatha was still hopeful. She had all the blind optimism of a life-long fan. 

Game four was the same. The Mets won decidedly 6-2 thanks to a pair of two-run homers. 

“Don’t give up hope yet! We can come back. I believe - you just gotta believe, Rio!” Agatha explained on the drive home. 

The belief paid off when Boston pulled out a win the next game. They just needed one more win. One last win to seal their name in the history books. 

“Rio, remember what you’re wearing today.” 

“What, why?”

“We need to re-wear these exact outfits on Saturday when we watch game six on TV.”

“What? No way!”

“Do it for me? Come on, what if this is the lucky charm they need to win?” Agatha was extremely superstitious and did believe in the power of a rally cap and a lucky outfit. 

That night, they put on their exact same outfits. Rio drew the line at putting on her old underwear - Agatha didn’t, and they sat in the living room with all their fingers and toes crossed for one more win. 

It looked promising. Boston went up early 2-0. The Mets tied it in the fifth inning, but then Boston drew ahead by one run in the seventh. They just had to hold it. 

“Come on! Two innings. They can do it!” Agatha held onto Rio’s hand with all her strength. 

Rio lost all feeling in her fingers but didn’t say a word as Agatha gripped her tightly and the game went into extra innings. It was still all tied up. 

In the tenth inning, Henderson of the Sox hit a homer that caused both Agatha and Rio to zoom around the room like chickens with their heads cut off. The team put one more run up on the board. Boston was leading 5-3. 

“Am I dreaming? Am I dead?” Agatha was huffing and puffing from all the running. 

“I’ll get you some water, mi amor.” Rio ran to the kitchen. 

In the bottom of the tenth, the Red Sox got two quick and easy outs. They were one out away from winning it all. It seemed inevitable. The graphic even flashed onto the screen “Congratulations Boston Red Sox, 1986 World Champions" on accident. 

Agatha watched the sure thing dissolve as the Mets rallied and tied up the game thanks to several hits, then a wild pitch by the substitute closing pitcher. The Mets now only needed one run to win and end it. 

They were gifted the win when an easy ground ball went to first baseman, Bill Buckner, and rolled right through his legs. It would have been comical if it wasn’t so devastating. 

“NOOOOOOOOO!!!!!” Agatha fell to her knees in front of the couch. “NOOOOOOOO!!!” 

She grabbed the pillow behind her and screamed “FUCK” so loud until her voice gave out. 

The Mets scored. They won. The series was going to a game seven. Boston had fumbled it. 

The final game of the series was played in New York as well. Boston went up early as usual. Hopes were high. Peggy and her family were over at the house to watch the game with Agatha and Rio. 

Eleven year old Stevie sat right next to Agatha the whole time, glued to the screen just like her role model. Both of them dug into the bowl of chips and salsa. 

“Agatha, are they going to win this time?” She asked. 

“They’re up by three runs. We gotta really wish hard, okay? They need all the good magic we can send them.” 

Agatha watched the young girl close her eyes and scrunch her face in concentration. 

Things looked good up until the sixth inning, when the Mets rallied and tied it up. They put five more runs on the board, and the Red Sox were World Series losers once more. All the wishing and praying was no help. 

The team really did seem cursed. 

“Fuck baseball.” Agatha held her head in her hands. 

Rio rubbed circles on her back, “You don’t mean that.”

“I sure as hell mean it right now.”

“You’ll forget next year.”

“Maybe.” Agatha gave her signature smirk. Baseball drove her crazy, but she would never give up hope on her team. She relished the calming hands running along her spine in relaxing, well-worn patterns. 

She was blessed and cursed to love her four things. The Red Sox, baseball, Nicky, and finally the one that got away and then came back, her wife, her friend, her constant supporter, Rio Vidal.

Chapter End Notes

I loved imagining their life together over the years 😭. Just the epilogue left and that's it. Get your tissues ready...

Thank you for reading and following this silly baseball story!

(Send good vibes to the Seattle Mariners to make the World Series irl - they are the only MLB team to have never appeared in the championship and have a real shot this year)

Chapter 26: That's game

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

If we never do anymore, we have gotten our display there for all America to see. Every man, woman, boy and especially the girls that travel through their hallowed halls will now see the All American Girls Professional Baseball League. They will see action shots, individuals, lots of league memorabilia and above all they will see the names, hometowns, and years played by our players. For this we should all be very, very proud.

June Peppas

 

November 5, 1988. Cooperstown, New York

Hundreds of women, along with their friends and families, descended on the Baseball Hall of Fame in Cooperstown on a brisk, early winter’s day. After years of lobbying and pushing, the All-American Girls Professional Baseball League was finally being recognized in its rightful place alongside the rest of baseball history. 

“If my photo isn’t front and center, I’m setting fire to this place.” Agatha remarked to Rio as they pulled into the parking lot. 

“Don’t even joke about that.” Rio put the car in park and ran around to help her wife out. Agatha had knee surgery a few years back and was still a bit wobbly at times. All those years of catching had taken their toll. Rio opened the back seat to grab Agatha’s cane. 

“Well, aren’t you two a sight for sore eyes.”

“Greta!” Rio accepted a hug from the tall woman, whose curled grey locks were styled like an old Hollywood starlet. 

“Trouble walking there, butterfingers?” Greta pointed at the cane Agatha now yielded. 

“I can swing this sucker like a bat - watch yourself.” Agatha lifted the wooden walking stick up and pointed it at their old friend. 

“Where’s Carson?” Rio asked. 

“There!” Greta pointed behind her at her partner pushing a stroller up the sidewalk. “Our youngest had his first baby earlier in the year.”

Carson greeted them and introduced baby Jessica to everyone. Their son, Calvin, came up carrying the diaper bag. 

“Where’s your British friend? Aren’t you two always attached at the hip?” Greta asked. 

“Speak of the devil…” Agatha saw Peggy and Steve in their big SUV pull up with their growing family. 

Steve held the door open for Peggy as she stretched from the long drive. She immediately saw her group of friends and walked over, “Are they serving snacks at this thing or what? I’m famished.”

“Everyone’s lining up over there.” Rio pointed towards the entrance where all the women were gathering. 

They walked together towards the entrance and admired the large banners along the wall announcing the new exhibit. There was a team photo of the Rockford Peaches on one of them. 

“Look, darling. It’s us!” Greta grabbed Carson and beamed as they found themselves in the photo and showed their son, who snapped a picture on his disposable camera.

“Is that an Angel I see?”

Agatha spun around and saw a vaguely familiar face. It took a second before she realized, “Buckets?”

The woman nodded quickly and hugged her, “Nobody calls me that since I got married. My new last name is Spencer - boring! Anyway, all the Comets are gathering over here, come on.”

She led them over towards the front doors where Agatha and Rio reunited with so many of their old teammates. They had maintained fairly regular contact with the ones who attended their wedding, and they saw most of the others at the annual reunions. But there were some they hadn’t seen in decades like Buckets, Helen, Shirley, and Babe. 

A man came up to Agatha cautiously. He looked to be in his early 40s, glasses, a neat short haircut. There was something in the eyes that reminded her of someone. “Agatha? Agatha Harkness?” He asked. 

“Who’s asking?”

“I’m Sam Grant.” He held out his hand, which she shook. 

“Grant…No. Are you…Coach Grant’s son? I didn’t know he had a kid.”

“Yeah, the old man had me after he left coaching. But he talked about it constantly. Always said it was the best job he ever had.”

“Where is Doc?” 

Sam’s face fell, “Lost him a few years ago. Smoking a pack a day finally caught up with him.”

“I’m sorry. He was a great coach.”

“Yeah, he coached me all the way through high school. Always used to compare me to the best catcher he knew. I’m glad I can finally meet that catcher in person.”

Agatha smiled and shook her head, feeling shy, “You were a catcher?” 

“Yup - I’ll probably be needing one of those in a few years.” He gestured to her cane. “Wait, is that Rio?”

“You know her too?”

“He talked about all of you. I’ve only seen a few pictures, but they are all pretty faded. He would go on and on about Rio though - saying that he would have drafted her to the big leagues if he had the chance.”

Agatha called her wife over, “Here she is.”

“I always wondered if I’d ever get a chance to meet any of you. It’s like meeting a superhero from a comic.”

“Rio Vidal - nice to meet you.” 

“Wow, pleasure’s all mine. Do you still pitch at all?”

“Just for our slow pitch league. I don’t think my elbow could handle a fastball anymore.” Rio exaggeratedly stretched her throwing arm. 

There was a flurry of activity as the crowd seemed to converge on the entrance. Sam was several heads taller than the group and could see the main doors, “Looks like they are letting us in.”

Agatha reached out and grabbed Rio’s hand. Like everything else, they were going to do this together.

The Baseball Hall of Fame was established in 1939. Its motto is “Preserving History, Honoring Excellence, Connecting Generations,” and it serves as the central collection of baseball history in the United States. 

The energy was palpable as the women in the room gathered next to the exhibit on the ground floor. There was a red ribbon roping it off. For years, women’s professional baseball was a forgotten moment in time. Nobody talked about it or even acknowledged it had happened. But for hundreds of young women from across the continent, it changed and shaped their entire lives. They were finally getting their long overdue recognition. 

The daughter of Mr. Wrigley, one of the founders and main proponents of the league, stood in front of the group. She dedicated the exhibit to all the players, staff, and fans who kept the league going through those tough years. 

As the ribbon fell, the old ballplayers flooded the space and re-lived their shared history in this space that previously only welcomed men’s accomplishments. 

Dead center, right next to the introduction sign, was Agatha. Well, a 30 year old version of the woman anyway. It was a larger than life size version of the picture that was featured in LIFE magazine - the one that Rio used to put up in her locker. 

“Ha! Check me out!” Agatha used her cane to recreate the batting pose. Rio felt her heart tighten and suddenly was overcome with emotion at the sight of her past and her present converging in front of her eyes. 

Peggy had joined them and had Sandy take a photo, “A little to the left Agatha. Raise your elbows. There.”

Rio quietly wiped her eyes. She remembered every inch of that photo of her love. It had been her light in the dark. It had encouraged her to change her life and circumstances. She had loved the batter before she even met her. That photo had been the key to unlock the chains around her heart that she’d fastened after losing Agatha the first time. Her copy was currently framed in their bedroom and sat on her bedside table. 

Now, thousands of strangers would get to witness her most prized possession and revel in its glory. 

“Are you tearing up on me already?” Agatha raised her hand to cup Rio’s face and wiped away a stray tear with her thumb. 

“No! No, I’m fine. Just seeing you like that again…”

“I was hot, right?” 

“You still are.” Rio was forever smitten.

Peggy waved them over to another glass case that had a picture of Rio pitching next to some displayed gloves, “It’s a shot from your perfect game.” 

Rio smiled widely at the picture of her on the mound. She was completely focussed, mid wind up, her leg stretched high. 

“God, you used to be flexible.” Agatha muttered lowly. Rio could hear the suggestive husk in her tone.

“I can still touch my toes!” Rio countered. She had kept up her exercises and tried to stretch every morning after waking up. 

They walked through the exhibits and checked out all the old memorabilia, uniforms, photos, and long forgotten tokens of the past. It was an impressive collection of the league - all thoughtfully presented and explained for visitors. 

“Whoa!” Agatha stumbled for a second as a toddler zoomed past her and almost knocked her off balance. She took in the shock of bright orange hair on the little tike. 

“Viv! Get back here!” A curly headed man chased after the kid.

“William?” Agatha asked. She hadn’t seen him in a decade or so since he’d moved across the country to California. 

“Oh! Hey Agatha!” He strained his neck and could see Viv running into her dad, Tommy’s arms - safe for now. 

“Are you on babysitting duty?”

William looked sheepish, “I was. She’s got my brother’s speed, that's for sure. Kid’s going to be a track star or something.”

“It’s one of life’s many mysteries - how can toddlers run so fast when their legs are so dang short?” Agatha remembered chasing Nicky all over the park near their house. “Where’s the other ginger?” She looked around for Wanda. 

“She’s down with the flu. Awful timing - I swear she was going to start a fight when the doctor told her she couldn’t travel. I think I saw her eyes glow red! She insisted that Tommy and I come anyway.”

“Look at that - she really has always been a fighter.” Agatha noticed a large photo of Wanda laying back on a bed as Lilia tended to a nasty slide wound on her thigh. Her leg looked like it had been grated - all the skin had peeled off in the dirt. 

“God damn, why did they make you play in skirts again?” William winced looking at the painful injury. 

Agatha explained it simply, “Misogyny, my sweet summer child.” 

After taking in all they could, the group retreated outside and expectedly ended up on the practice fields behind the museum. There was a ton of equipment ready and waiting. There were so many players they split up onto the various fields to play some pick up games. 

“Schoolyard pick!” Greta cupped her hands and yelled. She was as boisterous and commanding as ever. Despite the fact that they were all well into their later years. “Losers buy the first round at the bar!”

The ladies lined up along the foul line as the families took to the bleachers. Greta was one captain while Agatha was chosen as the other. They were both well-known in the league and loved to command attention. They used a bat to pick who would go first, stacking their hands until the last person won. Agatha got dibs. 

“Wonder who you’re going to pick…” Greta said as they stood next to each other. 

“Rio.” Agatha pointed to her wife. She would always be her number one choice. 

“Carson.” Greta fired back. Another obvious decision. 

“Peggy.” 

“Jo.”

“Jess.”

“Horsey.”

Back and forth it went with each captain choosing their favorite players for each position. They added a few extra outfielders per team to cover all the ground. Agatha’s team was batting first. 

Dottie was the lead off hitter. 

“We want a pitcher, not a belly itcher!” Agatha chanted from the dugout. Forever a menace. Nickels was pitching and shook her head at the heckling. 

Dottie grounded the first pitch to Mary at second, who easily scooped it up and tossed it to Greta at first for an out. 

Agatha was up next. She strutted out to the box. 

“Don’t choke, Angel!” Carson bantered from behind the plate. 

“You wish.”

She waited until she saw a low ball outside. She pivoted and let loose on it, sending it flying to a gap in left field. Her knees didn’t have it in her to sprint like she used to, but she made it easily to first for a single. 

“Lucky swing.” Greta pestered her at first as she watched Rio come up to bat next. 

“That wasn’t luck, doll. I was born with this talent.” Agatha boasted. 

Rio was ready and waiting at the plate. Agatha could see the intensity in her eyes. When she connected with the ball, she ran hard for second. She could hear her team urging her on to third base, but her knees were already screaming. Rio was coming up behind her. 

“Run, mi amor. Run faster!” Rio yelled on her heels. 

Agatha turned her neck to look back, “This is my top speed!”

The ball was still rolling in the outfield as it had bounced off the wall. Agatha managed to get to third by some miracle, and Rio was safe at second after a close throw. 

“Do you need a defibrillator?” Jo DeLuca joked at third base as Agatha huffed with her hands on her knees. 

“Shut up, Joey. Watch the game.” 

Agatha scored on a sacrifice fly ball, but Greta’s team were able to hold them at one run. 

Rio grabbed a glove and walked with Agatha out onto the field. She was in just a chest protector and mask. She couldn’t crouch low anymore but could catch slow pitch easily.

“Just like the old days - it’s like no time has passed.” Rio remarked as they made the familiar walk across the dirt. 

“I feel a million years old, Rio. You’re going to need to wheel me home after this.” Agatha waved to young Stevie, who was freshly 13 and angsty but lit up when in a ballpark. She was enraptured watching the former pros play again. 

Greta was up first, “Come on, Reaper. Give me something I can hit!”

“Be careful what you wish for.” Agatha opened her mitt and watched Rio abandon her plan to take it easy. She saw Rio wind-up and fire a fastball. It didn’t have the burning heat from her youth, but it was still speedy. It hit Agatha’s glove with a smack for a strike. 

“Oof.” Greta knocked the bat against her shoes and reset. 

Rio slowed it down and threw an easy one down the middle, which Greta sent soaring right into Bucket's glove. 

“Nice try, Greta. Guess we can’t all keep our skills into old age.” Agatha snarked as she watched her old friend stomp back to the dugout. 

The game was casual and fun as they traded sides and insults throughout. Rio loved watching Agatha come alive again when trashtalking and pushing herself to the limit. Somehow they had discovered the ability to time travel. All they needed was four bases, a bat, and a ball. Who needs quantum theory or a Delorean? 

Rio watched Agatha screaming with joy as Peggy scored and felt like she was 26 years old and falling in love all over again. It didn’t matter how many games they played or how often, each time she played ball with Agatha she was transported again. 

Agatha’s team won by six runs to two. Rio’s pitching was somehow still as dangerous as it was forty years ago. 

“Good game.” Agatha shook Greta’s hand. 

“We’ll get you next time.” 

“Yeah sure, and the Red Sox will win the World Series one day.” Agatha joked. It was a common phrase these days after the devastating loss in ‘86. It was as unlikely as pigs flying. 

Rio was tired and her arm was aching, but she felt so content. Agatha was glowing in the way she always did after a competition. Her face was flushed, her long white hair was wild and crazy, her beautiful laugh lines were on full display. 

In Rio’s eyes, there was no better sight in the world. 

 

 

October 2004 

Busch Memorial Stadium - St Louis, Missouri

The 100th World Series was like déjà vu for Rio. The Boston Red Sox were up against the St. Louis Cardinals again - just like they were in 1968. It was also the anniversary of the Red Sox loss against the Mets in ‘86. All the memories were blending together. 

“Abuela Rio, who’s that?” The little girl sitting next to her in the stands pointed to the man walking out to the mound. 

“Ah, that’s the pitcher. The most important player in the game.” Rio explained. “He controls the game. Everything starts and stops with the pitcher.”

“Margaret, did you know Abuela Rio was once a pitcher too?” Stevie asked her four year old daughter.

“Really?” The little girl looked at Rio, who was now riding in a wheelchair, and couldn’t seem to believe it. How could this old woman have once stood tall on the pitcher’s mound?

“Yes, mija. I once played with your Great-Grandma Peggy. Do you remember her?”

“Yup! Oooooh, who’s that in the red armor? Like a knight!” She pointed at the player who was walking out behind home plate. Her young mind glommed onto all the stimulation like a bee to a flower. 

“That’s the catcher. I used to know the best catcher in the world.” Rio’s eyes shined as she rubbed the worn baseball card she held tight in her jacket pocket. 

“My mommy?” Margaret looked to her mom. Stevie laughed and kissed her daughter on the head. It was true that Stevie did play catcher when she was younger - as if she would play any other position when she worshipped Agatha so. 

“Not your mommy. My wife was the number one catcher back in the day.” She pulled out the baseball card and showed Margaret the picture of Agatha posing in her catcher’s gear. The league had reprinted all the player cards not long after the blockbuster film, “A League of their Own” was released in 1992. 

Rio had bought up several copies of the Agatha “Angel” Harkness cards. 

“She’s so pretty. How come she’s not here?” The girl asked. 

Rio’s heart sank, and she couldn’t find her voice. Thankfully, she heard Stevie cut in, “Agatha lived a long, long life. She was very tired, and her body wanted to rest.”

“Like Ruffles?” Margaret’s cat had recently died suddenly. 

“Yes, sweetheart. Oh, do you hear that?” Stevie heard the announcer asking the crowd to please rise for the national anthem. Saved by the bell. Rio, for once, was grateful for the old tradition. 

It was game four. The Red Sox had won the first three games. But the fans had endured enough suffering to know that nothing was guaranteed. Baseball could take just as much as it could give. Rio had been there to see every disappointment, had heard every scream of defeat from her wife’s mouth, had watched Boston lose and lose and lose.

“Play ball!” Game four was underway. One more win. They could do it tonight. They could end the Curse of the Bambino once and for all. Legend had it that the Red Sox were cursed ever since the owners sold the greatest player of all time, Babe Ruth, to the Yankees in 1920. From that moment on the team was cursed, seemingly destined to lose, to pay for that original sin over and over again. 

An old curse, passed down for generations, is incredibly hard to break. But tonight, just maybe, they could do it. 

“Abuela, what’s happening now?” This was Margaret’s first major league game. She was a little young, but Stevie was a rabid fan thanks to Agatha’s indoctrination. There was no chance she was going to let her daughter miss potential history. They had paid an obscene amount of money for the tickets, the airfare, and the hotel, but they weren’t ever going to miss this. 

Rio explained the goings on of the game to the young girl, whose eyes were as big as saucers, trying to take it all in. There was so much to see, and the crowd was unlike any other that Rio had ever experienced. The anticipation was like a pot over fire - building and building with energy as the rolling boil threatened to overtake the water. 

“I can’t believe she’s not here.” Stevie commented as the first inning began. The Red Sox were batting first. 

“I know.” Rio felt the loss in the marrow of her bones. A piece of her disappeared when Agatha did.

But, she also felt Agatha’s presence everywhere around her. In a ballpark, it was like her wife’s atoms flowed through everything. The anticipation in the crowd was like the tension that always sparked between them. The fervor of the fans was a reflection of Agatha’s own relentless devotion to this team. The look in children’s eyes when they fell in love with the game for the first time recalled Agatha’s dedication to her students and players.

It was all there. 

Everywhere Rio looked she deeply felt connected to Agatha’s very soul. She looked to the sky. 

“Look at the moon, Margaret.” Rio pointed up. 

“Wooooooowwww!” 

The moon looked a dusky red - a blood moon, when the veil between the living and the dead is at its thinnest. This was the first lunar eclipse to ever take place during a World Series game. 

Agatha really did feel as close as ever. 

Baseball and their relationship was one. There was no disentangling the complicated weaving of the two. Her love for Agatha was wrapped up in strikes and fouls and slides into home base. As long as baseball continued on, so would her love for her wife. 

“I love you Johnny Damon!” A woman screamed behind them. Margaret almost spit out her juice as another Boston fan cheered on the batter. His signature long locks were the envy of many, and he sent the ball soaring into the stands in his first at-bat in the first inning to give the Red Sox the lead.

Stevie gripped Rio’s hand, just like Agatha used to. 

The Sox scored two more in the third inning. It was 3-0. 

“Rio, is it happening? Is this really happening?” Stevie looked over at her surrogate grandmother. Rio and Agatha used to babysit Stevie whenever her mother Sandy was at work and Peggy was too busy. Although Stevie was obsessed with Agatha, she loved Rio just as much. Since Peggy’s passing three years ago, Rio was the last one standing. 

“It looks good, kid.” Rio couldn’t let herself get too optimistic yet, but the hum of the crowd was building.

The Red Sox starting pitcher, Derek Lowe, pitched seven scoreless innings. It was brilliant. The Cardinals had no answer for his pitches and couldn’t put together any sort of offense. 

“Abuela, why is everyone standing?” Margaret looked around at the top of the ninth inning as the entire stadium seemed to stand. 

“They are sending all their good feelings to their team. They are showing support. Just three more outs, and we win.” Rio explained. 

“Can you stand with me?” The girl asked. 

“Oh, Margaret, Rio might not....” Stevie tried to say, but Rio shook her head and braced herself against her chair arms. She was 82 years old and still in mostly good health, but the long walk to the stadium from the parking lot was a touch too far these days. Her knees would protest tomorrow, but when a kid asks you to stand and watch with them, you stand and watch with them. 

Rio held onto the seat back in front of her for extra support, and Stevie helped Margaret stand on the chair to see. The little girl held tightly to Rio’s arm and watched the final inning of the World Series. 

“The pitcher is changing!” Margaret announced as she saw the man on the mound swap out with a new person. 

Red Sox closer Keith Foulke came in to pitch the last inning. The first batter hit a single, which quieted the crowd. Then, the next batter popped out. The third struck out. 

Two outs. 

“They’re going to do it.” Stevie was almost shaking with excitement. Margaret was jumping in her chair. Rio was in disbelief. The noise was thunderous. 

The fourth Cardinals batter came up to the plate. He knocked a ground ball straight back to the mound. The pitcher fielded it easily and tossed it underhand to first base.

It was like an explosion. 

The crowd went insane as the final out was called. There were only a couple thousand Boston fans in this St. Louis stadium but you’d never know it from the noise. 

“Red Sox fans have longed to hear it. For the first time in 86 years, the Boston Red Sox are World Series champions!”

Rio took out the card in her pocket and stared into Agatha’s brilliant blue eyes that seemed to sparkle even in print. Everyone was going crazy, the players were dogpiling each other on the field. Stevie was crying and holding Margaret in a tight hug, it was impossible to hear anything.  

Rio felt a chill up her spine, goosebumps rippled across her arms and legs. She smiled wide despite the tears balancing on the edge of her eyes. 

She could feel Agatha’s spirit so close - like they were one. 

Rio looked down at the card and spoke softly, “Congratulations, my love. 

I know you’ve been waiting for this day. You can rest easy now. 

Game over.”

Chapter End Notes

The end 😢. Thanks as always for everyone who read and followed and commented on this story! It was a fun journey, and I hope you enjoyed it too.

Side note: It really was a lunar eclipse on the night the Red Sox won the world series! It felt like a crazy parallel with Agatha’s trial in the show, so I had to throw that detail in.

Also: In 2026, women’s professional baseball will officially be back in the US for the first time since the league shut down in 1954! They just announced the locations of four of the teams (SF, NYC, LA, and Boston) in the newly formed Women’s Professional Baseball League, WPBL.

If you have any interest in this story, baseball, or women’s sports, please consider supporting this brand new league. Women haven’t been paid to play baseball for over 70 years, so pick a team to support and get out to a game if you can! Learn more here
